About the Authors

Alejandro de la Fuente is a Senior Economist with extensive experience in the
World Bank’s Poverty and Equity Unit and Social Sustainability and Inclusion
Unit, across low- and middle-income countries, including fragile contexts. His
current work involves providing high-quality analytics on poverty, program
evaluation, and risk and vulnerability; policy guidance; and hands-on support to
World Bank operations on statistics and social protection. de la Fuente
currently serves as Global Program Lead on Climate Resilience in the Poverty
and Equity Global Practice. Previous roles include positions with the United
Nations Development Programme, the United Nations Office for Disaster Risk
Reduction, and the Inter-American Development Bank and public service in
Mexico’s Ministry of Social Development and the Office of the President. He
holds a DPhil in development studies/development economics from the
University of Oxford, is author of multiple peer-reviewed articles, and is
coauthor or coeditor of six books.
Miki Khanh Doan is an Economist in the Distributional Impact of Policies Unit
at the World Bank, where she leads and supports analytical work on climate
resilience, agricultural policy, and poverty reduction. Her work focuses on the
distributional impacts of climate and development policies, including the
repurposing of agricultural subsidies; welfare effects of climate shocks; and
pathways for resilient, low-carbon farming systems. She has conducted
research on intra-household dynamics, rural livelihoods, and school meal
programs in East Africa and South Asia, in collaboration with institutions such
as the International Food Policy Research Institute, World Food Programme,
US Agency for International Development, and Stanford’s Global Center for
Gender Equality. Doan holds a PhD in agricultural and resource economics
from the University of California, Davis.
Samuel Freije-Rodríguez joined the World Bank in 2008. His areas of work
include labor economics, international development, and welfare economics.
He has participated in World Bank studies on labor markets, poverty, inequality,

and distributive impact of tax policy in the Plurinational State of Bolivia, China,
Colombia, the Dominican Republic, Mexico, Mongolia, the Russian Federation,
Türkiye, and República Bolivariana de Venezuela. He was co–task team leader
of Poverty and Shared Prosperity 2020 and team member of World Development
Report 2013: Jobs. Before joining the Bank, Freije-Rodríguez was Associate
Professor at Universidad de las Americas in Puebla, Mexico, and at Instituto de

editor of Economía LACEA Journal. Freije-Rodríguez holds a PhD in labor
economics from Cornell University, a master’s degree from Queen Mary
University in London, and a bachelor’s degree from Universidad Católica
Andrés Bello in Caracas.

Key Findings and Messages

This report focuses on poor and near-poor individuals and asks what can be
done to strengthen their resilience to climate risks. By combining new data,
case studies, and original research, it offers a detailed look at who is most at
risk, how climate hazards interact with poverty, and what policies can best
protect vulnerable populations in Latin America and the Caribbean (LAC).
It highlights four critical questions for governments of the region:
1. Are poor people more exposed to climate hazards?
2. What risks do these hazards pose to households and societies?
3. What adaptation measures are already in place, and how effective are they?
4. Finally, how can adaptation efforts be improved and accelerated to ensure that
­poverty reduction continues despite a changing climate?

CLIMATE RISKS AND POVERTY IN LATIN AMERICA
AND THE CARIBBEAN
LAC has made huge strides in reducing poverty over the past two decades. In
2000, half of the region lived below the upper-middle-income poverty line
defined by the World Bank; in 2024, it was one-quarter. Inequality, although
still high, has fallen to historic lows. Millions of families have built better lives.
Yet this progress is under serious threat. Climate change is bringing more
frequent and more intense hazards—hurricanes, droughts, floods, landslides,
and heat waves—that risk undoing these hard-won gains. The region
contributes little to global greenhouse gas emissions, but it is among the most
vulnerable to their effects. For millions who are just one shock away from
falling back into poverty, climate change is not a distant concern but an
immediate risk to livelihoods and well-being.
xiii

xiv

| Adapting to Adversity

The challenge is not simply about facing stronger hurricanes or hotter
summers. It is about ensuring that development can withstand these shocks.
The poorest individuals cannot afford to start over each time a climate event
destroys their home, wipes out their harvest, or interrupts their
children’s education. Building resilience is not only a matter of fairness but
also one of economic necessity.

WHO IS IN HARM’S WAY?
The scale of exposure in the region is staggering. As of 2020, more than a
third of the region’s population—240 million people—lived in areas exposed
to major climate hazards. Droughts are the most widespread, affecting more
than 13 percent of the population, followed by heat waves, hurricanes, floods,
and landslides. However, poor people face a disproportionate share of this
burden: Nearly 45 percent of poor households are exposed to at least one of
these severe climate hazards, compared with 34 percent among nonpoor
households.
Hotspots where climate hazards overlap with high concentrations of poverty
are scattered across the region, from Yucatán’s Caribbean coast; parts of
Mexico’s southern and Gulf coasts; Central America’s Dry Corridor through to
the Amazon basin; northeast Brazil and the Chaco region of the Southern Cone;
and the Caribbean coastal zones. In total, some 64 million people live in these
high-risk zones.
Exposure, however, tells only part of the story. Vulnerability determines
whether a family can bounce back after a hazard hits or falls into long-term
hardship. Poor households are often more vulnerable because they depend
on fragile livelihoods, such as rain-fed farming, street vending, or low-paid
labor. They tend to live in less resilient housing, lack savings or insurance to
fall back on or recover quickly, and have limited access to safety nets. When a
storm or drought hits, these families may be forced to sell what little they own
or cut back on essentials such as food and schooling. Over time, these coping
strategies deepen poverty rather than relieve it. Depending on the method
used to estimate vulnerability, 3 to 16 percent of the region’s population is
climate vulnerable.
The impacts of climate shocks go far beyond immediate damage and income
losses. They disrupt children’s learning, increase malnutrition, worsen health,
and reduce productivity, leaving lasting scars across generations. In many
cases, climate events do not kill large numbers of people, but they steadily
erode human capital and trap households in poverty. In this sense, climate
risk is a risk to development. Adaptation policies are not just about managing
disasters; they are long-term investments in people’s futures and long-term
development.

Key Findings and Messages

WHAT CAN BE DONE?
Adaptation is not an abstract idea in LAC. Governments, communities, and
international institutions have been experimenting with a wide range of
adaptation tools, from early warning systems to critical infrastructure, insurance
schemes, and social protection programs. Some of these efforts have shown clear
benefits. Flood barriers and early warning systems have saved lives. Protected
areas and recognition of Indigenous land rights have slowed deforestation.
Disaster funds and parametric insurance tools—such as Mexico’s Fondo de
Desastres Naturales, the Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance Facility, and
catastrophe-deferred drawdown options—have provided fast, predictable
financing after climate hazards hit. Countries such as Mexico and Nicaragua have
piloted innovative programs that link national insurance with local
microinsurance to protect poor farmers. And adaptive social protection systems,
where they are well established, such as in Argentina, Brazil, Chile, the
Dominican Republic, or Uruguay, have cushioned poor people from falling
deeper into poverty after climate shocks.
But progress remains uneven. Many countries still lack strong early warning
systems or widespread critical infrastructure. Many countries still rely heavily
on risk retention tools such as emergency funds or budget reallocations while
more advanced options such as catastrophe bonds and parametric insurance
remain rare. Innovative insurance mechanisms are concentrated in a handful of
countries and often exclude those who are poorest. Social protection systems in
much of the region remain nascent, reaching only a fraction of vulnerable
households and with outdated registries that slow emergency response. Even
where cash transfers exist, weak coordination or outdated registries often slow
down support, leaving those who are most vulnerable unprotected.
This unevenness means that although some countries are well positioned
to manage future risks, others remain dangerously exposed.
Another challenge lies in prevention. Governments tend to invest heavily in
recovery once a climate hazard strikes but underinvest in measures that could
reduce losses in the first place. Forecasting systems are often underfunded.
Many people remain on floodplains, drought-prone farmland, or landslide-prone
hillsides because they lack safer, affordable alternatives. Deforestation and poor
land management often worsen the impact of hazards. Addressing these
structural issues requires not only technical solutions but also political will.

MOVING FORWARD
To protect recent development gains, LAC countries will need to accelerate
adaptation and make it central to poverty reduction. This means building
comprehensive systems that combine prevention, insurance, and coping
mechanisms. The exact mix will vary by country, but the principle is the same:
The more layered and integrated the approach across three fronts, the more
resilient communities become.

|

xv

xvi

| Adapting to Adversity

Prevention—through resilient infrastructure, urban planning, and ecosystem
protection—can reduce the scale of losses. For insurance, governments should
expand access to affordable products while strengthening their own financial
protection against hazards through private or regional schemes. For coping,
social protection systems must become adaptive—able to expand rapidly
during crises and reach the poorest people without delay to ensure they can
recover quickly. Countries that invest in layered, coordinated systems across
all three dimensions are far better equipped to safeguard both lives and
livelihoods.
Information and incentives will play a key role. People need reliable climate
forecasts, transparent communication, and fair insurance pricing to make safer
choices. Stronger property rights, access to credit, and transport options can
give families the means to move out of harm’s way. At the same time,
institutions must be transparent and accountable, ensuring that resources are
directed where they are needed most.
Financing adaptation is an urgent challenge. The United Nations
Environment Programme estimates that the region may need to invest up to
$55 billion annually in adaptation by 2030, yet current flows are limited and
heavily skewed toward mitigation. Some promising initiatives exist—such as
Mexico’s green resilience bond for climate-smart farming and new
insurance programs for smallholder farmers in Central America—but scale
remains insufficient. Mobilizing more financing will require legal and
institutional reforms to attract private and multilateral investment while
embedding adaptation goals in national development plans. Governments
need to work together with development banks and the private sector to
close this gap, using innovative tools such as resilience bonds and blended
public-private partnerships. At the same time, governments can improve
access to climate data, strengthen property rights, and embed adaptation
into national development strategies to attract and channel funds more
effectively.
Above all, adaptation is not only about money or technology; it is also about
politics. Too often, governments prioritize short-term relief or visible
reconstruction projects that bring quick political rewards while long-term
resilience is overlooked. The poorest individuals, who have the least
voice in political systems, often pay the highest price for this shortsightedness. Shifting the balance requires informed citizens who demand
accountability, institutions that promote transparency in resource use, and
leaders who are willing to invest in solutions that may not yield votes
tomorrow but will protect lives and livelihoods for decades to come.
Stronger institutions, greater transparency, and active citizen engagement
are needed to shift these incentives and ensure adaptation policies serve
those most in need.

Key Findings and Messages

CONCLUSION
LAC stands at a crossroads. The region has shown that progress against poverty
and inequality is possible, even in the face of global challenges. But climate
hazards threaten to reverse these hard-won gains. Policy makers now face a
choice: continue reacting to these hazards as they come, or take bold steps to
build resilience that safeguards both people and prosperity.
Adaptation is not a luxury; it is development. Protecting poor people from
climate hazards means protecting the region’s future. By investing in
prevention, expanding financial protection, strengthening social safety nets,
and fostering transparent, inclusive institutions, LAC can weather the storms
ahead and ensure that continued poverty reduction is not just progress of the
past but a promise for the future.
Ultimately, adaptation in LAC is both an urgent necessity and an opportunity.
The region has already pioneered some of the world’s most innovative climate
risk management tools, and several countries have shown that resilience can be
built even with limited resources. But accelerating progress requires scaling
these lessons, closing gaps in social protection and infrastructure, and putting
vulnerable households at the center of adaptation strategies. If governments
can do so, they will not only safeguard recent gains against poverty and
inequality but also lay the foundation for more inclusive, sustainable growth in
the decades ahead.

|

xvii

Executive Summary

Climate hazards threaten to reverse the recent progress made by Latin
America and the Caribbean (LAC). Since the turn of the millennium,
millions of Latin Americans have lifted themselves out of poverty. Whereas
half of the population lived below the upper-middle-income poverty line
of $6.85 per person per day in 2000, this had fallen to one-quarter by
2024 (2017 purchasing power parity). Inequality, although high by
global standards, has also fallen to record lows.
The LAC region hosts some of the world’s most climate-risk-prone
countries, with their poor and vulnerable populations bearing the brunt
of the impact. Although the region has historically produced few greenhouse
gas emissions, it now faces more and stronger climate hazards that could stall
or even reverse poverty reduction. To stay on track, development efforts need
to boost the ability of households, especially poor households, to avoid being
plunged back into poverty. This report focuses on the resilience of poor and
near-poor individuals to climate hazards, pointing to adaptation measures that
prevent or diminish the risks of poverty, injury, damage, or even death. It also
outlines strategies to ensure their effective implementation.
The report aims to deepen the conversation around climate risk, poverty,
and adaptation, both in the LAC region and globally. First, it brings a
distributional lens to recent climate analytics in the region, providing new
evidence on the extent to which the poorest people are exposed and potentially
affected by climate hazards. It estimates how many poor people are exposed to
climate hazards across LAC, that is, in harm’s way, and maps them at the
subnational level. It estimates how many people could be pushed into poverty—
or become poorer—and identifies other losses tied to that risk. Second, the
report tailors widely used conceptual and policy frameworks to the LAC
context, producing new empirical insights by applying a poverty perspective to
five major adaptation policies currently applied in the region.
This complements past World Bank studies such as Rethinking Resilience
(Shilpi, Kahn, and Berg 2025), the Roadmap for Climate Action in Latin America
xix

xx

| Adapting to Adversity

and the Caribbean, 2021–2025 (World Bank 2022), From Resource Rich to
Resource Smart (Beylis and Lozano Gracia 2025), and Taxing and Subsidizing
Energy in Latin America and the Caribbean (World Bank 2025), as well as
contributes to awareness about how climate adaptation policies can help
sustain poverty gains. This report offers new data and tools to help regional
actors assess climate risks and design better policies.
The report combines new and existing data, tools, and methods to assess
climate risks and policies. Some of the analysis is informed by methods and
tools already used by the World Bank globally and in LAC to undertake
climate-informed Poverty and Equity Assessments and Country Climate and
Development Reports. In other places, the report advances improved
tools or new research to understand risk and guide policies that support
low-income communities. The report draws on a series of background papers
commissioned for this report featuring original research (refer to
appendix A), case studies, and literature reviews, along with a review of
academic studies on climate and adaptation mostly from the region.
LAC countries face four pressing questions about how climate hazards and
responses affect poverty and inequality:
1. Are poor people more exposed to climate hazards?
2. What risks do climate hazards pose to the general population, and especially
poor individuals?
3. What adaptation policies are already in place to manage these risks, and how
effective are they?
4. How can countries improve and accelerate adaptation efforts to protect those
who are poor and vulnerable?
The answers to these questions, provided by this report, are summarized next.

UNVEILING HARM’S WAY: ARE POOR INDIVIDUALS MORE
EXPOSED TO CLIMATE HAZARDS?
In this report, climate hazard risk refers to expected losses—in lives, income, assets,
or well-being—caused by severe climate events. This risk depends on three factors:
1. Hazard represents the likelihood and intensity of a damaging climate event.
2. Exposure is the presence of people or assets in harm’s way.
3. Vulnerability captures the susceptibility to harm and the ability to cope or adapt.
The report examines all three components to better understand how climate
hazard risks affect different populations.
This study analyzes hurricanes, floods, landslides, droughts, and heat waves,
which together make up the bulk of climate hazards in LAC. As of 2020, about
240 million people—more than a third of LAC’s population—were exposed to at

Executive Summary

least one of these five major climate hazards. Droughts are the most widespread,
affecting 13.4 percent of the population, followed by heat waves (9.9 percent),
hurricanes (7.9 percent), and floods and landslides (6.3 percent each).
Poor people in LAC face greater exposure to natural hazards; of them,
44.6 percent are exposed to at least one severe climate hazard, compared with
34.0 percent of nonpoor people. This pattern holds across all hazards except
heat waves, where exposure is roughly equal (around 10.0 percent). The largest
gap is in drought exposure, which affects 18.6 percent of poor people versus
11.4 percent of nonpoor people.
Hotspots where a high incidence of poverty and climate hazards overlap
include the Yucatán’s Caribbean coast, along with parts of Mexico’s southern,
Gulf, and Baja California peninsula coasts; the Central American Dry Corridor
(parts of El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras, and Nicaragua); parts of the
Caribbean; northeast Brazil; the Amazonian areas of Brazil, Colombia, Ecuador,
and Peru; and the Chaco region (Argentina, Bolivia, Brazil, and Paraguay) (refer
to map ES.1). About 10 percent of the continent’s population—64.4 million
people as of 2020—live in these high-risk zones.

RISK OF CLIMATE HAZARDS: ASSESSING VULNERABILITY
AND WELFARE LOSSES
Climate hazards can drive nonpoor individuals into poverty and worsen
hardship for those who are already poor. Together, these groups are
considered vulnerable. Depending on the estimation method used in this
report, between 3 and 16 percent of the region’s population is classified as
climate vulnerable.
Poor individuals face greater climate risk. Not only are they more exposed
because they are more likely to live in high-risk areas, but they are also more
vulnerable because of the nature of their jobs (often agricultural, informal, or
low paid) and their limited assets. As a result, when climate hazards hit, they
are often forced to cut back on essentials or sell what little they have.
Poor-quality housing and limited access to safety nets only add to their
risk. Those who depend on fragile livelihoods such as rain-fed farming and
ranching face an even harder road to recovery.
Beyond short-term drops in income and consumption, climate events also
cause lingering social impacts. Comparatively few people die from climate
disaster, but many more are affected (refer to figure ES.1), and this can translate
into lost earnings, poor health and malnutrition, and disrupted schooling,

Executive Summary

which potentially affect their future productive capacity and living standards.
This report draws on several studies that document these long-term impacts
in the region. These studies (and the report’s own analysis) show that the
direct consequences of climate hazards—deaths and destruction of assets—
are proportionately greater in poor countries and that the poor people in each
country are more vulnerable and suffer more. As such, well-designed
adaptation policies represent a highly cost-effective investment in
development. Although wealthier countries and subnational regions tend to be
less affected, several poor countries have also managed to increase their
resilience.

PROTECTING POOR PEOPLE FROM CLIMATE HAZARD RISKS
Government adaptation policies to reduce poor individuals’ exposure to climate
risk are vital to tackling poverty. Because the impacts of climate hazards will
only grow in the coming decades, individuals, governments, and businesses will
need to adapt and protect themselves by lowering their exposure and
vulnerability. Many adaptation efforts are private, but some actions require
public coordination, such as establishing early warning systems or building
flood barriers. Market failures such as poor access to information, weak
property rights, and limited credit or insurance, together with equity
concerns, also make government support necessary for the poorest and
most vulnerable people.
A useful way to analyze climate risk adaptation is the prevention—insurance—
coping framework. Prevention (for example, relocating people from flood
zones) reduces the chance of losses, but when risks remain or prevention is too
costly, people and governments turn to other mechanisms to manage residual
risk. This can be self-insurance when the individual or government believe they
have enough personal resources to finance the recovery or have market
insurance (crop or property coverage, weather index products) to transfer risk
and support recovery. Yet, not all risks are insurable, and many governments or
individuals choose not to insure. Finally, coping mainly takes place after climate
hazards occur, for example, giving cash to victims. Importantly, effective coping
requires preparation and resources beforehand. The capacity to cope and adapt
(or resilience) is sometimes built ex ante, through helping to stabilize
incomes, so that households and communities have a greater capacity to
cope effectively once climate hazards occur.
This report focuses on five adaptation efforts taken by different governments in
LAC to address climate risk, spanning prevention, insurance, and coping
systems. Under prevention, the report examines forecasting and early warning
systems, protecting ecosystems such as forests through declaring protected
areas and payments for environmental services, and smarter urban planning

|

xxiii

xxiv

| Adapting to Adversity

with resilient infrastructure such as dams, sewers, roads, and clinics. Under
insurance, the report looks at how governments use a mix of risk retention
(their own funds) and risk transfer (paying others to take the risk) instruments
to insure themselves. Retention involves ex post options, such as budget
reallocations or issuing new debt, and ex ante ones that are planned ahead,
such as disaster funds or credit lines. Risk transfer tools include insurance,
reinsurance, and catastrophe bonds. Finally, under coping, the report studies
how countries with different levels of adaptive social protection manage the
impact of similar climate hazards. How widely are these five sets of tool kits
(organized around prevention, insurance, and coping systems) used in the
region, and how effective are they in protecting poor and vulnerable
individuals?
The implementation of climate adaptation systems has so far had mixed results.
Early warning systems and dams have saved lives. Effective forest management
and protecting Indigenous land rights have helped fight deforestation and
support inclusion, although legal titling alone has not always produced results
on the ground. Gray infrastructure (such as dams, pipes, and roads) has sped up
urban flood recovery. Small payments to incentivize sustainable practices can
reduce poverty and deforestation but often have a modest impact because of
weak enforcement and poor targeting.
LAC has been an incubator of innovative climate risk financing tools and
index insurance to manage climate hazards. Colombia, Mexico, and Peru,
among others, use weather insurance to fund disaster response.
Catastrophe-driven drawdown options, first launched by the World Bank
in 2008, followed by the Inter-American Development Bank and the
Development Bank of Latin America and the Caribbean—have provided
fast, reliable funding after disasters, which is especially useful for lowincome nations. The Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance Facility, the
world’s first regional parametric insurance fund, offers affordable
coverage by pooling risks for 23 countries and has delivered more than
$200 million in fast payouts since 2007. Mexico’s CADENA program
allows states to buy weather insurance and receive payouts based on
triggers such as rainfall deficits, with clear rules for distribution of funds
to poor farmers. Nicaragua’s Climate Risk Insurance program, meanwhile,
blends national-level insurance with local microinsurance to protect
against climate hazards. Although the data are still accumulating, this
report produces new evidence that some of these large-scale tools show
promise in reducing poverty and improving cost-effectiveness in disaster
response and preparation.
From 2021 to 2023, the World Bank assessed how well 14 countries were
adapting their social protection systems to handle climate hazards. Protection
systems were rated as nascent, emerging, or established on the basis of how
well they scale support, coordinate across agencies, use data, and secure

Executive Summary

financing. Where social protection systems are well established—as in
Argentina, Brazil, Chile, the Dominican Republic, and Uruguay—the poverty
gap tends not to widen after climate hazards materialize. In countries with
nascent or emerging systems, however, the poverty gap can subsequently
spike for years (refer to figure ES.2). Well-established adaptive systems are
becoming more common, helping communities bounce back faster. Some
countries are coupling cash transfers with asset-building programs to help
people build sustainable livelihoods after the cash support ends, boosting
climate-ready safety nets that speed recovery.
Most countries in LAC are taking steps to help people adapt to climate risks.
Yet progress is uneven, with many countries still lacking key climate risk tools
or having developed only partial adaptation systems. In the 14 LAC countries
reviewed, risk retention tools (such as disaster funds) were used twice as
often as risk transfer tools. This reflects the fact that the former are typically
designed to cover frequent, low-impact events. Few countries use budget
reallocations, and advanced tools such as parametric insurance or catastrophe
bonds remain rare. Only Jamaica, Mexico, and Panama use a balanced, layered
mix of tools.

xxv

xxvi

| Adapting to Adversity

The World Bank assessment of adaptive social protection systems in the region
finds that many remain nascent. In Honduras and Panama, fewer than
40 percent of vulnerable people receive cash transfers. Digital payments are
improving, but many systems still lack modern platforms and updated
registries. Weak coordination between disaster and social protection
agencies further delays emergency response.
Countries should adopt climate adaptation instruments and systems that
integrate prevention, insurance, and coping mechanisms. A traffic-light
diagnostic across the region reveals wide disparities: Although some countries
have built robust systems across all dimensions, others still face significant gaps.
Brazil and Costa Rica, for instance, demonstrate strong readiness in multiple
areas, enabling them to manage risks more effectively even under high exposure.
In contrast, Honduras faces greater vulnerability and has fewer tools to mitigate
the impact of climate hazards that materialize (refer to table ES.1). The traffic
light assessment is not a ranking but a regional snapshot of risk profiles and a
benchmark for progress. It highlights the need for tailored strategies based on
each country’s specific vulnerabilities and risk profile. For some, the priority may
be expanding access to resilient infrastructure and contingency financing; for
others, scaling up social protection or early warning systems may yield the
greatest impact. Ultimately, countries that invest in coordinated, layered
adaptation instruments and systems are better positioned to protect their
populations and limit long-term climate-related losses.

IMPROVING ADAPTATION
Ideally, decision-makers should assess their country policy systems and strive
to build comprehensive climate adaptation systems simultaneously,
encompassing prevention, insurance, and coping. Where this is not feasible,
different priorities emerge depending on the country context.
More can—and should—be done. Development can be a good adaptation policy.
Wealthier countries face fewer climate-related losses, but some poorer nations
have made progress in building resilience. However, there is a notable gap
across systems for some low- and low middle-income countries. To further
reduce climate risks, the region needs a strategy to accelerate adaptation
focused on providing more and better information for individuals and the
private sector, stronger incentives to reduce their exposure, more investment in
adaptation, and inclusive political institutions.

xxviii

| Adapting to Adversity

Adaptation policies can be improved and accelerated. Prevention saves lives
and money, but governments often wait for climate hazards to strike.
Forecasting and warning systems are underfunded and weak in some
countries, and people do not always respond to alerts. Safe housing, dams,
roads, and hospitals can reduce damage and speed recovery, but these
investments sometimes get overlooked. Aid and insurance are not always fast
or reliable. Many people across LAC still live in climate hazard–prone areas to
be closer to work or services, and practices such as deforestation often make
things worse, especially for poor people. Yet people’s choices to stay in danger
zones are not always understood: They may underestimate risks or lack better
options. Effective adaptation needs both individual and collective action
focused on four things:
1. Providing smart incentives,
2. Getting the right information to people,
3. Making sure investments have benefits that outweigh costs, and
4. Ensuring political institutions support those most at risk.
Providing strong incentives helps encourage individuals and the private
sector to take steps to reduce their exposure and vulnerability to climate
hazards. Clear property rights, for instance, can boost resilience in urban
slums by making it more likely that people will invest in floodproofing their
homes. Building roads, providing buses to get to work, restoring parks, or
even cutting back services in extremely hazardous locations can nudge people
to reconsider their location, especially if their lives are at risk.
Good information is key to helping people and governments adapt.
National climate science institutions can fine-tune their predictions,
tapping into artificial intelligence and partnering with the private sector,
and clear and trusted communication from the authorities helps people
prepare and respond to climate hazards. Better data also support smarter
economic tools, such as setting fair insurance premiums or targeting cash
transfers during emergencies. When property values and insurance prices
reflect real climate risks, people can make safer choices, and governments
can plan better. Open access to data; active media; and transparent,
trackable relief payments all help build trust and maximize impact,
keeping public officials accountable.
As climate risks rise and budgets tighten, investment in adaptation is more
urgent than ever, especially because many of those investments also boost
broader development. According to the United Nations Environment
Programme, LAC may need to invest up to $55 billion per year in climate
adaptation in the remaining years until 2030, but funding remains slow,
limited, and skewed toward mitigation. Grants are scarce and do not always
reach those most in need. Development banks can help by scaling capital,

Executive Summary

using innovative tools, and partnering with the private sector. Examples of
the latter include Fideicomisos Instituidos en Relación con la Agricultura, the
region’s first green resilience bond, which supports climate-smart farming in
Mexico, and the Disaster Risk Insurance and Finance in Central America
Consortium, a public-private collaboration to expand agriculture insurance
for smallholder farmers in El Salvador, Guatemala, and Honduras.
Governments can attract more private and multilateral finance by improving
legal frameworks, expanding access to climate data, and embedding
adaptation into their national development plans.
Accelerating climate adaptation in LAC is not just about money or
improving technical aspects; it is also about politics. Political institutions
shape how resources are used and often favor short-term relief or visible
projects with immediate payoffs over long-term solutions such as
resilient infrastructure or relocation. Relief often gets more attention than
risk reduction because recovery and payouts bring votes. This short-term
focus hurts the poorest people, who have the least political influence but
need adaptation the most. Shifting incentives toward effective, long-term
action requires informed citizens and stronger governmental accountability.
Audits, transparency, and local oversight can push leaders to prioritize
effective, equitable adaptation.

ORGANIZATION OF THIS REPORT
This report is organized into four chapters. Chapter 1 (“Unveiling Harm’s
Way: Are Poor Individuals More Exposed to Climate Hazards?”) maps where
climate hazards are more prevalent and intense and how they overlap with
people—especially those who are poor—across countries and cities. Chapter 2
(“Risk of Climate Hazards: Assessing Vulnerability and Welfare Losses”)
determines the extent and profile of those vulnerable to climate risk in the
region, especially focusing on those who could fall into poverty (or become
poorer) because of climate hazards. It then looks at impacts on mortality,
assets, health, education, and (long-term) productivity, sometimes broken
down by country income level. Chapter 3 (“Protecting Poor People from
Climate Hazard Risks”) reviews the scope of five public adaptation systems in
the region—early warning systems, gray (for example, dams and roads) and
green (forests) resilient infrastructure, insurance, and adaptive safety nets—
and how well they protect populations from climate risk. Chapter 4
(“Improving Adaptation”) explores why adaptation remains weak in many
places, highlighting barriers such as low income, high costs, weak markets,
and political disincentives. The chapter also offers solutions to strengthen
adaptation through smarter policies to align incentives, better information
systems, and political institutions to protect poor individuals, and
mobilization of more resources for adaptation.

Unveiling Harm’s Way
ARE POOR INDIVIDUALS MORE
EXPOSED TO CLIMATE HAZARDS?

INTRODUCTION
To understand the risk of climate hazards, it is necessary to understand
the components of risk. Risk can be defined as the size of losses that are
likely to occur because of an event. In the case of climate hazards, it refers
to the expected of losses (in terms of lives, assets, incomes, livelihoods, or
some other measure of loss) that are likely to occur because of a severe
climate event.
Usually, climate hazard risk is understood as the result of the combination of
three factors: hazard, exposure, and vulnerability. A hazard is the potential
occurrence of a natural or human-induced physical event or trend that may
cause loss of life, injury, or other health impacts, as well as damage to and loss
of property, infrastructure, livelihoods, service provision, ecosystems, and
environmental resources. Exposure is the presence of people, assets, livelihoods,
infrastructure, and ecosystems in places and settings that could be adversely
affected by climate hazards. Vulnerability is the propensity or predisposition to
be adversely affected. It encompasses a variety of concepts and elements,
including sensitivity or susceptibility to harm and lack of capacity to cope and
adapt (sometimes called resilience). Figure 1.1 is a representation of this
understanding of climate risk.
This chapter concentrates on the hazard and exposure components of climate
risk in Latin America and the Caribbean (LAC). We compute the number of
people, poor and nonpoor, exposed to a selected list of climate hazards of
special importance to the region. Chapter 2 discusses the sources of
vulnerability to these hazards and the risk they pose in terms of selected
indicators of well-being.

1

2

| Adapting to Adversity

A HAZARD-PRONE REGION
Climate hazards tend to strike many people or households in a community or
region simultaneously, but, depending on multiple factors, they usually affect
people and households differently. The frequency of hazards varies. Minor
floods occur frequently, whereas category five hurricanes are rarer. Moreover,
the severity of hazards can range from catastrophic, such as a drought that
leads to famine, to minor, such as a few days with poor rains. Climate hazards
also differ in their onset speed. For example, droughts and heat waves are slowonset events, but floods or hurricanes often have a rapid onset. In this section,
we select and define levels of severity of the climate hazards affecting the
population of the LAC region and account for the number of people, poor and
nonpoor, exposed to these events.
Climate hazards are pervasive in LAC because of its geographical location.
Lying within the North Atlantic basin hurricane belt, the Caribbean and Central
America are subject to frequent hurricanes and intense storms that can lead to
extensive damage from strong winds, storm surges, and extreme precipitation.
Storms and hurricanes make landfall every year, and in some parts of the region
they can occur as often as multiple times per year or as rarely as every five to
10 years. Additionally, river flooding is fairly frequent across the continent, and
the region is also vulnerable to flash floods and landslides from severe storms.

Unveiling Harm’s Way

Floods, hurricanes, and droughts are not new to the region. A severe
drought coupled with deforestation may have contributed to the collapse of
the Mayan civilization. Historical Spanish documents reveal the first
reported Atlantic hurricane in the Caribbean basin occurred in 1494 at
Isabella, Santo Domingo. When Columbus arrived at Hispaniola (the island
in the western Caribbean that is now divided into the Dominican Republic
and Haiti) on June 29, 1502, his ship had weathered a storm but was
denied docking by the governor. Although Columbus himself
survived the weather, the fleet that departed was later destroyed by a
hurricane.
Although storms, floods, and droughts have gotten most of the attention, other
hazards such as heat waves and wildfires are also becoming more common and
more intense. This study looks at five key climate hazards in the region—
hurricanes, floods, landslides, droughts, and heat waves—because they make up
the bulk of climate events in the region.1 According to the Emergency Events
Database (EM-DAT), floods and storms (dominated by hurricanes in LAC) are
the most common, making up nearly half of all weather- and climate-related
disasters between 2003 and 2023 (refer to figure 1.2). Droughts and landslides
come next, followed by heat waves and wildfires.
Extreme climate hazards are not just happening more often, they are hitting
harder than ever. The Amazon drought in 2023 was the worst on record, with
the Negro River near Manaus, Brazil, dropping to its lowest level since
measurements began 120 years ago. Hurricane Otis jumped from a tropical
storm to a category five hurricane in just 12 hours, and Beryl became the
earliest category five hurricane ever recorded. There is an urgent need to
study the most effective ways to reduce the costs of these events, especially
because the poorest households are those hit hardest. Weather- and climaterelated disasters have increased fivefold, according to the World
Meteorological Organization. Our own analysis based on EM-DAT data
corroborates this increase.2
Countries experience climate hazards in unique ways, but some contend
with multiple hazards, each with varying significance. Hurricanes are
recurrent on the Atlantic and Pacific coasts of Central America and Mexico
and on the Caribbean islands. Floods and landslides affect coastal regions of
South America and some of its inland areas. Droughts are more prevalent
across semiarid tropical regions, such as northeast Brazil, the Central
American Dry Corridor, and Northern Mexico. Figure 1.3 shows that Mexico
and the Caribbean countries most often get hit by storms and floods. In
Central America, droughts are also recurrent. In South America, the top
climate hazard is mainly floods. But just tracking how often and where these
events happen does not tell about how many people are actually
exposed to them.

5

At the same time as climate hazards are increasing, years of economic expansion
in Latin America may have spurred migration into and development of hazardprone areas. This trend is noticeable in booming coastal cities and towns across
the Yucatán Peninsula and in growing cities in the Caribbean, Central America,
and South American floodplains alike. Hispaniola’s population was sparse in the
precolonial era but the Dominican Republic and Haiti now house millions
exposed to hurricanes and rank among the world’s most storm-exposed nations.
Similarly, the Yucatán Peninsula, where many cities were abandoned between
800 and 950 AD after the collapse of the Mayan civilization, has again become
one of Mexico’s fastest-growing, high climate risk population zones. Migration
into hazardous regions is not solely the result of lack of knowledge.
So, how many people are actually exposed to these hazards in LAC? To figure
that out, one can combine maps of where climate hazards happen with maps
showing where people live. By matching them up at the grid-cell level
(basically, a small territorial unit defined by data availability), one can estimate
how many people live in areas exposed to each hazard—or even multiple ones.
This is a two-step procedure.
First, a map of the distribution of climate hazards over the territory of the
continent is needed. Data for each hazard come with a spatial resolution, which
refers to the geographic correlation of this hazard over a specific territory. For
example, a data set with a 3-arcsecond spatial resolution has grid cells
corresponding to a square area of approximately 100 meters by 100 meters at the
equator. Then, for each hazard, an event is considered severe on the basis of
specific thresholds of intensity and frequency. Hence, every unit of the territory
is assigned a dichotomous variable if it is above or below these thresholds.
Namely, the territory is designated as either prone to a high-severity event or
not. Admittedly, every territory is exposed to some probability of a severe climate
event, but, because the probability is above or below a certain predetermined
threshold, this transformation designates a territory as either exposed to a severe
event or not. Box 1.1 describes the sources of data for the five climate hazards
included in this study, as well as the thresholds that define a severe event.3

Box 1.1

Climate hazards data
This study looks at five climate hazards: floods,
landslides, droughts, heat waves, and hurricanes.
These events are defined by how intense they are
and how often they occur (known as the return
period). For example, a 100-year return-period event
is expected to happen once every 100 years or has
about a 1 percent chance of happening in any given

year. The thresholds used focus on events likely to
cause serious damage, following Doan et al. (2023)
and Hill et al. (2025). The analysis uses modeled
probabilistic hazard data that consider variability in
the weather over several decades and do not
represent a single historical year. Only drought is
deterministic, given the availability of data sources.
continued

6

| Adapting to Adversity

Box 1.1 Climate hazards data (continued)

• Floods: The analysis covers three main types
of flooding: fluvial (river), pluvial (surface
water), and coastal floods. The flood hazard
data are sourced from the 2019 Fathom global
flood data set (version 2.0; https://www​.fathom​
.global/product/global-flood-map/) for fluvial
and pluvial floods and the Deltares coastal
flood data set (https://microsoft.github.io​
/­AIforEarthDataSets/data/deltares-floods.html)
for storm-surge-related flooding. The analysis
considers a threshold of at least 50 centimeters
maximum inundation depth for a 100-year
return period.
• Landslides: Landslide hazards are assessed
using the Global Landslide Hazard map
(https://datacatalog.worldbank.org/search​
/­dataset/0037584/global-landslide-hazard​
-map), which estimates the annual frequency
of landslides triggered by both rainfall and
seismic activity. The analysis combines the
highest frequency of either trigger to create a
comprehensive hazard profile. Rainfall-induced
landslides are based on historical data from
1980 to 2018, and seismic-induced landslides
account for unpredictable but potentially
widespread events. The analysis focuses on
landslide size of at least greater than 100 square
meters for a 100-year return period.
• Droughts: The analysis uses the Food and
Agriculture Organization of the United Nations
Historic Agricultural Drought Frequency data
set, which identifies severe drought occurrences
over a 39-year period (1984–2022). This data
set is based on the Agricultural Stress Index
(https://www.fao.org/giews/earthobservation​
/asis/index_1.jsp?lang=en), which uses remote
sensing data such as the Vegetation Health
Index to monitor crop stress resulting from
drought. Locations where at least 30 percent of
cropland or grassland is affected in any growing
season are classified as exposed in any year over
a 39-year period.

• Heat waves: The study incorporates fiveday heat wave maps from the World Bank’s
Climate Change Knowledge Portal (https://
climateknowledgeportal.worldbank.org/),
which uses the Environmental Stress Index
(ESI) as a proxy for wet bulb globe temperature.
The data set models heat wave intensities
based on temperature, humidity, and solar
radiation. To improve accuracy, the ESI is
adjusted for underestimation using correction
factors. Historical data from 1950 to 2022 are
detrended and fitted to generalized extreme
value distributions to estimate return levels for
five-day heat wave events. The analysis then
considers a threshold of at least 33°C maximum
five-day average of daily peak ESI values for a
100-year return period.
• Hurricanes: Hurricane exposure is assessed
with the Synthetic Tropical Cyclone Generation
Model (STORM) by Bloemendaal et al. (2020).
This data set extends historical hurricane track
data from the International Best Track Archive
for Climate Stewardship by resampling and
simulating 10,000 years of hurricane activity.
The analysis defines exposure on the basis of
wind speed thresholds, categorizing locations as
vulnerable if the 10-minute average maximum
wind speed exceeds 37.6 meters per second
(category two) for a 100-year return period.
Although the STORM data set primarily focuses
on wind hazards, associated storm surge and
heavy precipitation impacts are incorporated
through flood hazard maps.
The data for hazards and population come from
different sources and vary in resolution. Among the
five hazards, the flood layer has the highest spatial
resolution; the other four hazards have lower spatial
resolutions. The sources of data for each hazard and
the thresholds for the definition of severe climate
events (in terms of return period and intensity of
the event) are summarized in table B1.1.1.

These are absolute thresholds for each
hazard. This means that, like international
poverty lines, they may not be as relevant in every
country. They identify people relatively more
exposed at a global or regional level. Some areas
might have more heat waves compared with other
parts of the same country, but this does not mean
the level of heat is the same as that experienced in

tropical regions. The results also depend on the
variables used to measure hazards and the data
sources that are documented. There are many
ways to measure droughts and heat waves, for
example. We are consistent across countries for
global work. Country-specific work might use
appropriate thresholds in the same way as it uses
national poverty lines.
continued

8

| Adapting to Adversity

Box 1.1 Climate hazards data (continued)

Moreover, it is important to underscore that these
thresholds define a dichotomous variable of highor low-severity events and then define a
population as exposed or not exposed. But the
entire population is to some degree exposed to
climate hazards. These thresholds are somewhat
arbitrary, and a different definition of the

intensity and return period of the event would
render different exposure rate estimates.
The estimates included in this study follow
previous research by the World Bank and are
intended to facilitate international comparisons
between countries and regions.

Second, a map of the percentage of the population exposed to severe hazards is
produced for each hazard and a combination of hazards. The five binary hazard
layers are combined into one multihazard layer, where each grid cell contains
information on which hazards the cell is exposed to. The final step is to
weight each of the single-hazard maps, as well as the multihazard map, with
information about population in each similar cell. Given that these cells can be
grouped into specific administrative units, this allows us to calculate the
number of people exposed to any given combination of hazards without
double-counting individuals exposed to multiple hazards. Exposure rates are
then defined as the percentage of the population in cells of high-severity
hazard within the total population of a given administrative unit.4 Map 1.1
shows the population exposure rate of the five hazards described in box 1.1,
separately. Map 1.1f provides the population exposure rates to any of these
five hazards.
High flood exposure (that is, more than 60 percent of the population exposed)
of the total population of a given area is seen on the Pacific Coast and in the
Amazonian plains of southern Colombia, some municipalities of western
Ecuador, and the west and northwest jungles of Peru, as well as the upper and
delta regions of the Amazon River in Brazil (refer to map 1.1a). High exposure
to landslides is seen in the municipalities across the Andean mountain chain of
Colombia, Ecuador, Peru, and the La Paz department of Bolivia, as well as some
municipalities in the Atacama and Coquimbo regions of northern Chile, the
western and eastern slopes of Sierra Madre del Sur in Mexico, and southern
Guatemala (refer to map 1.1b).
High exposure to drought is seen in vast sections of the Brazilian northeast,
Andean mountain chain in Peru, southern Bolivia, Paraguay, central Chile, the
plains of western Colombia, the western half of Honduras, Nicaragua, the
Pacific coast of Panama, and the Sierra Madre Occidental and Oriental in
Mexico (refer to map 1.1c). High rates of population exposure to heat waves are

Caribbean coast of the Yucatán Peninsula; Belize and parts of Central America;
the area of the Amazon River around Manaus in the Brazilian states of
Amazonas and Para; and the Chaco region of Argentina, Brazil, and Paraguay
(refer to map 1.1d).

Finally, high population exposure to hurricanes is concentrated in the
Caribbean islands, most of the coast of the Yucatán Peninsula, the eastern parts
of Honduras and Nicaragua, the southern tip of the California peninsula, and
the central-eastern Pacific coast of Mexico (refer to map 1.1e).
Although vast sections of the region have low exposure to some hazards (for
example, all of South America has low exposure to hurricanes), almost the
whole continent has a high proportion of the population exposed to any of the
five considered in this study (refer to map 1.1f ). This calls for preparedness
suitable to the different climate hazards faced by different population groups
and locations.

MORE POOR PEOPLE IN HARM’S WAY?
Severe climate hazards are spread across much of LAC’s territory, which is why
so many people in the region live in high-risk areas. But are these highexposure areas also where poverty is most common? Are poor regions and
neighborhoods more exposed to severe climate hazards than wealthier ones? In
this section, we break down exposure rates by type of hazard, comparing poor
and nonpoor populations across the region—and in a few selected countries.

Unveiling Harm’s Way

An increasingly less poor but highly unequal region
According to most recent estimates, poverty in LAC dropped to its lowest
point thus far this century, with one in four people living below the uppermiddle-income-country poverty line of $6.85 per person per day (2017
purchasing power parity).5 The poverty rate for the region was 27.1 percent in
2020 (the reference year for the maps), increased to 27.8 percent in 2021, and
has since declined to 22.9 in 2024 (estimate), well below the poverty rate a
decade ago (28.3 percent in 2015) and half the level at the beginning of the
century (49.8 percent in 2000). This equates to around 151 million people
experiencing poverty in the region as of 2024 (down from around 175 million
in 2020). A profile of the poor in LAC shows that they are more likely to be
younger, have lower levels of educational attainment, and live in rural areas
than population in the vulnerable group and the middle class.
Latin America continues to be one of the most unequal regions in the world.
However, there is an important dispersion among countries in the region. Brazil
and Colombia are the countries with the highest inequality levels as measured
with the Gini coefficient (53.9 points and 51.5 points as of 2023, respectively),
whereas the Dominican Republic has the lowest inequality levels in the region,
with a Gini coefficient below 40 points. However, over the past two decades,
most countries in the region have experienced a slight decline in the Gini
coefficient (World Bank 2024b).6

When climate hazards meet poverty
The link between climate hazards and poverty is elusive, and causality is
difficult to establish empirically. Among other reasons is the two-way
relationship between the vulnerability to poverty and climate hazards. Poor
individuals struggle to protect themselves adequately from climate hazards
because of their lower physical and human capital. Whole communities are
often affected, undermining the effectiveness of coinsurance, particularly
when combined with the limited resources of impoverished households.
At the same time, whether in rural or urban settings, poor individuals are
often compelled to inhabit hazard-prone areas, such as steep lands or squatter
settlements where land ownership is more accessible and rents are affordable,
to be closer to jobs. Poverty itself sometimes encourages practices that
increase exposure to climate hazards. The use of inexpensive yet low-quality
housing materials and engagement in unsafe or less-resilient activities, such
as rain-fed agriculture, exacerbates exposure. Chapter 2 discusses this in
more detail.
An initial method for examining the connection between climate and poverty
involves quantifying the impoverished population in regions exposed to climate
hazards without implying direct causal relationships.7 This entails overlaying
poverty maps with modeled hazard maps for various events and probabilities.
Beyond just the likelihood of facing a hurricane or coastal floods, understanding

|

11

12

| Adapting to Adversity

the geographical distribution of hazards is crucial for decision-making.
This enables the assessment of exposure, which includes evaluating the
number of people that could be affected if a hazard occurs.
On the basis of map 1.1, produced for this report, just over a third of LAC’s
population—around 240 million people as of 2020—is exposed to at least one of
the five major climate hazards included in this study. Droughts top the list,
affecting 13.4 percent of the population. Heat waves and hurricanes follow,
reaching 9.9 and 7.9 percent of the region’s population, respectively, and
landslides and floods each affect 6.3 percent. The rest of the population—about
63.0 percent—is also exposed to climate hazards, but not at levels considered
severe in this study (refer to table 1.1).8
People living in poverty have a higher exposure to climate hazards than
nonpoor people. About 44.6 percent of poor people are exposed to at least one
severe climate hazard, compared with 34.0 percent of nonpoor people. Poor
individuals have higher exposure rates than nonpoor individuals across all five
hazards considered—except for heat waves, for which both groups are equally
exposed (around 10 percent). The biggest gap in exposure rates between poor
and nonpoor people is found for droughts: Nearly one in five poor people are
exposed, versus just one in 10 nonpoor people (refer to table 1.1). This gap is
partly explained by the higher incidence of poverty in rural areas, where
droughts are more common.
Most people are exposed to only one hazard (30.7 percent), with far fewer
exposed to two (5.6 percent) or three (0.6 percent). Fewer than 0.1 percent deal
with four or five hazards. The trend is similar for both poor and nonpoor
individuals, but poor individuals are more affected—37.8 percent versus
28.7 percent for one hazard and 7.8 versus 4.8 percent for two. So although a
large percentage of the population is exposed to at least one hazard, very few
are exposed to multiple ones. And most people are exposed to different hazards,
which makes policies for prevention and protection more complex because a
single policy may not be appropriate for the variety of climate hazards and risks
involved. Chapters 3 and 4 dig into this more.
Table 1.1 shows how many poor people are exposed and how their exposure
rates compare with those of nonpoor people. But where are poor individuals
actually located? In terms of poverty incidence, the continent looks almost
bimodal. Regions with low poverty (less than 10 percent) are mostly in
Argentina, Chile, Uruguay, Santa Cruz in northern Bolivia, Lima in Peru,
Cundinamarca and other central provinces in Colombia, southern Brazil,
Panama, central Costa Rica, and parts of Mexico, such as the Valle de México
and the area around Monterrey city in Northern Mexico. In most cases, these
areas are usually connected to the large metropolitan areas of the capital cities,
showing a clear rural-urban poverty divide in the region. Most of the rest of the
continent, though, has poverty rates of more than 20 or even 30 percent (refer
to box 1.2 for a description of the region’s poverty maps).

Box 1.2

Poverty measurement and poverty maps in LAC
Poverty rates and profiles do not give a detailed
description of the location of poor households
beyond the urban-rural divide. In some cases,
poverty rates can be produced at subnational,
county, or locality levels, but in others such
data do not exist. Poverty mapping is then a
useful technique.
Poverty maps are a well-known approach to
studying the geographical distribution of
welfare in general and poverty in particular.
They are used to add spatial granularity to
welfare measurement that is not usually
available from other sources of data (usually
surveys) that are not representative at the local
or even provincial level. Poverty maps use
census and survey data, together with statistical
and econometric techniques, known as small
area estimation, to estimate measures of welfare
at smaller levels of geographic territory than
conventional surveys permit. There is now a
large literature on the advantages of this type of
measure and the methods used to build them—
for instance, Bedi, Coudouel, and Simler (2007);
Corral, Henderson, and Segovia (2025); Elbers,
Lanjouw, and Lanjouw (2003); and Molina and
Rao (2010).
This report uses poverty maps derived from
Brunckhorst et al. (2026), who use data from
two sources. First, national poverty maps at the

municipal level provide official estimates of the
share of poor households at the administrative
boundary level 2 (that is, county or
municipality) using the national poverty
threshold in the local currency units. These
poverty maps are available for eight countries:
Brazil, Chile, Colombia, Ecuador, Honduras,
Mexico, Panama, and Peru. Second, the World
Bank’s Poverty and Inequality Platform
(https://pip.worldbank.org/home) provides the
share of poor households at the administrative
boundary level 1, (that is, province or state), for
most other countries in the region. In a few
cases (for example, Suriname, República
Bolivariana de Venezuela), the poverty map
rate is at the national level. All the maps from
this platform are produced using an
international poverty line of $6.85 in 2017
purchasing power parity (PPP) units (refer to
map B1.2.1). The authors use an iterative
algorithm to convert poverty rates using
official estimates into poverty rates under the
international poverty line of -$6.85 in PPP
units, so that poverty rates in the maps are
under the same poverty line for all countries.
Finally, the surveys and maps used for this
exercise refer to, or are near to, 2020 (except
for Panama, where they refer to data for 2015,
and República Bolivariana de Venezuela, for
2006).

Laying a poverty map over a climate hazard exposure map (such as map 1.1f, the
climate hazard map, with map 1.2.1, the poverty map) can serve to identify the
hotspots of high incidence of poverty and exposure to climate hazards. The
dark blue areas in map 1.2 show regions where more than 60 percent of the
population are exposed to any hazard and poverty rates are above 30 percent.
These hotspots include the Yucatán’s Caribbean coast along with parts of
Mexico’s southern and Gulf and Baja California peninsula coasts; the Dry
Corridor (parts of El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras, and Nicaragua) and parts
of the Caribbean; northeast Brazil; Amazonian areas of Brazil, Colombia,
Ecuador, and Peru; and the Chaco region (Argentina, Bolivia, Brazil, Paraguay).
About 10 percent of the continent’s population—64.4 million people as of
2020—live in these high-risk zones.
If one widens the lens to include areas with poverty rates above 10 percent
and more than 30 percent of the population being exposed, that covers about
40.6 percent of the population in LAC.

Unveiling Harm’s Way

Because of its size and shape, countries in LAC face very different exposures
to climate events. On top of that, differences in socioeconomic development
imply that exposure levels vary not just between countries, but also between
the poor and nonpoor groups within them.9 A couple of examples help to
illustrate these differences.
Chile has an exposure rate to any hazard well below the continent’s average.
Only 15.8 percent of the population is exposed to severe climate hazards.
Like all other countries, the exposure rate is higher among poor individuals
(20.6 percent), and it is mostly high drought exposure (16.5 percent).
In addition, there is almost no difference in exposure between poor and
nonpoor people for the other four hazards. Only 1 percent of the population is
exposed to two hazards, and none to two or more. Chile is—according to this
methodology—a country with low exposure to severe climate hazards.
Colombia, in contrast, stands out with the highest rate of population exposure
to any of these five hazards among the eight countries studied—40.5 percent of
its population is exposed. It is followed closely by Honduras (39.7 percent),
Mexico (38.9 percent), and Ecuador (36.6 percent). As with the regional trend,
poor people in Colombia face a higher exposure rate than nonpoor people,
especially to droughts and heat waves. But unlike the regional average, poor
people in Colombia are more exposed to heat waves, likely because these occur
in both large cities, such as Cartagena, and poorer areas, such as the Guajira
Peninsula. Most of the population in Colombia are exposed to just one hazard,
similar to the regional pattern, but a sizable group is exposed to two hazards.
Here, the gap between poor and nonpoor people is significant: Of the poor
people, 11.5 percent are exposed to two hazards, compared with just 5.2 percent
of the nonpoor people—a 6.3 percentage point difference, the largest among all
countries analyzed.
In summary, poor individuals are more exposed to climate hazards than
nonpoor individuals in LAC, particularly to droughts and landslides. But
nonpoor individuals also exhibit significant rates of exposure to these hazards
and, in some cases, for some events (for instance, heat waves in Brazil, landslides
in Ecuador, or hurricanes in Mexico), the exposure gap between poor and
nonpoor individuals reverses. Most of the population is exposed to one hazard,
with fewer people exposed to multiple hazards. Geographic hotspots include the
Brazilian northeast, the upper Amazon region, the Chaco region, Caribbean
islands, and parts of Mexico and Central America. These areas require special
policy attention because of high hazard exposure and poverty rates.
Looking at countries with more disaggregated poverty data, major differences
are seen in exposure to climate hazards across countries. For instance, some
(such as Chile) have lower exposure rates and others (such as Colombia) have
very high exposure rates; in some countries (such as Ecuador) high exposure
rates are concentrated in only some parts of the country, and in others (such as
Mexico) exposure rates are high in almost the entire territory. There is also a

|

18

| Adapting to Adversity

highly differentiated exposure to floods between poor and noonpoor in the
Dominican Republic, using an alternative method to stratify risk exposure
(refer to box 1.3)

Box 1.3

Exposure to severe flood risk across socioeconomic groups
An innovative way to assess the exposure of
households to climate hazards by wealth levels is
to stratify risk exposure into a household survey
with national coverage by geographic domains
and then assess the population in high-risk areas.
This is what a 2021 household flood risk survey
did in the Dominican Republic. According to
the survey, 2.8 million people, equivalent to
27 percent of the Dominican Republic’s
population, live in areas at high risk of flooding.
Almost half (46 percent) of the population who
live in highly flood-prone areas are poor (belong
to the bottom 40 percent of the wealth index).

Wealthier households are less likely to live in
areas at high risk of flooding. Thus, poor
households are the most vulnerable to flooding.
Among the bottom two quintiles of wealth
distribution, one in every four households is at
high risk of flooding. In rural areas, the share of
the poor population living in high-risk areas is
around 29 percent, falling to 25 percent in urban
centers. The incidence of high flood risk among
better-off households (the top quintile of the
wealth distribution) is around 14 percent in
urban areas and 10 percent in rural areas (refer
to figure B1.3.1).

Unveiling Harm’s Way

FLOODS IN CITIES: ARE POOR INDIVIDUALS ALWAYS MORE
EXPOSED? NOT NECESSARILY
The urbanization of Latin America has been very fast, with “no parallel in the
history of the world” (Fox 2012; Kemper 2022).10 In 2024, 82 percent of the
continental population was living in urban areas (refer to figure 1.4).
Urbanization plays a crucial role in development. The benefits of scale,
agglomeration economies, and specialization increase productivity and
prosperity. This draws people to migrate to cities from rural settings. The
density of cities promotes productivity and offers opportunities to improve
people’s livelihoods and quality of life, eventually helping to lift many out of
poverty. For firms and workers in urban areas, proximity makes skills matching
and job searching more efficient. For governments, basic public services and
infrastructure can be provided at a lower cost, thanks to economies of scale.

Urban poor exposure to floods
Population growth in cities and urbanization may also imply that more people
are moving into harm’s way. Rapid urban expansion can lead to congestion
effects, including increasing vulnerability to climate hazards and amplifying
strain on public services and infrastructure. Such challenges are especially
prevalent in low- to middle-income nations where there is insufficient capacity
for risk-aware urban planning and limited resources for investing in
transportation and protective infrastructure, especially in unplanned and
informal settlements.

Estimates suggest that as much as 80 percent of losses caused by climate
hazards in Latin America occur in urban areas, where most of the population
lives. Latin American cities are particularly exposed to floods, landslides, heat
stress, and wildfires (Mukim and Roberts 2022). Furthermore, 60 of the 77 most
densely populated cities in LAC are coastal and are experiencing sea levels
rising at a faster rate than globally. The World Bank estimates that 2.6 percent
of the region’s population, totaling some 17 million people, will be internally
displaced because of climate change by 2050, which will increase urban growth
by 10 percent over current projections (Clement et al. 2021).
The importance of urbanization in LAC makes it crucial to look closely at how
cities are exposed to climate hazards. The previous section covered nationallevel exposure to climate hazards—how many are exposed and where. This
section complements the preceding analysis by zooming in on selected cities in
Colombia and Mexico to see how climate hazards and poverty intersect. These
two countries were chosen for the new analytical work conducted for this
report because data were available and the cities face varying levels of flood
risk.11 Using global flood maps and local neighborhood vulnerability data, we
estimate the number and location of people exposed to climate hazards and
determine whether poorer communities are more exposed to climate hazards
than nonpoor communities.
As in the preceding section, the maps are produced in two stages. First, flood
hazard maps offer inundation depths along a continuous scale, and the values
are aggregated into five hazard categories12 based on a 100-year return period
for all types of flooding (that is, coastal, pluvial, and fluvial floods with an
annual probability of occurrence higher than 1 percent) for all territorial
units of 30 meters × 30 meters within each studied city. High hazard is then
defined as floods with inundation depths above 0.50 meter. Second, a
multidimensional poverty index using indicators of housing, demographics,
education, labor, and health renders a six-category indicator ranging from 1
(most poor) to 6 (most affluent), where areas with a multidimensional poor
population are those with an indicator below 4.13 Unlike the rest of the
chapter, which looks at income-based poverty, this section uses
multidimensional poverty because it relies on census data to measure wellbeing. The overlay of these two maps simplifies the distribution of the
territory into four quadrants: low flood risk/low poverty, low flood risk/high
poverty, high flood risk/low poverty, and high flood risk/high poverty.
Map 1.3 shows the city of Cali, Colombia. Map 1.3a shows flood hazard
areas, where the Cauca River raises the threat in the northern part of the
city. Map 1.3b displays where the multidimensional poor population lives,

Unveiling Harm’s Way

|

21

mostly in the northwest and northeast fringes of the city. In map 1.3c, both
layers are overlaid, showing precisely that the northeast part of the city has
the highest overlap between flood hazard and vulnerable communities.
Some less-poor groups are also exposed to high flood hazard—such as in the
far south and along Calle 70 in the northeast—providing a reminder that
exposure to high flood hazard is not limited to the poorest individuals.
For example, looking at the cities’ total population, the poorest residents
are not always more exposed to flood hazard, but in some cities, they are.
In Cali, the poor population is more likely to be exposed to high flood
hazard than the less-poor population by more than 20 percentage points.
In Barranquilla, Cartagena, Medellín, and Guadalajara, the poor
population is also more exposed, but with a narrower difference. In
contrast, Bogotá, Valle de México, and Puebla-Tlaxcala show the nonpoor
population being more exposed than the poor population by a minimal
margin. In Monterrey and Tijuana, both groups show similar exposure
rates (refer to table 1.2).
