In relative terms, poor individuals consistently suffer greater losses from
floods than nonpoor individuals. Although wealthier individuals may
experience higher absolute losses of assets or income from floods, this is
expected to be because of their greater wealth. It is these relative losses,
rather than the absolute figures, that have more significant implications for
livelihoods and well-being (Hallegatte and Rozenberg 2017). Urban poor
people exhibit lower coping abilities and slower recovery rates from climate
hazard impacts, underscoring the detrimental cycle between poverty and
disaster losses: Poverty significantly contributes to people’s vulnerability to
natural disasters, which subsequently exacerbates poverty in a measurable
and significant manner (Hallegatte et al. 2020).
However, urban flooding can cause significant disruption to cities’ amenities,
such as roads, schools, and access to health centers, which can have wider
impacts on the population, poor and nonpoor. Floods also cause damage to poor
individuals’ businesses or homes, an important loss considering their crucial
role in providing an income. Climate hazard events have the potential to inflict
damage on a city’s infrastructure and disrupt essential services to everyone.
Flooding can overwhelm unprepared water treatment facilities, leading to
contamination of water supplies. Interruptions to the transportation system,
including roads and public services, may isolate certain areas of a city from
critical services, exacerbating the impact of the floods.
Many damaging impacts of floods can be prevented by improving institutions
(such as having concerted and inclusive plans, improving clarity of land rights,
and so forth); raising awareness and providing education through the
dissemination of information, which can effectively protect individuals’ assets

Unveiling Harm’s Way

from the adverse effects of floods; differentiating investments that benefit every
resident from those focused on the most marginalized urban population and
short-term from long-term support; and thinking about incentives to avoid
trade-offs. Adaptation strategies strengthening resilience should not only
address poor individuals’ higher exposure but also consider institutional and
socioeconomic factors to effectively reduce vulnerability to climate hazards. It
will be necessary to leverage existing and often affordable technologies such as
community-tailored early warning systems and certain flood defenses (for
example, public spaces) before carefully choosing gray infrastructure. More
information is provided in chapters 3 and 4 of this report.

ONE IN FOUR POOR PEOPLE LIVE IN HIGH-VALUE
ECOSYSTEMS, MANY EXPOSED TO CLIMATE HAZARDS
As indicated in the previous section, LAC is a highly urbanized region.
However, although more people in urban areas live in poverty overall (62.8
percent of the total number of poor people as of 2023), rural areas face a higher
poverty rate—41 percent compared with 26 percent in urban areas.14 Natural
capital such as forests and land are vital for the livelihoods of poor individuals—
particularly in rural areas—so paying attention to their exposure is crucial
because these ecosystems are also highly vulnerable to climate hazards.
Agriculture is a key source of food and income for rural poor households in Latin
America—often one of the few assets they rely on, alongside household labor
and local resources. A study across nine countries found that the poorest
households depend almost entirely on agricultural income, whether from their
own farms or wage work, whereas wealthier households diversify away from
agriculture (Gáfaro et al. 2023). The livelihoods of poor people disproportionally
depend on the services and resources provided by ecosystems. In countries such
as Bolivia, Guatemala, Haiti, and Honduras, the income sources and food
security of 30–50 percent of the population depend on agriculture, which in turn
largely depends on the quality of the soil, forest, and water sources. Forests can
contribute to food security and provide fiber, fuel, and wood-based products.
LAC is home to a wide range of terrestrial natural landscapes and ecosystems,
including, but not limited to, the Amazon, the Mesoamerican Biological
Corridor, and the Gran Chaco and Pantanal regions (CAF 2023). Natural
landscapes play a crucial role in climate resilience, poverty reduction, and
agricultural productivity. Undisturbed, these landscapes mitigate natural
event impacts, such as erosion or coastal flooding (IPCC 2022). They buffer
against floods and storms, regulate temperature and rainfall, and mitigate
slower-onset crises such as droughts. Land and coastal-marine ecosystems,
for instance, provide crucial protection against hurricanes.
Poor people live in high-value ecosystem areas. A background paper for this
report (Turner, Lozano Gracia, and Hawthorne 2025) suggests that at least
43 million people living in poverty reside near highly valuable (within the top

|

23

24

| Adapting to Adversity

20 percent) ecosystems such as forests, representing 24 percent of the region’s
poor households.15 Other studies suggest that at least 92 million people living in
poverty reside near highly valuable local land ecosystems (refer to map 1.4)
(Bagolle, Costella, and Goyeneche 2023). Thus, climate hazard can exacerbate
mostly rural poverty if areas with high-value ecosystems are washed away. In
the five countries of the region with the highest biodiversity (Brazil, Colombia,
Ecuador, Mexico, and Peru), on average, 71.5 percent of poor people live in
areas of high-value ecosystems.
If vast areas of natural ecosystems or productive land in the region are prone to
climate hazards, their degradation poses a growing threat to impoverished
households in regions benefiting from coastal habitats such as mangroves and
coral reefs. Climate change, combined with deforestation, poor farming practices,
resource overuse, and pollution, is severely damaging ecosystems and natural
capital in LAC (IPCC 2022). Climate extremes, such as worsening droughts,
increase tree deaths, intensify fires, and reduce forest carbon absorption
(Turner et al. 2020). The depletion of environmental resources, particularly in
rural areas where communities heavily rely on ecosystems for their livelihoods,
disproportionately affects impoverished populations (IPCC 2022).

PEERING INTO THE FUTURE
The evidence on climate hazard exposure described so far is based on
historical data. In other words, the probability of events such as floods or
droughts happening in specific areas (as described, for instance, in box 1.1) is
determined by looking at patterns over past decades. These data already show
that large parts of the region are affected by one or several climate hazards,
and a substantial percentage of the population, poor and nonpoor, are in
harm’s way. This alone justifies policy action to reduce the risk of lives and
livelihoods being affected by these events. Climate change is likely to increase
these risks, so there is even more reason to invest in prevention and
protection policies now.
The high levels of exposure to climate hazards raises the question of how
climate change will alter the region’s exposure to them in the future.
As the IPCC (2022) has concluded, human beings are exposed to climate
change through changing weather patterns (for example, more rainfall or
more intense and frequent climate hazards) and indirectly through
changes to water, air, ecosystems, agriculture, and the economy. Weather
patterns can include climate-induced hazards and the slow-onset effects
of climate change (such as sea level rise, increasing temperatures, and
ocean acidification).
Climate change is already measurably affecting many parts of LAC.
From 1900 to 1980, average temperatures were pretty stable—around
24.2°C in Central America and 21.6°C in South America. The Caribbean
countries and Mexico saw a slight uptick in average temperature during
that time (refer to figure 1.5). From 1980 to 2020, however, things changed
fast. All four regions saw noticeable warming, with average temperatures
rising between 0.7°C (Central and South America) and up to 1.5°C
in Mexico.
The IPCC (2022) has concluded that climate change is making extreme
weather more frequent and intense in LAC, with rising temperatures,
heavier rainfall, and stronger hurricanes posing growing risks—especially
in coastal areas. There is some evidence, for instance, that the number of
Atlantic hurricanes has increased in recent years, with a larger proportion
of them being major cyclones (refer to figure 1.6). LAC has also faced a
surge of extreme climate hazard events recently—massive wildfires, severe
droughts, deadly floods, and powerful hurricanes. From record fires in
Brazil’s Pantanal and historic drought in the Amazon to hurricanes Beryl
and Otis battering the Caribbean and Mexico, the region has been hit hard.
Other impacts include blackouts and shipping delays. Although diverse,
these events share a common driver: rising temperatures fueled by
climate change.

27

By 2050, regional temperatures could rise by 1.5–2.5°C, with some areas seeing
up to 20 percent more heavy rainfall, increasing the threat of floods. Global
temperatures have already climbed about 1.4°C since preindustrial times (that
is, 1850–1900), mostly because of greenhouse gas emissions from fossil fuels,
and they are projected to rise 2–3°C by the century’s end. The record-breaking
heat of 2024, fueled in part by a strong El Niño, exemplifies how warming
trends are amplifying extreme events such as heat waves, droughts, wildfires,
and major hurricanes, many of which are now being linked to climate change
through attribution studies (refer to box 1.4).
Without strong adaptation strategies, climate risks will continue to escalate. For
example, the frequency of droughts in the region could increase by up to
25 percent by 2100, particularly in areas such as Central America and the
Amazon. These changes are expected to have a big impact on agriculture, water
resources, and ecosystems, leading to economic losses and tough challenges for
local communities, including further rural-urban migration. The IPCC (2022)
stresses the urgent need for comprehensive climate strategies to safeguard the
region’s communities and ecosystems.

Box 1.4

Summary of attribution studies
Attribution studiesa is a relatively new field of
scientific research that measures whether and by
how much climate change influences the intensity,
frequency, or impact of extreme climate hazards
such as droughts, floods, or hurricanes, either by
increasing rainfall and winds in some cases or by
causing temperatures to soar and drought to
develop in others. They then examine the
consequences for people and property and the
nature of the changes in those events.
There are several ways to carry out an attribution
study, but the most widely accepted method in
the past two decades involves simulating extreme
events in today’s climate and comparing them
with a model of that event in a hypothetical world
without human-caused warming. The differences
show how rising emissions have affected the

event’s likelihood or severity. By comparing realworld data with this counterfactual “no-warming”
scenario, scientists can determine whether global
warming made the event more likely (Otto 2017).
As of November 2024, researchers had analyzed
32 extreme weather events in LAC from heat
waves in Central America and Mexico, to drought
in Brazil and the South Amazon, to heavy rainfall
in Colombia and Peru and Hurricane Maria in
Puerto Rico. They found that human-caused
climate change was a significant driver in 23 of
these events (refer to map B1.4.1) (McSweeney
and Tandon 2024). For example, a 2022 study
found that climate change made the deadly heat
wave in Argentina and Paraguay 60 times more
likely (Rivera et al. 2022).

Unveiling Harm’s Way

Box 1.4 Summary of attribution studies (continued)

The connection between an increase in the
frequency and intensity of severe climate
events and climate change has been established
in multiple scientific studies, but more
scientific work is needed to establish the
impact of climate change on other variables,
such as health or economic costs of extreme
events (Otto 2023).
In Latin America, some specific studies have
researched the connection between climate

change, hurricanes, and public debt (Cavallo
et al. 2025), between extreme events and human
well-being (Nagy et al. 2018), or between climate
hazards and poverty (Rodríguez-Oreggia et al.
2013). In the introduction to a special issue with
several studies on climate change and disasters
in LAC, Cavallo, Hoffman, and Noy (2023)
summarized a series of studies that document
the negative impact of climate events on
economic growth, trade, investment, fiscal
accounts, and educational attainment.

a. In 2004, researchers studied how much human-caused climate change contributed to Europe’s deadly 2003 heat wave, which killed
more than 70,000 people. They found that human activity had at least doubled the risk of such extreme heat. The findings grabbed
­global headlines and launched the field of extreme event attribution.
Source: https://www.worldweatherattribution.org/.

NOTES
1. The sixth event of importance, forest fires, is more difficult to measure with the
methodology used for this report—which is explained in following paragraphs—so it
is not included in this study. Moreover, it represents a minor percentage of the
events occurring in the region.
2. Although recent improvements in measurement and reporting may contribute to an
upward bias, the overall upward trend in the number of events is evident.
3. This approach centers on identifying who is directly in the path of such events,
rather than considering indirect effects such as economic disruptions, price
fluctuations because of crop damage, or impacts on infrastructure. This method also
does not account for the cascading effects that often follow disasters, such as mass
migrations or disease outbreaks.
4. This study uses maps at administrative level two, referring to county, district, or
municipality, rather than at the departmental or province level. These level two
maps are, however, only for a subset of eight countries for which official poverty
maps exist. For the rest of the countries in the region, we use either provincial or
even national population data and hazard maps.
5. Poverty rate estimates and trends are from the World Bank’s Poverty and Inequality
Platform (https://pip.worldbank.org/).
6. For trends in the Gini coefficient over time for countries in the region, see the
World Bank’s Poverty and Inequality Platform (https://pip.worldbank.org/home).
7. Refer to box 1.3 for other innovative methods to explore the connection between
exposure to climate hazards like floods and poverty.

29

30

| Adapting to Adversity

8. Hallegatte et al. (2016) published an early study with measures of differential
exposure rates between poor and nonpoor individuals for floods and droughts only
at the national level. A more recent global study (Hill et al. 2025) compares
exposure rates across regions and finds that about 33.9 percent of the LAC
population is exposed to any of four climate hazards (hurricanes, droughts, floods,
and heat waves). This is below the exposure rates for South Asia and East Asia and
Pacific (87.0 and 69.1 percent, respectively), but similar to exposure rates in SubSaharan Africa (38.2 percent), the Middle East and North Africa (34.5 percent), and
North America (31.1 percent). The lowest exposure rate is in Europe and Central
Asia (27.1 percent).
9. Moreover, these estimates include data from a mixture of poverty maps with
different territorial detail. Some countries have data at the municipal or county
level, whereas for others data are at the provincial or even national level. This
implies that estimates may be less precise because vast tracts of territory are
assigned a single exposure-plus-poverty rate. Analysis using data for Mexico and
Peru at varying levels of geographic granularity (that is, municipality, district,
province, and national) reveals that exposure-plus-poverty rates tend to be higher
when assessed at smaller geographic units. One exception was observed for Peru,
where heat-wave-related rates were similar—and slightly higher—at the
department level compared with districts. Overall, however, coarser administrative
units tend to underestimate exposure-plus-poverty rates, highlighting the value of
more granular analysis. Importantly, the purpose of these maps is not only to
quantify the number of poor and nonpoor people exposed to hazards but also to
pinpoint their exact locations. The finer the geographic resolution, the more
accurately risks can be identified and policy responses targeted. For a discussion,
see the background study for this report by Brunckhorst et al. (2026).
The methodology is based on a global study by Doan et al. (2023). Hallegatte et al.
(2017) describe an early application of this approach to floods in Viet Nam.
10. This part of the report draws data and text from a background study by D’Aoust, de
la Fuente, and Lozano Gracia (2025) that was commissioned for this report.
11. The cities analyzed are Barranquilla, Bogotá, Cali, Cartagena, and Medellín.
12. The definition of flood hazard here relies on Fathom 3, where high hazard is
defined as more than 50 centimeters, which can disrupt livelihoods and economic
activity and cause some risk to life for select populations, especially vulnerable
groups such as children and disabled people. This data source, however, may not
capture differences in drainage infrastructure (and maintenance) over the urban
territory, which may lead to overestimating pluvial flood hazard in rich areas and
underestimating it in poor areas.
13. These maps combine flood hazard and vulnerability rather than poverty.
Vulnerability and poverty are different concepts, and, in this regard, these maps are
not fully comparable with those in the preceding section. However, they illustrate a
similar message and aim to answer the same question: whether the poor (or
vulnerable) population is more or less exposed than the rest of the population.
A more extensive treatment of the concept of vulnerability and its connection to
climate hazards is developed in chapter 2.
14. Urban-rural distribution of the total poor individuals is from World Bank (2024b).
Urban versus rural poverty rates are from ECLAC (2023).
15. If one considers an expansive definition of high ecosystem service valuation (any
value in the top 80 percent), up to 17 percent of the total population and 79 percent
of the poor population could be considered to live in an area with at least some
ecosystem service valuation. See Turner, Lozano Gracia, and Hawthorne (2025).

2

Risk of Climate Hazards
ASSESSING VULNERABILITY AND
WELFARE LOSSES

INTRODUCTION
Climate hazards affect households, businesses, and entire countries in many
ways. This report focuses on the impact on individuals and households, looking
at flows such as income and consumption poverty. It also considers
nonmonetary assets, such as health, nutrition, and education, because they have
long-term effects on well-being.
As mentioned in chapter 1, climate hazard risk comes from a mix of hazards,
exposure, and vulnerability. That chapter highlighted the main climate hazards
in the region and who is exposed to them. This chapter looks at how vulnerable
people in Latin America and the Caribbean (LAC) are to these hazards,
especially in terms of poverty. We measure vulnerability by estimating how
many people could fall into poverty (or become poorer) because of climate
hazards. We also report vulnerability as those people being highly exposed to
climate hazards and without coping mechanisms such as financial products or
social protection. Last, we explore the real impacts on well-being of climate risk
once it materializes, using historical data to explore both immediate costs
(on flows) and long-term effects (on stocks).

HOW MANY PEOPLE IN LAC ARE VULNERABLE TO CLIMATE
HAZARDS, AND WHO ARE THEY?
Several approaches are used to identify and quantify vulnerability to climate
hazards. The main approach used in this report is a method that consists of
estimating the probability and size of a loss in income because of materialized
climate hazards. Those with a loss larger than a given threshold are defined as
vulnerable.
Climate hazards may push near-poor people into poverty and poor people into
deeper poverty, but to what extent and how deep? A background paper for this
report (de la Fuente and Serio 2025), using household survey data and climate
35

36

| Adapting to Adversity

hazard data collected for the past 20 years, offers the first comprehensive
estimates of LAC populations who are vulnerable to poverty (that is, their
estimated expected per capita income is lower than the poverty line of -$6.85
per day in 2017 purchasing power parity) or deeper impoverishment resulting
from climate hazards.
This background paper (de la Fuente and Serio 2025) states that a household’s
vulnerability to poverty depends not only on its expected (mean) income but
also on the volatility (variance) of its income stream. Households that are not
poor today can slip into poverty if they are hit by major shocks, and families
already living in poverty may end up even worse off. These shocks—such as job
loss, economic crises, or climate shocks—create income ups and downs that can
push nonpoor households below the poverty line or trap poor households
deeper in hardship. It is thus not enough to look only at a household’s average
income; the stability of that income also needs to be considered. de la Fuente
and Serio (2025) look at two types of vulnerability: (1) poverty-induced (PI)
vulnerability refers to households that are already poor because their expected
income falls below the poverty line and (2) risk-induced (RI) vulnerability refers
to households with income above the poverty line but still at risk of falling into
poverty because of income volatility.
Expected income is estimated from household factors such as demographics
and socioeconomics, but measuring income volatility is trickier. One way to
measure it is through Chaudhuri’s (2003) method, which treats disturbances or
residuals that cannot be predicted in the income process as shocks to income.
The variability of the disturbance can serve as an estimate of income variance.
This estimate is not random; it depends on household traits and community
factors, such as exposure to climate hazards or other community characteristics
not related to climate. Consequently, de la Fuente and Serio’s (2025) study
breaks down RI vulnerability into three groups: (1) climate risk induced (CRI),
(2) nonclimate risk induced (NCRI), and (3) idiosyncratic risk induced (IRI).
Most people are at risk of falling into poverty because of structural issues such
as the activity they perform, their level of education, or personal setbacks rather
than because they are affected by climate hazards. In total, about 14.5 million
people in the region are vulnerable to climate-induced poverty.
Looking only at income disturbances misses bigger structural issues. It assumes
household characteristics stay stable during shocks, which is not always true—
large aggregate shocks can change income dynamics entirely. A broader way to
capture risk is to look at the spread of total income, not just its residuals when
predicted. By estimating overall income variance—across personal, nonclimate,
and climate factors—one gets a fuller picture of vulnerability. With this
approach, the share of people in LAC vulnerable to climate-induced poverty
jumps from 14.5 million (when looking only at disturbances) to nearly
59.8 million under this fuller method (refer to figure 2.1).1 Among the countries
studied, Brazil, Colombia, Mexico, and Peru have the largest numbers of people
vulnerable to climate-driven poverty, and Chile and Uruguay have the lesser.

Note: The indicators correspond to the latest available household survey year for each country. Estimations are derived from the
model that considers the variability of the disturbances and structural issues in the income process. A single person may
simultaneously face multiple types of risks and thus may be classified concurrently experiencing IRI, CRI, or NCRI vulnerability.
CRI = climate risk induced; IRI = idiosyncratic risk induced; NCRI = nonclimate risk induced; PI = poverty induced.

Like poor households, households with CRI vulnerability tend to be
younger, with many heads of households in their prime working years.
Unlike poor households, however, households with CRI vulnerability show
higher employment rates, have fewer unemployed members, and are more
likely to hold salaried jobs. Economically, they cluster in income deciles
three and four, whereas poor households are mostly in deciles one and two.
They are also more urban based and less likely to live in low-quality housing
than poor households (refer to figure 2.2).
The number of people vulnerable to poverty because of climate risk might not
seem large in a region of 667 million (as of 2025). As we show later, though,
many more are affected in other ways, especially through nonmonetary impacts
such as health and long-term well-being. And yet, climate hazards can actually
hit harder than many other risks. Figure 2.3 shows that it takes many more
resources per person to recover from climate hazards that materialize and
reach the poverty line than from individual-level risks, underscoring how hard
these events hit household welfare.
Climate hazards may push many nonpoor people into poverty but also affect
many who are already poor. This is problematic because it creates a poverty
trap. But are poor and rich people equally affected? By definition, poor
people have lower endowments and are therefore more vulnerable to
climate hazards. This is true in at least three ways.

Risk of Climate Hazards

First, poor people have lower physical and human capital endowments to
protect themselves. Climate hazards often affect entire communities, making
coinsurance less effective, which, combined with lower physical and human
capital endowments characteristic of poor households, make them badly
situated to handle risk-related losses.
Second, poor people tend to settle in hazard-prone areas, where they can afford
to live as a result of the narrow prospects they face. Poor rural and urban
households are typically pushed to steep land or squatter settlements where
land ownership is easier and rent is cheaper.
Third, poverty can lead to practices that magnify exposure to climate hazards—
for instance, the use of cheap but poor-quality housing materials and engaging
in production activities that are typically unsafe or less resilient to climate
change, such as rain-fed agriculture.
Vulnerability is not just about income volatility and susceptibility to losses
because of a climate hazard—it is also about how hard it is to bounce back
afterward. Even though households try different ways to cope with climate
shocks, these are often not enough to keep their consumption steady. Skoufias
and Vinha (2013) found that rural households in Mexico could not fully deal
with the impact of rainfall and temperature changes—food and nonfood
spending both took a hit. After Tropical Storm Agatha hit Guatemala in 2010,
Baez et al. (2017) showed that families cut food spending by 10 percent, which
meant more than 100 fewer calories per person per day. To cope, adult men
worked extra hours a week, and child labor jumped by 11 percent.
Another approach understands the vulnerability of by identifying people who
are both highly exposed to climate hazards and vulnerable because they lack
key assets or services to cope with them (Doan et al. 2023). Following such an
approach, the study gauges these dimensions of vulnerability by population
percentages of access to safe water, electricity, or an all-season road to markets
(as measures of physical vulnerability) and by rates of poverty, social security
coverage, basic education, or financial inclusion (for measures of vulnerability
as inability to cope). As of circa 2021, about 15.6 percent of the population in
LAC was exposed and vulnerable to climate risks. Among those exposed,
2.2 percent were vulnerable because of low income, 3.7 percent were
vulnerable because of low education, 6.3 percent lacked financial access, and
7.1 percent had weak social protection. Fewer faced infrastructure gaps—such
as the 1.7 percent without electricity, 1.2 percent without safe water, and only
0.3 percent with no access to all-season road markets. Overall, 4.7 percent
faced two vulnerability factors, and 1.4 percent faced three or more. In LAC,
vulnerability is mostly driven by limited financial access and inadequate social
protection coverage, more so than basic infrastructure gaps. Rates are lower
than in South Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa, similar to the Middle East and
North Africa but higher than in East Asia and Pacific and Eastern Europe and
Central Asia.2

|

39

40

| Adapting to Adversity

Those who are vulnerable—especially poor individuals and women—should
always be a priority when tackling climate risks. By some definitions, they make
up a sizable group of the population in some areas, or they face sharp declines
in welfare, which can trap them in poverty. They are often more exposed to
risks on the basis of where they live or the work they do, and they usually have
fewer resources to protect themselves or recover after a climate hazard
materializes. As shown in the next section, climate hazards hit these groups
hardest, affecting everything from income and consumption to health,
nutrition, and education—affecting not just money but long-term well-being.

CLIMATE HAZARDS RISK: THE IMMEDIATE IMPACT ON
INCOMES, ASSETS, AND POVERTY
The direst and most immediate consequence of many climate hazards, once
materialized, is death or severe health or asset losses. These effects are
discussed in the following subsection and can be considered permanent or
long-term impacts. Temporary malnourishment leaves lasting effects on
children through stunted growth and lower mental abilities. Hurricanes and
floods also can have persistent effects on poorly endowed households: For
them, assets used for coping or lost can lead to persistent poverty.
However, for many survivors, climate hazards pose challenges that—given
adequate measures—can be solved over time but represent important short-term
losses in their living standards. These challenges manifest through disruptions to
socioeconomic activities and damage to public infrastructure, natural resources,
and productive assets. Urban areas experience harm to housing, often a vital
source of livelihood. Infrastructure (water, sanitation, electricity) and social
facilities (schools, health centers) can also get damaged. In rural areas, climate
hazards result in crops and livestock losses, as well as housing and infrastructure
destruction, mirroring the challenges faced in urban settings.
When the focus is shifted from individual victims to larger groups, the overall
impact depends on how the shock affects nonvictims, too. Nonaffected areas
might step in to produce more, benefiting from higher prices, or damage might
not be permanent (such as roads reopening after flooding), allowing for recovery.
However, if the affected area produces unique inputs or consumes goods with no
substitutes, output could drop in both the affected and the unaffected areas.
These are the indirect effects of climate hazards that can still ripple out and affect
people. Some effects (such as lives or buildings lost) are on stocks, whereas others
affect flows (such as income, consumption, gross domestic product [GDP], and
investment) as a result of disruption, even if assets were not directly damaged.

Climate hazards increase consumption variability and poverty
The inability to generate income because agricultural production drops or
jobs are lost or to smooth consumption after climate hazards can push people

Risk of Climate Hazards

|

41

into poverty. Assets play a crucial role in generating income, enabling households
to access goods and services. The loss of or disrupted access to incomegenerating assets because of climate hazards can significantly reduce income.
Box 2.1 highlights the different ways in which people, especially those in
vulnerable jobs or sectors, can see their income drop when hit by climate
hazards. Whether it is the kind of work they do or the activities they rely on,
these factors make them more exposed to the short-term impacts of climate
hazards once materialized.

Box 2.1

Channels through which climate hazards can affect incomes in the short term
The asset-based framework proposed by LópezCalva and Rodríguez-Castelán (2016) explains the
main elements that contribute to household
income generation and offers a way to identify the
channels through which climate hazards can
influence household living standards. As shown
in figure B2.1.1, household market income is
derived from the accumulation of productive
assets, their intensity of use, and the returns they
generate, along with net transfers (that is,
transfers received minus taxes paid). Each of
these components is vulnerable to external
shocks, with climate hazards playing a
particularly significant role.
Agriculture is one of the sectors most affected by
climate hazards because it depends so much on

weather and natural resources. Climate shocks
affect the return to assets by damaging crops and
reducing earnings. Smallholder farmers are
especially vulnerable—losing crops or livestock
can wipe out their income. After Hurricane
Mitch, for instance, a third of households saw
crop-related income losses, with total crop value
dropping 25–40 percent (Morris et al. 2002).
In Costa Rica, Guatemala, and Honduras,
87 percent of maize farmers and 66 percent of
coffee farmers said climate change was hurting
their yields (Harvey et al. 2018). And it is only
getting worse: Seo and Mendelsohn (2007)
project that farm earnings in Latin America could
fall by as much as 62 percent by 2100 under severe
climate scenarios.

42

Box 2.1 Channels through which climate hazards can affect incomes in the short term (continued)

Climate hazards affect the intensity of land asset
use in agriculture. Because the climate is so
unpredictable and the risks are high, many
farming households play it safe—often choosing
low-risk, low-return options. Eakin (2000) found
that smallholder maize farmers in Mexico knew
about better seed varieties for drought or frost,
but many stuck with familiar, lower-yield crops to
ensure food for their families and livestock.
Similarly, Tucker, Eakin, and Castellanos (2010)
saw that coffee farmers in Guatemala, Honduras,
and Mexico recognized the climate risks but were
hesitant to adapt, often viewing extreme weather
as just part of normal climate ups and downs.
Climate hazards also affect the use of human
resources. Acevedo (2016) found that floods in
Colombia’s Caribbean region (2001–10) resulted in
work hours and income being cut, with women
facing higher unemployment than men.
Households eventually recovered after about
13 months, but often by sending kids to work.
Similar effects were seen with storms and
landslides in Mexico (Acevedo et al. 2023) and
excessive rain in Colombia (Bohórquez-Penuela
and Otero-Cortes 2020). In Jamaica, Pecha Garzón
(2017) found that hurricanes raised the chances of
staying in informal work by 8.5 percent and pushed

14.5 percent of formal workers into informal work,
largely because of impacts on the tourism sector.
Climate hazards also take a toll on health and labor
productivity, which involves a deterioration in the
accumulation of productive assets such as human
capital. In El Salvador, agricultural workers on
lowland sugarcane and cotton plantations suffer
from heat stress, which is linked to declining
kidney function (Peraza et al. 2012). In 78 big cities
across Latin America, long dry spells lead to lower
employment, wages, work hours, and incomes—
especially among informal workers (Desbureaux
and Rodella 2019). Desbureaux and Rodella (2019)
suggest that the potential pathways come from a
decrease in productivity likely tied to more power
outages during droughts and worsening health
conditions.
Mueller and Osgood (2009) show that droughts in
Brazil reduce rural wages, with agricultural
dependence as a key factor determining the
severity of the impact. Arceo-Gómez et al. (2020)
further investigated the welfare impacts caused
by droughts in Mexico. Rural households that
experienced a drought saw their earnings
reduced by almost 6 percent, translating into a
5 percentage point increase in the probability of
falling into poverty.

Tightened budgets may in turn lead to reduced consumption and push
individuals into poverty.3 In Central America, Ishizawa and Miranda (2019)
document that more intense hurricanes increase moderate and extreme
poverty by 1–2 percentage points, with rural areas affected twice as much as
urban areas. In Guatemala, Baez et al. (2017) find that urban areas bore the
brunt of the burden from 2010’s Tropical Storm Agatha, with consumption per
capita dropping by 12.6 percent and poverty increasing by 5.5 percentage points.
Evidence from Mexico reinforces the effects of climate hazards on poverty.
Rodriguez-Oreggia et al. (2013) reveal that municipalities in Mexico affected by
climate hazards between 2000 and 2005 experienced a loss of two years of
human development and an increase in food poverty by 3.7 percent, capacities

Risk of Climate Hazards

poverty by 3.0 percent, and assets poverty by 1.5 percent. A comparative study
on mobility in 15 countries in Africa, Latin America, and South and East Asia
found that climate shocks and lack of social protection were the second most
important reason why people became poor, only after health shocks and death
(Narayan, Pritchett, and Kapoor 2009).

Climate hazard losses disproportionately affect the poorest people
It is not just that climate hazards push people into poverty—poorer households
get hit hardest. In Argentina, Gonzalez, Santos, and London (2022) found that
frequent, widespread climate shocks from 1970 to 2010 made poverty worse,
especially in areas that were already struggling. Floods and landslides were
major culprits. The same is true in Brazil: After a 2008 flash flood in Santa
Catarina, Wink et al. (2023) saw a spike in extreme poverty, hitting non-White,
rural, and less-educated groups the hardest. In Mexico, Rodriguez-Oreggia
et al. (2013) showed that climate hazards hit municipalities with lower human
development the most between 2000 and 2005.
When Hurricane Mitch hit Honduras in 1998, it laid bare some harsh inequalities.
The poorest farming families lost 18 percent of their assets, whereas the richest
lost just 3 percent (Morris et al. 2002). Although wealthier households bounced
back within three years, poorer households faced long-lasting setbacks (Carter
et al. 2007). Similar patterns showed up in Nicaragua, too; Premand and Vakis
(2010) found that weather shocks and the coffee price crash kept the poorest
people stuck at the bottom, making it even harder to move up.
Cruz, de la Fuente, and Escobedo (2018) constructed flood risk scores (the
probability of monetary losses from floods or hurricanes in low-income
housing) for all municipalities in Mexico. These were then spatially correlated
with the poverty incidence corresponding to each municipality in 2005. They
found that poor families in Mexico are likely to bear higher losses in housing
from flood hazards. The study also found that the poorest municipalities have
fewer federal hospitals, bridges, and roads (in value and number), which are
critical facilities to attend the population after floods or other similar events
strike. Fortunately, the risk to housing floods has decreased substantially in
recent years, not just in Mexico, but also in other countries in the region, mainly
because of improvements in the construction materials used for housing
(Soriano et al. 2024).
Some studies help explain why climate hazards hit poor individuals so
differently. After Hurricane Mitch, Van den Berg and Burger (2008) found that
poor households in Nicaragua did not seek outside help; they just cut back on
consumption, whereas wealthier households managed to maintain their
spending. In El Salvador, it was the opposite: Poor households kept up
consumption but had to sell off assets to do it (Rodríguez-Meza and GonzálezVega 2004). And it gets worse over time. Van den Berg and Burger (2008) found
that Nicaraguan coffee farmers who sold assets early on after Hurricane Mitch

|

43

44

| Adapting to Adversity

or a drought were more likely to resort to even harsher coping strategies later,
such as selling land or future harvests.
Additionally, a study in Jamaica (Henry, Spencer, and Strobl 2020) further
suggests that poorer households tend to reside in more hurricane-prone areas,
and only those living in buildings with less wind-resistant walls are significantly
affected. The poor-quality housing materials forced affected households to
reallocate budgets away from other essential spending, likely to afford the
increased expenditure on building repairs.
More recently, a study using new household survey data from five Caribbean
countries (Belize, Dominica, Haiti, St. Lucia, and Suriname) has offered further
insight into household-level preparedness and capacity to cope with climate
shocks (Anglade et al. 2024). The analysis shows a clear income gradient in
disaster preparedness, with wealthier households more likely to have access to
emergency supplies and poorer ones remaining significantly less prepared.
Education also plays a role: Households with more educated heads are more
likely to discuss risk mitigation strategies and better cope with shocks.
Conversely, those in rural areas or with children are generally less able to
handle shocks.
Furthermore, experiencing credit constraints had impacts similar to having few
assets because these households could not easily smooth consumption. Along
the same line, Carter et al. (2007) suggest that access to robust labor and capital
markets could have offset the entire loss for lower-wealth households.
As argued by Alpízar (2007), access to financial services increased the
efficiency of households’ production and reduced the negative impacts of
droughts and earthquakes for farmers in El Salvador. Indeed, households with
outstanding debts to informal sources experienced a sharp decline in efficiency,
indicating that past shocks may have lasting negative effects on efficiency.
Climate shocks do not hit just those who are poor—they hit other groups hard,
too, especially women. Fruttero et al. (2023) review how weather shocks often
have gendered impacts. For example, in Bolivia, female-headed households
were hit hardest by climate hazards, with spikes in poverty and food insecurity.
Women’s employment dropped by 2.59 percent, compared with just
0.66 percent for men (Escalante and Maisonnave 2022). In Chile, Pérez-Silva,
Castillo, and Cazzuffi (2023) found that droughts from 1990 to 2017 hit
Indigenous women the hardest, pushing them out of agriculture and into
unpaid family work—or out of the workforce entirely.
Gender norms and disparities in access to capital profoundly affect women’s
resilience against shocks. In Nicaragua, Segnestam (2017) found that women
facing drought relied more on short-term coping, contrasting with men’s
preference for long-term adaptation. Bradshaw (2010) notes women’s
dependence on male approval before evacuating homes during floods because
of their limited decision-making power. Additionally, shocks heighten women’s
vulnerability to intimate partner violence. Díaz and Saldarriaga (2023) show

Risk of Climate Hazards

that in the Peruvian Andes, droughts increase women’s risk of physical intimate
partner violence by 65 percent. This stems from economic insecurity affecting
men’s emotional well-being and reduced female empowerment hampering
negotiation ability.
In sum, climate hazards bring short-term setbacks for many, even if they
can recover over time with the right support. These events disrupt
everyday life and damage crucial resources such as public infrastructure,
natural assets, and livelihoods. In urban areas, homes—which are often tied to
income—can be seriously damaged, along with basic services such as water,
electricity, and schools. Rural communities face similar issues, plus the added
blow of losing crops, livestock, and income from farming. When people
cannot earn income—because jobs vanish or crops fail—or cannot smooth out
their consumption after a shock, they risk falling into poverty. For those who
are already poor, income drops fast when climate events wipe out or cut off
access to these assets, pushing vulnerable households further behind.
Of course, both poor and vulnerable people, even if they recover, are obviously
worse off for having experienced the climate shock. Although many indeed
recover, others pushed over the edge may suffer permanently.

CLIMATE HAZARDS RISK: LONG-TERM CONSEQUENCES
Climate hazards, when they occur, leave a trail of death and destruction. As the
immediate destruction subsides, both the landscape and the people undergo a
recovery process. However, hazards sometimes impose lasting disadvantages
with long-term consequences. Although the enduring effects of climate hazards
may be less apparent, this section explores whether there is evidence of such
long-term impacts in the context of LAC.
Short-term drops in consumption can last a few weeks or months. If immediate
remedial action is taken by governments, households and communities (selfinsurance and activation of risk-pooling mechanisms), or both, consumption
drops, or income losses can be smoothed and overturned. Public health
interventions can stabilize morbidity surges. Likewise, poor nutritional status
may be reversed if the specifically affected groups receive feeding support.
Welfare impacts from climate hazards and climate variability can persist,
however. If households have few assets to buffer the impact of a climate hazard
and public protective measures come too late or do not come at all, the
transitory impacts of a shock can turn permanent, particularly for highly
vulnerable groups, such as children.
In short, damage and loss estimates measure material things, but all losses, not
just physical, should be considered, and the literature particularly refers to
losses in human (health, nutrition, and education) and natural capital
(biodiversity and the like). For some people, especially those near subsistence
consumption, the inability to smooth consumption requires cutting back on

|

45

46

| Adapting to Adversity

health care, nutrition, and education, and this can lead to persistent negative
effects on human capital.

Deaths and distress from climate hazards
LAC is the second-most-disaster-prone region in the world, behind only Asia and
the Pacific, with more than 900 climate hazards killing more than 30,000 people
and affecting more than 188 million people since 2000.
The 2019 Global Climate Risk Index ranked the region the world’s second most
affected by climate hazards over 1998–2017,4 with hurricanes and tropical storms
recurrently causing high human and economic losses. Its average Global Climate
Risk Index score of 71.8 reflects high vulnerability, worse than all regions except
South Asia (with a score of 66.6).5 Floods together with storms alone affected
115 million people between 2000 and 2024. There is a clear need to study the
most effective ways to reduce the costs of tropical storms, floods, droughts, and
other climate hazards, which especially affect poorest households.
While a more visible and direct impact of climate change is more and stronger
storms and floods, which can cause death, fewer people die from climate
hazards. According to the Center for Research on the Epidemiology of Disasters
Emergency Events Database, known as EM-DAT, climate disasters claimed the
lives of more than 120,037 people in LAC between 1970 and 2024. However,
figure 2.4a shows a clear drop in deaths over the past 20 years, although the
trend has leveled off lately. Of course, some hazards can cause early deaths over
time through indirect effects that EM-DAT does not capture—for example,
droughts worsening air pollution and health (refer to box 2.2).
The absolute number of affected populations (those injured, homeless, and in
need of immediate assistance) has been increasing historically, mainly because
of the impact of floods and storms, although this trend may have changed in the
past decade (refer to figure 2.4b). The historical rise could be attributed to
improved database coverage or increased reporting of climate hazards, but it
may also indicate a real increase in exposure and vulnerability to these hazards.
The surge in damage is likely influenced by the expanded exposure resulting
from urbanization. Notably, middle-income countries are especially susceptible
to these trends.
Analysis conducted for this report on the impacts of major floods in LAC at the
city level between 2000 and 2023 also confirms that cities across the income
spectrum have made progress in reducing mortality from floods (refer to
figure 3.5 in chapter 3). However, this optimism is moderated by challenges in
low-income countries, where flood impacts remain substantially higher
(although they are gradually decreasing). Exploiting variations in city-level
income, urban areas with a higher per capita GDP in 2015 were less likely to
experience more deaths when a flood occurred (D’Aoust, de la Fuente, and
Lozano Gracia 2025).

Box 2.2

The hidden mortality costs of droughts in LAC: Pollution from power swaps
A background paper for this report by Eriksson, del
Valle, and de la Fuente (2025) shows that droughts
in LAC do not just dry up rivers, they also dirty the
air. Why? Because when hydropower (which
supplies about half the region’s electricity) runs low
during droughts, countries fall back on dirtier
energy sources, such as fossil fuels and biomass.
That shift boosts harmful air pollution, specifically
fine particulate matter (PM2.5), around power
plants. On average, PM2.5 levels rise by 0.83
micrograms per cubic meter near combustion
plants during droughts. That is a lot when 443
million people live close enough to breathe that air.
The result is up to 10,000 premature deaths per year
and a staggering -$150 billion in welfare losses
between 2000 and 2020. Unsurprisingly,

low-income communities living near these plants
are hit the hardest.
Looking ahead, even if countries stick to climate
targets such as those in the Paris Agreement, the
combination of drought-related pollution could keep
harming people through mid-century unless action
is taken. What is needed? An integrated approach to
climate adaptation, energy security, and public
health with investments in energy storage, betterconnected power grids, stricter emissions rules,
phasing out of polluting plants near cities, and
making sure to incorporate environmental justice
considerations as part of the energy transition. As
droughts worsen with climate change, tackling this
issue is more urgent than ever.

48

| Adapting to Adversity

Both wealthy and impoverished nations in LAC face climate hazards, yet the
majority of deaths over the past five decades have occurred in low-income
countries (refer to figure 2.5). Regional benchmarks indicate that LAC only
performs worse than the Middle East and North Africa, Afghanistan, and
Pakistan. Yet, countries such as Haiti and Honduras have higher death and
exposure rates than even the regional averages for South Asia and Sub-Saharan
Africa. The impacts of climate hazards seems to be concentrated in poorer
countries in LAC. Table 2.1 classifies countries on the basis of climate risk
impact captured by the Global Climate Risk Index for 2000–2019 and
vulnerability to poverty (refer to the “Climate hazards increase consumption
variability and poverty” section of this chapter) using mean thresholds. Richer
countries such as Chile, Panama, and Uruguay have lower rates of vulnerability
to climate-induced poverty and climate impacts. Lower-middle-income
countries such as El Salvador and Honduras fall in the opposite spectrum
(refer to table 2.1). Clearly, getting richer favors adaptation. Disparities among
countries highlight varying levels of adaptation capabilities against climate
hazards.

Geography or history do not determine fate. Although poorer countries fare
worse on average, being economically disadvantaged does not always condemn
a country to inevitable consequences. Despite facing similar paths and
intensities from hurricanes, some poor and lower-middle-income countries in
the region have successfully decreased mortality rates and enhanced resilience.
Notably, Hurricane Mitch in 1998 (which caused at least 11,000 deaths) and
Hurricanes Eta and Iota in 2020 (which caused between 200 and 300 deaths)
affected Central America similarly, yet the death toll from the latter was
significantly reduced. The stark contrast in the death toll between Hurricanes
Janet (1955) and Gilbert (2005) in the Caribbean and the Gulf of Mexico—both
category five storms following similar paths—emphasizes that even
economically challenged nations can reduce fatalities. The implementation of
early warning systems plays a crucial role in mitigating deaths caused by storms
and floods. More generally, many countries in harm’s way have managed to
adapt and protect their population over time, and chapter 3 of this report
examines how this has happened.
Despite the recent decline in the number of affected people, the continued risk
should not be lightly dismissed. Complacency might arise from keeping
mortality under control while ignoring climate hazards. Even if they recover,
victims are worse off for having experienced the hazard. And although many
survivors recover, a disaster pushes some over the edge, and they suffer
permanently. Children might not return to school. Even temporary
malnourishment can have lasting effects on children because of stunted growth
and lower mental abilities. A review of the effect of natural hazards on human
capital conducted for this report finds long-lasting effects of climate hazards, as
discussed next.

Declines in health, nutrition, and schooling
The effects on human capital are particularly worrying because human capital
accumulates across the life cycle. Any interruption in cognitive abilities and
nutrition at early stages may cascade into lasting effects on various aspects of
well-being later in life, such as schooling and productivity. Climate hazards
therefore represent a great threat to development because of the magnitude and

50

| Adapting to Adversity

irreversibility of some of their impacts and their intergenerational dimension.
Here, we present selected impacts of climate hazards on human capital
outcomes identified through a literature review conducted by the team.
Climate change affects human capital development across the life cycle
unevenly: During critical stages, such as early childhood, its effects on health
and productivity can be severe, hindering future returns on early investments
(Carneiro and Heckman 2003). School-age children face disrupted education
because of rising temperatures and extreme weather events, leading to longterm social and individual repercussions. Youth and adults experience job
disruptions, reduced earnings, and climate-related health issues, altering labor
decisions and affecting human capital accumulation. Older adults facing
physical, psychological, and socioeconomic challenges are among the most
vulnerable, with limited mobility, higher disease prevalence, and financial
dependence exacerbating their susceptibility to climate impacts.
Climate hazards directly affect welfare by increasing morbidity and mortality, as
well as damaging health, sanitation, and educational facilities. Furthermore,
resource-constrained households may be compelled to make suboptimal
investment decisions regarding health and education to cope with the aftermath
of a shock, resulting in severe and long-lasting consequences for their children.
Adverse impacts of climate shocks on children’s health and nutrition emerge in
various country contexts. In the semiarid region of northeastern Brazil,
negative rainfall shocks are correlated with higher infant mortality, lower birth
weight, and shorter gestation periods (Rocha and Soares 2015). However,
mortality effects are reduced for municipalities with higher access to piped
water and sanitation coverage. Skoufias and Vinha (2013) find that rainfall
shocks also have a negative impact on the average height for age; however, the
effects vary spatially and temporally in rural Mexico. Furthermore, there is
suggestive evidence that, on average, weather-related illnesses are more
prevalent with increases in precipitation. Examining rural households in
Central Mexico from 1998 to 2000, de la Fuente and Fuentes-Nieva (2010)
observe substantial negative impacts of weather-related shocks (droughts,
floods, frosts, and hurricanes) on self-reported child morbidity. Children in
households affected by extensive droughts faced an 11 percent higher chance of
becoming ill than those in unaffected households. This figure rose to 20 percent
for children in households affected by floods and frosts. Analyzing the impact
of Hurricane Mitch in Nicaragua, Baez and Santos (2007) discover that affected
regions saw an 8.7 percent increase in the probability of child
undernourishment and a 30 percent decrease in the probability of receiving
medical consultation when sick. In particular, Hurricane Mitch had a large and
negative effect on children’s nutritional status, especially among those from
poorer, larger households with less-educated parents.
A study examining the intergenerational impact of climate hazards spanning a
century across 16 Latin American countries reveals significant consequences,

Risk of Climate Hazards

particularly during gestation and early childhood (Caruso 2017). Although the
impacts vary depending on each country’s degree of development, the general
finding is that these events lead to persistent adverse effects on health, human
capital formation, and asset accumulation. Specifically, an individual exposed to
flooding while in utero attains 0.47 fewer years of education (or 0.1 standard
deviation) compared with those not exposed. Moreover, there is notable
intergenerational transmission of these impacts, as children born to mothers
who were exposed to natural disasters experienced lower education levels and
higher rates of child labor.
A study in Ecuador (Rosales-Rueda 2018) finds that exposure to the 1997–98
El Niño floods in utero reduced children’s height, weight, and cognitive ability
and increased their probability of having anemia five and seven years after the
shock. The author argues that a potential mechanism behind these persistent
effects is the reduction in the duration of exclusive breastfeeding because
mothers substituted alternatives such as formula and other food for breast
milk.
Research has also documented the long-term impacts of weather shocks on
children’s school attendance, enrollment, and performance. Looking at birth
cohorts spanning more than 40 years in Colombia, Carrillo (2020) finds that
long-term in utero exposure to adverse rainfall shocks reduces years of
schooling, literacy rates, and likelihood of working while also increasing mental
disability rates. The effects are larger for those born in areas with a higher risk
of malaria. This result also holds at the municipality level. Palacios and RojasVelásquez (2023) show that excess rainfall shocks in Colombia increase school
dropout and decrease enrollment rates and academic performance in exposed
municipalities. The result can be explained by the impacts on income, as well as
the spread of weather-related diseases and the damage caused by rainfall to
roads and educational centers. Analyzing the impacts on high school
standardized exams in 13 Caribbean countries, Spencer, Polachek, and Strobl
(2016) also reveal negative and significant effects of hurricanes on performance
when school was in session. Furthermore, there is evidence of dire long-term
consequences into adulthood. For example, in Argentina, exposure to climate
shocks during gestation and the first year of life reduces the number of years of
schooling (with floods being particularly detrimental), and increases the
chances of being unemployed (with storms, heat waves, fog, frost, and rain
having stronger effects), and increases the likelihood of living in
multidimensionally poor households in adulthood (González, Santos, and
London 2022).
Many households lack sufficient coping mechanisms in the aftermath of
climate hazards, opting to put children to work or substitute for parents in
household chores. Evidence from rural Central Mexico shows that drought
reduces schooling and increases children’s labor force participation if other
risk-coping instruments are insufficient (de Janvry et al. 2006). In Nicaragua,
although school enrollment remained unaffected after Hurricane Mitch, labor

|

51

52

| Adapting to Adversity

force participation among children in affected areas surged by 58 percent, and
the proportion of children simultaneously enrolled in school and working more
than doubled (Baez and Santos 2007). For a comprehensive review of climate
change impacts across the life cycle, refer to Caruso, de Marcos, and Noy
(2024). Overall, climate hazards tend to produce long lasting effects on human
capital, especially among children.
This chapter looked at how climate shocks hit LAC households in terms of
welfare and poverty. Although plenty of research focuses on big-picture
effects—such as GDP levels and growth—we zoom in on the micro side, using
household data to see how people’s living standards and vulnerability change
after climate hazards materialize. That said, climate shocks also ripple through
the wider economy, affecting growth, public finances, and trade—a topic of
great interest and active research that is summarized in box 2.3.

Box 2.3

The macroeconomic consequences of materialized climate hazards
Economic theory offers competing hypotheses
about the impact of materialized climate hazards
on economic growth. Neo-classical models of
economic growth á la Solow (1956) would predict
faster economic growth in the aftermath of a
disaster because the destruction of capital assets
would increase the marginal productivity of the
remaining assets, propelling investment and
growth. Similarly, the idea of creative destruction
á la Schumpeter (1991) would suggest that after a
disaster old firms would be replaced with more
productive, better organized firms. But learningby-doing models á la Barro (Romer 1986) would
indicate that the destruction of human and
physical capital would have negative effects on
productivity and growth.
Initial research offers conflicting results. Raddatz
(2009) and Hochreiner (2009) find negative
impacts of disasters on gross domestic product
(GDP) growth, whereas Fomby, Ikeda, and Loayza
(2013) find a positive effect for some countries and
some types of disasters (floods). More recent
studies, however, seem to converge to a
consensus. According to Cavallo, Becerra, and
Acevedo (2022), using a sample of disasters

worldwide for the period 1970–2019, there was an
average decline of between 2.1 and 3.7 percentage
points of GDP growth the year of the shock. This
impact becomes larger with the severity of the
shock and smaller with the level of development
of the economy. However, these researchers find
little or no effect of disasters on GDP growth in
the medium term. This implies that GDP resumes
its pre-event rates of growth—but does not grow
faster—and hence output lost during the disaster is
not recovered.
National averages in after-shock GDP growth may
hide important differences across events, regions,
and activities (refer to Cavallo, Hoffmann, and
Noy 2023; Noy and duPont 2018). Some disasters
(for example, hurricanes) can be very serious and
deadly, but because their impact is circumscribed
to a region or locality, it is dimmed when
subsumed into average national statistics. But the
relative size of the country also plays a role: Small
islands tend to be more affected than any other
type of country because a disaster has a large
relative size in the economy. Some events have
specific impacts on certain activities and not others
(for example, droughts on agriculture), which
continued

Risk of Climate Hazards

|

53

Box 2.3 The macroeconomic consequences of materialized climate hazards (continued)

again may be absorbed into general economic
growth, particularly if the sector affected is
relatively small. Moreover, some disasters destroy
capital assets such as infrastructure, buildings, and
machinery (for example, earthquakes), whereas
others mainly affect durable goods (for example,
floods). Some authors such as Strulik and
Trimborn (2019) and Hallegatte, Hooste, and
McIsaac (2024) argue that this difference is key to
better understanding the medium-term impact of
disasters: The former would lead to lower growth,
but the latter could lead to higher growth, and a
combination may lead to no effect.
Public finance is also affected by climate
disasters. The net impact is again ambiguous and
depends on multiple channels that may tilt the
effect of disasters on fiscal balance one way or
another. The budgetary constraint of any
government, general or local, is defined by the
sum of expenditures and interest payments on
debt, balancing the sum of tax collections,
international aid, intergovernmental transfers,
insurance payments, withdrawals from public
funds, and new debt issuance. Changes on one
side of the equation have to be equal to changes
on the other, but specific changes have different
transmission channels and diverse consequences
on a country’s fiscal stance. Changes in general
expenditures by spending more on disaster relief
can be balanced by less spending on other items,
such as investment (which may affect long-term
growth) or withdrawals from a public fund
(which are designed to smooth out economic
growth over the cycle). Similarly, disasters may
affect the tax base (because people or firms
migrate out of the disaster zone) and the
availability to tap international aid (which is
somewhat unpredictable because it is outside the
discretion of the affected country) or trigger
payments from a previously contracted
insurance scheme. In the end, the institutional

setting and the characteristics of the event define
whether the country has to incur fiscal deficits
and issue new debt.
A recent review of empirical studies finds a
negative fiscal balance after climate shocks. With
some exceptions, postdisaster expenditures
increase and tax collections decline, which leads
to increased debt levels (Deryugina 2022). This
finding underlines the importance of active policy
design by fiscal and monetary authorities with
respect to these events. Climate hazards may
affect the fiscal stance of a country and its levels
of public debt. They may also have an influence
on the financial sector, interest rates, and
inflation as a consequence not only of the direct
impact of these hazards, but of the uncertainty
they may cast on economic activity and the value
of productive assets (Eickmeier, Quast, and
Schüler 2024).
Finally, climate hazards can also influence the
trade balance. As in the cases of economic growth
and public finance, the theoretical assessment is
somewhat ambiguous. Natural disasters that
damage transport infrastructure affect trade in
general; if the disaster has a large impact on
production of certain products, exports may
decline; if the impact is on general incomes, then
imports may decline. A review by Osberghaus
(2019) of more than 20 multicountry studies finds
that natural disasters are generally associated
with declines in exports, but there is no
consistent impact on imports. The impact is
usually larger in less-developed and small
economies. However, disasters are also correlated
with an increase in remittances and foreign aid,
as a response by families and governments to help
those affected by a disaster.
Trade in services shows a similar pattern.
Disruptions in economic activity may lead to fewer
exports of services but also more demand for
continued

54

| Adapting to Adversity

Box 2.3 The macroeconomic consequences of materialized climate hazards (continued)

imports. A study for the period 1995–2012 using
data for 190 countries and territories summarizes
empirical evidence showing a consistent negative
impact of disasters on exports but not on imports
of services (Xu and Kouwoaye 2019). The average
impact involves a decline of 2.0 to 2.9 percent in
exports of services after a large natural disaster.
This impact is more intense in some sectors than
others, particularly communication,
transportation, and travel services.

The research agenda on the impact of natural
disasters on macroeconomic variables such as
economic growth, public finance, and trade
balance is still ongoing. Questions about the
impacts of disasters on specific regions, on the
optimal fiscal policy to cope with climate
hazards, or on which trade policy would inhibit
the negative effects of climate are still open. Good
policy options on these macroeconomic factors
will provide better outcomes for the population’s
standards.

in de la Fuente and Serio’s (2025) version. However, their results are available in an
appendix upon request to the authors.
2. This method was initially developed by Doan et al. (2023) and recently extended by
Hill, Gorgulu, and Brunckhorst (2025). Data in the paragraph comes from the latter,
which also includes global data for comparison across regions. The rate of LAC
population exposed and vulnerable, 15.6 percent, is lower than in South Asia and
Sub-Saharan Africa (31.9 and 36.1 percent, respectively) but higher than in East Asia
and Pacific (10.6 percent) and similar to the Middle East and North Africa
(19.7 percent). The lowest exposure-cum-vulnerability rates occur in Europe and
Central Asia (4 percent) and North America (0.2 percent). These global indicators
are also the measure used for one of the eight vision indicators of the
World Bank corporate scorecard, “Improving resilience to climate hazards.” See
https://scorecard.worldbank.org/en/home.
3. Most studies focus on analyzing the impacts on poverty of extreme weather events
such as storms and floods, but a few look at how climate change itself fuels poverty.
López-Feldman and Mora Rivera (2018) predict that rural Mexico’s poverty
headcount could jump five to 13 points by 2046–55 (up from 45 percent in 2002).
Even bigger hits show up in the poverty gap and severity—meaning the poorest
individuals will suffer most. It is not even across the board: The south and southeast
could see poverty rates as high as 74 percent by mid-century.
4. The Global Climate Risk Index, calculated by Germanwatch, analyzes to what
extent countries and regions have been affected by weather-related loss events
(tropical storms, winter storms, severe weather, hail, tornados, and local storms;
hydrological events such as storm surges, river floods, flash floods, and landslides;
and climatological events such as snow and ice, wildfires, and droughts) in terms of
fatalities and economic losses, based on data from Munich Re’s NatCatSERVICE
(https://www.munichre.com/en/solutions/for-industry-clients/natcatservice.html).
5. Mean Global Climate Risk Index scores by region, from most to least affected, are as
follows: South Asia, 66.6; Latin America and the Caribbean, 71.8; East Asia and
Pacific, 77.95; North America, 87.33; Europe and Central Asia, 92.73; Sub-Saharan
Africa, 102.97; and Middle East, North Africa, Afghanistan, and Pakistan,
111.83. Lower scores indicate greater historical impacts from climate-related hazards.

Protecting Poor People from
Climate Hazard Risks

THE CASE FOR ADAPTATION NOW
As noted in chapter 1, climate change is expected to make climate hazards more
frequent and intense, with wide-ranging short- and long-term impacts on
welfare poised to stay. Although meeting the Paris Agreement target of keeping
the increase in the average global temperature below 2°C could lessen some
effects, the planet will keep heating up as emissions remain high. Currently,
efforts are off track; existing pledges are not enough to halve emissions in the
next decade. At this pace, global temperatures will surpass 1.5°C of warming
before mid-century and could exceed 3°C by 2100. 2024 was the first full
calendar year in which global temperatures were 1.5°C above 1850–1900
preindustrial average levels, and the 2015–24 decade has been the hottest on
record (WMO 2025).
Much of the focus in the region (and elsewhere) to counter the effects of
hurricanes, droughts, and, more broadly, climate change has been on mitigation,
that is, curbing global warming by encouraging countries to burn less coal, oil,
and gas to reduce the emission of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases
and prevent temperatures from rising, which could in turn lead to more
extreme climate events.
Cutting emissions is essential and still the best long-term fix, but, as indicated
in chapter 2, welfare impacts from climate hazards that materialize—such as
heat and drought—will persist unless adaptation takes place. Adaptation
involves acknowledging the reality of climate change and taking measures to
safeguard people from its consequences. That means risk reduction and loss
prevention policies to support adaptation are crucial in the short term. This
section reviews some key adaptation responses.

61

62

| Adapting to Adversity

A conceptual framework to understand adaptation
Ehrlich and Becker (1972) laid out a useful way to think about how people and
governments adapt to risk that aligns closely with the two-pronged
framework of prevention and preparedness used in World Bank reports on
risk layering and resilience.1 It starts with the choices individuals or
institutions have. First is prevention—such as moving away from flood zones—
to reduce the probability of losses. Second, when prevention is too costly or
risk cannot be fully avoided, people then turn to insurance as a preparedness
mechanism to manage residual risk. This mechanism could be self-insurance,
when the individual believes that they have enough personal resources to
finance the recovery, such as using savings or building up assets, or marketbased insurance, such as buying coverage for crops or property from an
insurer. Last is coping, which refers to strategies mainly undertaken after a
climate hazard has occurred and includes reducing consumption, selling
goods, borrowing or leaning on family or friends through remittances, or
government aid. More important, however, coping also needs ex ante
resources and preparation to be efficient. In other words, the capacity to cope
and adapt (sometimes called resilience) is sometimes built ex ante, by helping
to stabilize incomes, so that households and communities have a greater
capacity to cope effectively once climate hazards occur.
In practice, these three tools—prevention, insurance, and coping—are often
intertwined. For instance, an insurance program that is heavily subsidized
acts more as a social protection system than as insurance, and in some places,
the resources for social protection are insured on markets (refer to
figure 3.1). Furthermore, in this context, the risk layering approach becomes
essential: It ensures that cheaper sources of financing, such as contingency
budgets and reserves, are used first for frequent, lower-impact events,
whereas more expensive tools, such as insurance or market-based risk
transfer instruments, are reserved for rare, high-impact events. This
structured, layered strategy helps ensure that resources are available when
most needed and that limited budgets are used efficiently to protect those
who are most vulnerable.
Ehrlich and Becker’s (1972) framework assumes people weigh their options
carefully to get the most out of their resources and maximize their welfare.
Thus, their choices are not arbitrary but strategic. On the one hand, for rare or
extreme hazards such as a category 5 hurricane, prevention alone may be
impractical because of high upfront costs and delayed benefits. In those cases,
market insurance makes more sense. On the other hand, frequent, smaller risks
are often best managed through self-insurance. As for the income and costs
faced, only the wearer knows where the shoe pinches, and so families
ultimately choose what fits their preference and budget best. As prevention gets
cheaper or risks shrink, self-protection becomes more attractive. Full
prevention, such as moving away from hurricane areas, might be out of reach if
it means losing job opportunities. In those cases, people may just choose to live
with the risk and deal with it if and when it materializes.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks
