A family is better off using all three risk management tools—prevention,
insurance, and coping—rather than just one. They might avoid floods by living
on high ground but still farm in low-lying, fertile areas. They could insure some
risks, such as crops, while managing others themselves. These tools work
together, creating trade-offs. For example, if storm insurance is too expensive
because of low risk, they might choose self-insurance. Another trade-off
involves survival priorities: Prevention can seem like a luxury when immediate
needs are more pressing. So far, this framework assumes individuals can only
influence their own climate risks.
Adaptation is often seen as a private choice, but public adaptation is also
warranted. In reality, many preventive actions cannot be taken individually,
such as retrofitting a school or health facility to make it hurricane resistant or
constructing a levee to avoid flooding. They require a plan that coordinates
collective action rather than individual effort. This is where adaptation policy
steps in.

|

63

64

| Adapting to Adversity

Adaptation policies are also crucial when barriers such as poor information,
weak property rights, and limited access to credit or insurance prevent
households from managing climate risks effectively (Carleton et al. 2024).
Some may not even realize they are in harm’s way, whereas others might be
aware but unable to afford risk reduction measures—such as relocating—
because of insecure property rights or high costs. So government policies to
create early warning systems (EWSs) using radio broadcasts, enforcing land
tenure security and sea walls, and protecting mangroves can likely have large
welfare gains.
Even without market failures, equity-driven concerns warrant public
adaptation, especially for poorer communities. Climate change deepens
inequality because of uneven exposure (refer to chapter 1, “When climate
hazards meet poverty” section) and adaptation spending (see chapter 4,
“Institutions” section). A dry planting season can push vulnerable households
without proper risk management tools toward safer but lower-return
activities, trapping them in poverty. Droughts or floods can cause lasting
damage, forcing desperate actions such as selling assets or pulling kids from
school. Acknowledging the limits of self-insurance, governments provide
safety nets—cash transfers, emergency aid, and shelters (such as schools on
high ground or stilts)—and insurance to protect those most at risk.
Just like households, governments’ approach to risk can be understood through
the lens of prevention, insurance, or coping. Government’s approach depends
on the type of hazard, costs, and budget but also on perceptions and equity
concerns. It might fully prevent certain risks (for example, by relocating people
from flood zones, even if those areas are more fertile), insure against others
(such as buying weather insurance), and handle the remaining risks (such as
handing out cash during floods). Table 3.1 shows an abbreviated overview of
the adaptation strategies available to individuals, communities, and
governments.
Effective adaptation needs both individual and government decisions to
work together: weather forecasts and moving to shelters or zoning and road
access for commuting. Other actions are substitutes, such as using fire
extinguishers at home versus funding fire departments. Similarly, local, state,
and federal services may complement or substitute for each other.
Ultimately, the effectiveness of one depends on the other. Although
collaboration is essential, it also poses challenges. More evidence is needed
on how public systems compare with informal ones in terms of efficiency
and fairness, as well as the risk of crowding out private efforts. For example,
how do government risk reduction efforts affect personal mitigation actions?
How can governments show their commitment to risk reduction? Although
finding the optimal balance is outside the scope of this report (for a recent
discussion on this, see Shilpi, Kahn, and Berg 2025), chapter 4 touches on
incentives, information gaps, and constraints affecting household and
government choices.

Searching for multiple wins
By now, it should be clear that although reducing some risks is smart, full
prevention can sometimes be excessively costly. In those cases, some losses
could be insured against, and the risk that is left is borne. This is why adopting a
mix of adaptive strategies simultaneously—not just one after another—is key.
Still, this does not always happen. Households or governments might not be
able to afford or choose not to invest in upfront actions, sometimes because of
trade-offs, constraints, or moral hazard. The answer is to design interventions
that tackle multiple goals at once.
Multilateral financial institutions, international donors, and governments have
increasingly been advocating for adaptation actions that bring multiple
benefits. The first dividend comes from the ability of the investment to reduce
future losses. A second dividend could come from positive economic benefits
through reducing risk, increasing productivity, and driving innovation through

Donor assistance (contingent
loans)

66

| Adapting to Adversity

the need for adaptation. There could even be social and environmental benefits
that cut emissions, which in turn lower the probability of more and stronger
climate hazards (Global Commission on Adaptation 2019).
Take insurance, for example. Payouts help families quickly replace lost assets,
protecting their livelihoods and preventing further losses after a disaster.
Insurance can also improve creditworthiness for low-income households or
governments, encouraging investment in riskier but more rewarding activities,
leading to higher incomes. That extra income boosts resilience and provides
quicker access to resources when climate hazards materialize. Plus, when
insurance providers offer lower premiums for risk reduction, it motivates
people or governments to invest in prevention. In short, insurance can be
transformative by promoting higher-income opportunities, protecting assets
and livelihoods, and providing incentives to reduce risk—especially for poor
individuals.
This report examines five adaptation measures that can reportedly deliver
multiple benefits:
1. Better forecasting and widespread warning systems,
2. More critical infrastructure and climate-smart urban planning,
3. Protection of natural defenses against climate hazards such as forests and
other key ecosystems,
4. Improved financial tools and contingent financing to respond to climate-­
related hazards, and
5. Reinforced adaptative social protection systems and income support for poor
and vulnerable groups during realized climate hazards.

PREVENTION
People and assets concentrate in areas exposed to climate hazards through
population growth, migration, urbanization, and economic development.
However, when households, communities, and governments have adequate
preparations to counter the potential damage from such hazards, these
exposures can be prevented from translating into losses. For instance, effective
EWSs—for example, hurricane forecasts and evacuations—together with safe
housing and strong infrastructure, can significantly reduce vulnerability. This
section reviews key adaptation policies and mechanisms that help prevent
losses and increases in poverty and examines their current status in the region.

EWSs
Severe weather can hit any time—Atlantic storms from June to November and
wildfires and heat waves in the dry months. When danger looms, governments

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

can send loud phone alerts or flash warnings on TV, giving people time to reach
designated shelters and stay safe. This system of timely alerts and preparedness
is known as an EWS.
Timely warnings give people a chance to evacuate and protect their lives—and
sometimes their property. Robust EWSs linked to existing social protection
systems enable timely, risk-informed responses when climate hazards
materialize. (World Bank 2023). EWSs have been saving lives for centuries,
even showing up in ancient stories. An example is Noah’s Ark in the Old
Testament: Noah got a warning of an approaching flood, and he and his family
prepared for the impending disaster by constructing an ark that saved their
lives. Have early warning systems saved lives in Latin America and the
Caribbean (LAC) in recent decades?
An exercise conducted for this report looked at the number of casualties caused
by hurricanes of similar magnitude over the past 40 years in the region. The
scatterplot in figure 3.2 suggests that countries with advanced warning
capacities have fewer fatalities during storms of comparable intensity than
those with limited capabilities. Of course, this correlation likely reflects broader
governance and institutional factors. Countries with strong EWSs often exhibit
reinforcing advantages—such as robust infrastructure and greater
administrative capacity—that also contribute to lower mortality, making it
difficult to isolate the specific effect of any one factor.

| Adapting to Adversity

Even though EWSs are a low-cost, low-tech way to save lives from climate
hazards, many countries in LAC still face challenges. Not all of them have
strong systems in place. According to the World Meteorological Organization
(WMO), a good multihazard EWS has four key parts: knowing the risks,
monitoring and sending alerts, clearly communicating warnings, and having a
solid response plan. The more of these that are in place, the more advanced and
effective the system is.
Based on the available data from 32 WMO members from LAC, almost half are
currently providing climate services at advanced, essential, and full capacities.
However, six operate at a basic level for climate services, and 11 members do not
report information on climate services (refer to figure 3.3) (WMO 2022). As a
result, for instance, only 60 percent of people in South America are covered by a
multihazard EWS according to 2020 data available from nine countries
WMO 2022).

|

The inadequate capacity for end-to-end forecasting and warning services
affects mainly those living in lower-income countries and small island states.
Within countries, poor households seem to struggle the most: In the
Dominican Republic, for example, poorer households tend to search less for
weather information or receive flood warnings (refer to figure 3.4). The main
channel used to search for weather information also varies across wealth
levels: Poorest households rely more on TV, whereas wealthier households
tend to scan social media or check web pages. However, only two-thirds of
poor households have a TV, and only 9 percent have a computer or tablet with
which to access web pages. As such, improving access to weather information
could increase the level of awareness during floods and bring huge dividends
to poor households.
Better-educated households are also more active in searching for weather
information and attending flooding information seminars. Households headed
by someone with tertiary education are better informed on weather conditions:
Of them, 74 percent actively look for weather information, relative to 55 percent
of those with secondary education and 45 percent of those with primary
education or less.

70

| Adapting to Adversity

Ensuring information reaches those in need is not sufficient—better
weather forecasting is also key. In Brazil, meteorologists missed the mark
in February 2023, failing to predict heavy rains that led to deadly mudslides
in Petrópolis and more than 200 deaths. In places such as Bahia and
Minas Gerais, however, more accurate forecasts helped keep casualties
down (Sengupta 2022). In Mexico, Hurricane Otis caught everyone off
guard, rapidly intensifying before landfall. If forecasters had picked up on
its strength earlier—when it was still a tropical storm—they could have
issued stronger warnings and given people more time to get out of
harm’s way.
According to the UN (2022), every country should put end-to-end EWSs in
place in the next four years to reduce the impacts of weather- and climaterelated hazards. With the exposure of populations and economies to climate
events on the rise, the consequences of failing to ensure that every person in
LAC is protected by EWSs could be onerous. Chapter 4 proposes some ways to
produce better forecasts, make them more widely available, and make people
heed warnings.

Resilient cities
Cities can lift people out of poverty. People live where it is convenient to work.
Urban areas attract people from the hinterland because manufacturing and
services provide better employment than farming. The 2009 World
Development Report (World Bank 2009) shows how the benefits of scale,
agglomeration economies, and specialization increase productivity and
prosperity. These benefits draw people to migrate to urban centers from rural
settings. The density of cities promotes productivity and offers opportunities to
improve people’s livelihoods and quality of life, eventually helping lift many out
of poverty. For firms and workers in urban areas, proximity makes skills
matching and job searching more efficient. For governments, basic public
services and infrastructure can be provided at a lower cost thanks to economies
of scale. For the urban poor, economic diversity and clustering create pathways
out of poverty.
By contrast, recurrent floods—common in LAC cities—can undermine urban
agglomeration’s role in reducing poverty and integrating labor markets by
causing power failures, service disruptions, property damage, and business
closures. In Guayaquil, a key economic center in Ecuador, flooding repeatedly
paralyzed transportation systems over 2018–19, preventing low-income workers
from reaching their jobs and resulting in lost wages. A one-week disruption of
critical road segments in this region could result in losses of approximately
-$35 million for households (Quichimbo-Miguitama et al. 2022). In Chile and
Colombia, high flood risks limit upward mobility (Nakamura et al. 2023). And
with climate change increasing extreme precipitation, floods—already the most
common climate hazard in LAC—are expected to become more frequent and
intense (IPCC 2022).

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

When development outpaces a local government’s ability to provide adequate
services (electric, water, and sewerage connections; land titles; and so forth),
shanty towns emerge, making housing particularly vulnerable to flood
damage.2 It is therefore not surprising that housing accounts for much of the
damage. In contrast, commercial buildings, with secure land titles and better
construction, often remain intact, leaving the government to decide whether
to permit rebuilding in the same location. When shoddy homes are destroyed
but industries survive, rebuilding is simpler—public infrastructure can be
relocated to safer areas or rebuilt to better standards, and residents can choose
to stay or move. In Tegucigalpa, Honduras, repeated flooding has pushed some
low-income neighborhoods to safer but more remote areas, reducing flood risk
but increasing economic vulnerability by distancing residents from jobs and
social networks (Fay 2005, in Hallegatte and Rozenberg 2017). Yet, as
chapter 2 highlights, many poor communities continue to reside in high-risk
flood zones.
Cities need to continue adapting to floods and other risks, including those from
urbanization and climate change. As urban areas and populations grow, the
dynamic nature of urbanization alters the cost-benefit assessments of risk
reduction strategies. The 2009 World Development Report emphasized that
governments should support, not prevent, urbanization by providing necessary
institutions, services, and jobs. There are no ready recipes to create better
adaptation, but much can be done to foster it. Although exposure to climate
hazards may rise, vulnerability may fall as incomes increase. Governments have
various tools to address these challenges effectively.
As table 3.1 in this chapter shows, the adaptation toolbox offers a range of
solutions, from migration and weather forecasts to less disruptive options such
as sandbags and ditches for households and schools. Many flood impacts can be
mitigated by improving institutions—such as creating inclusive urban plans,
clarifying land rights, and raising awareness. Interventions should benefit all
residents, with a focus on marginalized populations. To reduce climate
vulnerability, adaptation strategies must address both the increased exposure of
poor people and the institutional and socioeconomic factors. Leveraging
affordable technologies, such as community-based EWSs and flood defenses
(for example, public spaces and nature-based solutions), is crucial, as noted
earlier. But other, more costly options can also prevent flood risks, such as gray
infrastructure (of which dams are one example). Although existing literature on
resilient cities often focuses on urban planning—for example, building codes
and general adaptation (World Bank 2016)—this section highlights two less
explored areas: dams and critical infrastructure for recovery.
On the basis of econometric analyses using a 23-year city-level panel data set
covering 1,013 flood events in 582 LAC cities from 2000 to 2023 (Gandhi et al.
2024), we attempt to understand whether people and governments succeed in
alleviating the negative impact of floods on population, mortality, and economic
activity through migration and gray infrastructure. Do they move to locations

|

71

72

| Adapting to Adversity

with lower risk? Do public investments in prevention through dams upstream
(which can reduce flood risk by regulating water flow downstream) reduce
mortality? Does critical infrastructure such as roads, water supply, and sewage
disposal systems reduce negative economic impacts and help recovery?
Short-term migration does not seem to be a key response to floods, especially in
poorer countries. In LAC countries, whether high or low income,3 past-year
floods have no significant impact on population, indicating that residents
remain despite the floods. However, cross-sectional analysis shows lower
population growth in flood-prone cities in high-income LAC countries (refer to
figure 3.5). The limited migration response in poorer nations raises climate
justice concerns, because the cities in poor countries face greater risks with
fewer adaptation options.
Consistent with the overall drop in deaths from climate hazards shown in
figure 2.4 in chapter 2, flood-related deaths in LAC have been slowly declining
over time—by about 2.2 percent per year, after accounting for factors such as
population, urbanization, rainfall, and elevation. This figure is below the global
average of 6.8 percent. The trend is not seen in high-income countries or
coastal cities, but cities in low-income countries show a sharper drop
(16 percent), as do inland cities (4 percent). This likely reflects progress in
preparedness against climate hazards, EWSs, emergency response, and other
risk reduction efforts in places that previously lacked them (refer to figure 3.6).
Upstream dams also appear to help reduce flood-related deaths, but mostly in
cities in high-income countries. In low-income countries, dams do not show
a clear effect on flood resilience. Inland cities, particularly capitals, seem to
benefit most from upstream dam infrastructure (refer to figure 3.7).

Disparities in flood adaptation in LAC are evident in various forms. Wealthier
nations are better equipped to anticipate and respond to climate hazards. Lowincome cities experience high economic losses and death rates but show no
significant out-migration, highlighting limited self-protection options. Also, in
low-income countries, upstream dams are often ineffective, pointing to the
need for increased investment and operational efficiency. Within poor
countries, capital cities mitigate losses more effectively, suggesting that fiscal
constraints limit broader geographical adaptation. Floods in capital cities,
which are better funded, tend to have less impact on average. However, coastal
cities in low-income countries, which are associated with a high concentration
of population and economic activity (Balboni 2025), suffer the most. These
cities generally have less critical infrastructure and fewer downstream dams
than inland cities—and significantly less than cities in high-income countries.
Some, however, are tourism hubs and may be better served in specific areas.
Despite the high payoffs in saved lives and avoided damages through large-scale
critical infrastructure that reduces urban flood risk, there is a clear protection
gap between rich and poor countries in LAC, and between coastal and capital
cities within poorer countries in LAC.
In short, LAC has experienced swift urbanization, becoming the most urbanized
developing region globally while escalating exposure to climate hazards.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

|

75

The urban landscape serves as a nexus for significant economies of scale
and agglomeration. However, it is also where stark inequalities, poverty,
informality, and environmental vulnerability are expressed. The dichotomy of
these urban realities underscores the intricate challenges faced by the region
in balancing growth and development with social equity and climate
resilience (refer to box 3.1).
What can the public sector do to reduce the risk of urban floods, with a focus
on disparities in particular? Ex ante, good institutions that allow land markets
to function properly regulate development in hazardous locations and
provide public infrastructure that reduces risk while ensuring growth are all
necessary. But also, what is the appropriate role of the public sector in urban
redevelopment efforts after climate hazards strike (assessment of need for
large-scale redevelopment efforts; political economy aspects)? Chapter 4
looks at some of these issues more carefully.

Box 3.1

Heat waves and inequality
Floods and storms are not the only threats hitting
cities in LAC; heat waves are on the rise, too.
Thanks to the urban heat island effect, city areas
get much hotter than the countryside. Concrete
and tightly packed buildings trap heat and stop it
from escaping, turning cities into ovens and
making them feel even hotter.

hotspots (refer to maps 3.1.1c and 3.1.1f). In
Barranquilla, these hotspots show up mainly in
the west and south, whereas the wealthier
northern areas stay cooler thanks to the coast.a
In Mexico City, the heat hits hardest in the east,
where many lower-income communities are
located.

Densely built, low-income neighborhoods tend to
be much hotter. Informal settlements often pile
on poverty, heat risk, and weak infrastructure.
Poorly built homes trap heat thanks to bad
ventilation and heat-absorbing roofs. On top of
that, these areas often lack clean water to refresh,
parks and green space, and good transit. And
because they are usually on the city’s edge,
residents have a tough time reaching things such
as health care or cooling centers.

Unfortunately, research also shows big disparities
in technological adaptation to rising heat. Noy
and Strobl (2022) found that heat waves drive
innovation in cooling technology, especially air
conditioning (AC). As expected, hotter
temperatures lead more people to adopt AC, but
not equally across the board. Using detailed
electricity data from Colombia, McRae (2023)
found that wealthier households get AC faster
and use it more when temperatures rise. Lowerincome households, however, adopt it more slowly
and often do not use it as much, even when they
have it—likely because of high running costs.
More optimistically, lower-income households
seem to be increasingly adopting AC technology,
gradually converging toward the use rates that
are observed for higher-income households.

A deep dive into Barranquilla, Colombia, and
Mexico City, Mexico, shows a clear link between
heat and multidimensional poverty (refer to
map 3.1.1). In both places, the hottest areas
(refer to maps 3.1.1a and 3.1.1d) often line up
with the poorest neighborhoods (refer to
maps 3.1.1b and 3.1.1e), creating major risk

a. Geography creates exceptions from city to city. In Bogotá, poorer areas have fewer trees but stay cooler thanks to higher elevation.
Medellín flips the script—heat intensity is lower in poorer areas, likely because many informal settlements are tucked into the greener hills
around the city. And although low-income communities often live in hot coastal zones, Barranquilla’s poorer residents are mostly inland,
missing out on the sea breezes.

Climate-proofing the future: The role of land protection and
ecosystems in adaptation
Although EWSs and gray infrastructure provide some protection against
climate hazards, they cannot prevent these events or fully eliminate their
impacts. Nature, however, can also be a powerful ally. Forests regulate
temperatures and capture carbon, and coastal ecosystems—such as coral reefs,
mangroves, salt marshes, and dunes—form a natural barrier against storms,
flooding, and erosion. Grasslands help safeguard water sources during
droughts. Beyond their environmental benefits, these ecosystems support
social equity, because many marginalized communities depend on forests and
fisheries for their livelihoods. By protecting lives, coastal properties, and
biodiversity, these so-called nature-based defenses offer another solution for
adaptation to climate challenges. The Global Commission on Adaptation (2019)
says mangrove protection, for instance, pays off big—more than $5 in benefits
for every $1 spent. Another global study finds that mangrove flood protection
benefits exceed -$65 billion per year (Menéndez et al. 2020). In Central
America, del Valle et al. (2019) found that wide mangrove belts can help
cushion the economic hit from hurricanes.
Protecting ecosystems is not easy, however—people need food, and that puts
pressure on nature. As economies grow, roads cut into protected areas, and
people push further in. Greed for minerals and profits also fuels illegal logging
and forest clearing. The biggest driver of deforestation is converting land for
cattle and crops. In LAC, forests are being cleared for ranching, farming, land
grabs, and mining. Between 1990 and 2020, forest cover dropped from
53 percent to 46 percent—a loss of 138 million hectares of forested land
(CEPAL 2021). Brazil has historically been the leading country in the region in
deforestation, especially in the Amazon. Whereas agriculture wipes out
habitats on land, overfishing depletes life and ecosystems in the oceans.

|

77

78

| Adapting to Adversity

Smaller countries such as Anguilla, The Bahamas, Belize, Guyana, and
Suriname are especially vulnerable to mangrove loss, relying on those coastal
ecosystems for major economic protection relative to their GDP.
On top of that, the world still depends on fossil fuels, making climate change
worse and adding to the challenge. Staple crops such as wheat, corn, and rice
are already experiencing reduced yields because of changing climate conditions
(IPCC 2022). A study of 20 Latin American countries found negative impacts
on yield across most crops (except for soybeans). Average percent yield losses
between 2022 and 2049 were at least 10 percent for most crops, with some up
to 60 percent loss in yield. Agricultural labor productivity losses were also
significant, with Central America and the Caribbean most affected
(Banerjee et al. 2021).
A review of policies to improve land use and sustainability in LAC shows mixed
results in reducing deforestation and tackling poverty. Setting up and enforcing
protected areas can help slow deforestation, but it can also affect nearby
communities. Securing land rights has been around for a long time in the
region, but its impact on both forests and poverty varies. Strengthening
Indigenous land rights could lead to better results. Paying farmers for planting
cover crops, no-till farming, rotational grazing, tree planting, wetlands
restoration, and careful use of fertilizers can curb deforestation while reducing
poverty. Finally, adjusting agricultural incentives is key to balancing land use in
the region, with potential benefits for both the environment and household
incomes (refer to table 3.2) (Turner, Lozano Gracia, and Hawthorne 2025).

Protected areas and land tenure security and management
Over the past century, Latin American countries have drawn boundaries to
shield thousands of their most valuable ecosystems, from the Amazon forests to
Argentina’s savannas. The region leads in conservation, with more than
24 percent of its land protected—well above the global average of 15.3 percent
(as of 2021).6 LAC’s total protected area exceeds Brazil’s landmass, highlighting
its leadership in terrestrial and marine conservation (Hatrick 2023).
Protecting land is a straightforward way to fight deforestation—it cuts rates
by about 41 percent, although results vary (Wade et al. 2020; Wolf et al. 2021).
A global review found that 55 percent of protected areas reduced deforestation
both inside their borders and in nearby areas, and 12 percent saw it spiking just
outside. In Latin America, deforestation is usually lower in protected areas, but
spillover effects are mixed. Andam et al. (2008) point out that comparisons can
be tricky if protection pushes deforestation elsewhere.

80

| Adapting to Adversity

Protected areas support rural livelihoods by preserving soil, water, and
biodiversity. They boost incomes through farming, fishing, and tourism, with
environmental products making up 22–28 percent of rural incomes, benefiting
poor individuals the most (Angelsen et al. 2014; Vedeld et al. 2007). In the
region, many poor communities live in or near protected land: of poor
individuals, 23 percent live in areas with at least 10 percent protected land, and
59 percent live near some protection (refer to map 3.1). In places such as
Bolivia, Costa Rica, and the Peruvian Amazon, protected areas have reduced
poverty—often through tourism—while preserving forests (Ferraro and
Hanauer 2014).7
Despite their value, protected areas face major challenges. Poverty and human
pressure often lead to overuse and environmental damage. In the Amazon,
large-scale farming and ranching have driven deforestation since 1990 (Bagolle,
Costella, and Goyeneche 2023). From 1990 to 2015, the region’s forests shrank

MAP 3.1

Overlays of monetary poverty and protected areas (as share of
total land) in LAC

   
Source: Turner, Lozano Gracia, and Hawthorne 2025.
    Note: LAC = Latin America and the Caribbean.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

by 14 percentage points—seven times the global average. Latin America has
suffered the highest biodiversity loss in 50 years (WWF 2022). In 2022 alone,
10.2 million acres of primary forest were lost, with Brazil accounting for more
than 40 percent of the destruction. Limited funding is a major issue; current
budgets cover just 54 percent of basic financial needs (Bovarnick, Alpizar, and
Schnell 2010). Connectivity is another challenge—only 9 of 51 countries have
their protected areas well linked, which further weakens ecosystem protection
(IUCN 2021).
Indigenous land rights and forest management play a key role in reducing
deforestation and promoting inclusion, although legal titling alone has mixed
impacts. In Latin America, Indigenous-managed lands consistently show lower
deforestation—often matching or outperforming other protected areas
(Blackman et al. 2017; Blackman and Veit 2018; Busch and Ferretti-Gallon 2023;
Holland et al. 2014; Schleicher et al. 2017; Vergara-Asenjo and Potvin 2014).
Studies across Bolivia, Brazil, Colombia, Ecuador, Panama, and Peru back this
up (Busch and Ferretti-Gallon 2023). Indigenous peoples occupy 35 percent of
the region’s forests. In places like the Dry Chaco (Camino et al. 2023) and
Ecuador (Tanner and Ratzke 2022), land titling with nongovernmental
organization support has driven real forest gains. Studies on forest property
rights across 24 countries report positive or mixed results, although much of
the evidence is still observational (Miller et al. 2021).
Climate-smart agriculture
Climate-smart agriculture (CSA) is gaining recognition for its role in reducing
deforestation while boosting agricultural productivity and resilience. Practices
such as agroforestry (growing trees alongside crops, a land management
approach that improves soil health, boosts yields, and provides habitats for
wildlife), conservation farming, and better crop varieties promote sustainable
land use and carbon storage (Zheng, Ma, and He 2024). Agroforestry in
particular helps increase production, build resilience, and fight climate change.
CSA also supports soil health and water management, easing pressure on
forests (Williams and Green 2023; Zheng, Ma, and He 2024).
CSA and land improvements show potential to cut deforestation and ease
poverty, although the evidence on poverty impacts is still emerging. These
methods can raise productivity and farmers’ incomes. A review of 19 CGIAR
case studies, including examples in Colombia, Costa Rica, Mexico, and
Nicaragua, found mixed to positive effects on malnutrition, productivity, and
fertilizer use, lowering costs and boosting profits (Dinesh et al. 2015).
Argentina offers a striking example of large-scale adoption of regenerative
agriculture. Faced with severe soil erosion, the country promoted no-till
practices to reform its agricultural sector. Although initial experiments began in
the early 1970s, widespread adoption only took off in the 1990s. By 2011, no-till
farming covered more than 23 million hectares—around 79 percent of

|

81

82

| Adapting to Adversity

Argentina’s grain-cropped land, up from just a few hundred thousand hectares
in 1990. A key driver of this transformation was the active role of farmers
associations, motivated by the promise of higher yields, increased profits, and
effective erosion control (Peiretti and Dumanski 2014).
Payments for ecosystem services
Sometimes local people chop down trees for charcoal and clear space for
subsistence farming to make a living. A cheap and easy way to slow down the
pace of deforestation is to pay landowners small sums not to cut down their
trees or restore them or to finance the adoption of more efficient technologies.
The idea of paying people in poorer countries to protect their forests has long
attracted interest from those concerned about climate change, but mainly for
reducing emissions from deforestation and forest degradation.
Payment for ecosystem services (PES) programs have a long history in Latin
America and are key tools for enhancing ecosystem services. Globally, at least
550 PES programs handled $36–$42 billion in annual transactions as of 2018
(Salzman et al. 2018). Latin America had at least 57 PES programs focused on
water, but the actual number is likely much higher. A previous study found 40
PES schemes evenly split between water and other sectors, suggesting broader
coverage (Grima et al. 2016). Although carbon credits get most of the attention,
other PES models can help promote sustainable land use and CSA by
addressing gaps left by other measures.
PES programs can help reduce deforestation, especially when well targeted, but
challenges such as additionality and permanence remain. A review of 19 studies
across eight Latin American countries found PES impacts similar to protected
areas (Börner et al. 2020; Wunder et al. 2020). In Brazil, Bolsa Verde cut
deforestation by encouraging locals to report illegal clearing (Wong et al. 2022).
In Mexico, payments reduced loss of tree cover and led to more patrolling, fire
breaks, pest control, and soil conservation (Alix-Garcia, Sims, and Yañez-Pagans
2015). Recent evaluations of PES programs outside LAC also show promising
results (Jayachandran et al. 2017). Still, issues like poor targeting—such as many
deforestation PES systems not being targeted at high-deforestation-risk
locations; weak enforcement; and self-selection, in which people take money to
protect their own land but then cut down trees elsewhere (Badgley et al. 2022;
Coffield et al. 2022; Jack and Jayachandran 2019)—limit effectiveness. Longterm impact on conservation, restoration, or afforestation also depends on
sustained payments because landowners may game the system over longer
periods of time.
PES programs can help reduce poverty, although the benefits are usually
modest (Ola et al. 2019; Pagiola, Arcenas, and Platais 2005). Balancing
environmental goals with poverty reduction is tricky—focusing too much on
one can limit the other. Because most programs mix compensation payments
for conservation with pure transfer to improve welfare, their impact depends

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

on costs, design, and who gets them. When tailored to local needs, well funded,
and closely monitored, PES can deliver win-win results. They are especially
effective when opportunity costs are low and environmental gains are high. In
Uganda, PES showed modest pro-poor effects, and in Mexico, it did not worsen
poverty and slightly improved assets, education, and local conditions (Gauvin
et al. 2010; Jayachandran 2023).
So, what does all the discussion on preventive efforts against the impacts of
climate hazards mean? Acting fast—within the first 24–48 hours—can save
lives. Early warnings give people time to escape, and longer lead times help
protect property. Today’s technology can provide that lead time, and despite the
costs, forecasts bring even bigger benefits. Still, many countries underfund
forecasting and have weak warning systems. Critical infrastructure that can
withstand climate hazards also helps, but prevention efforts often fall short—
not necessarily from lack of spending but from poor allocation. True prevention
needs smart coordination across all levels of government and society. Even with
tools such as warning systems, flood barriers, or natural assets like forests, the
poorest people may still be left vulnerable; this is where insurance steps in as
the next layer of protection. The next section dives deeper into this issue.

INSURANCE
We noted before that people and governments (who cannot or may not choose
to shoulder the costs of adopting risk reduction measures) should not rely only
on preventive measures to eliminate climate risks—nor do they. Sometimes
weather forecasts fail. Floodproofing sparsely populated areas may not be costeffective. People underinvest in protecting their housing when they lack formal
land titles. Insufficient information on hazard exposure keeps households
close to hazard-prone areas. Mayors underserve slums through poor-quality
services.
Both individuals and governments can buy insurance to cover property or crop
damage in exchange for regular premiums. By pooling risks, overall losses
become more predictable, letting people protect themselves from financial
shocks. Although insurance does not reduce total losses, it improves overall
well-being by preventing catastrophic personal setbacks. Because premiums
often exceed expected losses, those who do not make claims help cover the
costs for those who do.
By offering a payout when an insured loss occurs, market insurance
compensates losses and avoids other costly ways of coping. Furthermore, when
support is guaranteed, people can take opportunities to escape poverty that
would be too risky to undertake without additional protection. So insurance
measures are designed for and executed after climate hazards strike but can
also improve prevention.

|

83

84

| Adapting to Adversity

Not all risks can be insured, and not everyone chooses commercial insurance.
For frequent but low-cost events, people often rely on self-insurance because
verifying claims can be expensive and thus formal insurance requires paying
higher premiums. Commercial insurance works better for rare but severe
hazards, although premiums are often high because of climate uncertainty and
the risk of mass payouts. Insurers add a “catastrophic load” to stay solvent, and
issues such as adverse selection and moral hazard drive costs up, especially in
remote farming areas. As a result, many farmers in Latin America cannot afford
traditional or multiperil insurance, limiting its use.

The promise of index insurance
Index insurance is a form of insurance that cuts costs and speeds up payouts by
linking claims to measurable events—such as rainfall or wind speed—rather
than actual losses. This lowers fraud risk and monitoring costs, reducing
premiums. It is commonly used for crops facing drought or excess rain,
whereas other triggers, such as wind speed, are linked to payments to protect
property and assets. Because these parameters are objective and highly
correlated with losses, payouts are swift and automatic, are predictable, and can
be based on a previously agreed-on and publicly available formula, with no
formal claims needed.
Parametric insurance can cover farmers or small businesses directly, but the
focus here is on governments. By pooling risks across populations, governments
can use index insurance to protect budgets when revenues drop and welfare
costs spike. Unlike emergency funds or appeals—which are prone to political
disputes and often to delayed disbursements during crises—index insurance
provides fast, predictable payouts after climate hazards hit, speeding up relief
and recovery. Unlike microinsurance, macro-level coverage uses regional
weather indices, making it more efficient, easier to manage, and less prone to
basis risk (Kramer et al. 2022).
Governments in LAC have used index insurance in two main ways. First, they
pool climate risks across the population and then buy catastrophe bonds (CAT
bonds) or weather derivatives, shifting extreme risk to regional or international
markets. These instruments can provide automatic triggered payouts to support
relief efforts. Second, once a government secures macroinsurance, it can offer
contracts to individuals, helping them manage risks in advance and rely less on
climate hazard recovery assistance.
The jury is still out on the impact of parametric insurance on welfare because
the evidence is by no means complete at this point. However, a handful of
studies have analyzed the penetration and impacts of index insurance. Such
evidence begs a review to highlight emerging lessons and consensus (if any)
and to point to the gaps in knowledge. The next section summarizes and
reviews existing literature on these aspects of parametric insurance in
the region.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

Has index insurance in LAC delivered or lived up to its promise?
Index insurance has expanded in LAC but still has low uptake among
individuals.8 A global survey found that the region accounts for just 0.4 percent
of all policyholders, although the value of its policies is relatively high. Most
programs are index based, with about half using area yield indexes. Drought
and flood are the most commonly insured risks. With a median program age of
11 years—the highest among developing regions—many initiatives are well
established. Around 80 percent receive government or donor subsidies (Hazell,
Jaeger, and Hausberger 2021).
Macro-level weather index insurance has gained more traction in LAC, helping
countries boost climate resilience for poor individuals. Nations such as
Colombia, Mexico, and Peru have used it to quickly inject cash into emergency
funds. A standout example is Mexico’s Fondo de Desastres Naturales
(FONDEN), launched in the late 1990s, which relies on parametric insurance,
specifically through CAT bonds and parametric reinsurance schemes. These
mechanisms provide financial protection for FONDEN against losses from
earthquakes and hurricanes by triggering payouts based on predefined
thresholds rather than actual damage. It allows for preapproved action plans,
including making money available in advance of a hurricane and immediate
funding for relief and reconstruction. By law, the fund receives 0.4 percent of
the federal budget annually (with unspent funds rolling over). FONDEN was
once seen as one of the world’s most advanced disaster financing tools, thanks
to its clear rules and dedicated bureaucracy.
A background paper for this report by del Valle et al. (2025) analyzes how
FONDEN affected household living standards in urban areas hit by excessive
rainfall. Using spatially granular measures of household living standards and a
natural discontinuity in FONDEN’s eligibility rules, they find that eligible
municipalities (those to the right of the dotted line in figure 3.9), compared
with those just below the threshold, saw improved living standards in the year
in which the climate hazard occurs—because those municipalities could start
relief and reconstruction immediately afterward (refer to figure 3.9). The gains
were especially notable in poorer municipalities.
Although these effects faded after one to two years, del Valle et al. (2025)
suggest that impacts on human capital might last longer. FONDEN was one of
the few programs globally with proven accomplishments protecting poor
individuals. However, the institutional landscape for disaster financing in
Mexico has evolved, including changes to FONDEN and CADENA (the
Mexican Catastrophe Climate Contingency Insurance Program), although
other forms of index insurance funding remain in place.9
Another way in which indexed instruments have helped finance disaster
assistance in the region is through sovereign risk pools. For example, the
Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance Facility (CCRIF), established in 2007,
pools risk using parametric policies (refer to box 3.2).

Box 3.2

Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance Facility: Balancing benefits and challenges
Since 2007, Caribbean and (increasingly) Central
American countries have been able to purchase
insurance from the Caribbean Catastrophe Risk
Insurance Facility (CCRIF) to cover risks from
hurricanes, earthquakes, and heavy rains (even
droughts have been considered). CCRIF is a
regional risk pool owned by Caribbean and
Central American governments, funded by a
multidonor trust fund and membership fees of its
19 Caribbean and four Central American
partners. It is designed to mitigate financial
impact by quickly providing short-term liquidity
to governments when a policy is triggered after a
climate hazard occurs. As the world’s first

regional fund using parametric insurance, CCRIF
has revolutionized how governments handle
climate risk, offering affordable coverage by
pooling regional risks. Similar pools exist in the
Pacific (the Pacific Catastrophe Risk Insurance
Company) and in Africa (the African Risk
Capacity) since 2014. Of the three country pools,
CCRIF has the longest experience and has
delivered more than $200 million in payouts.
In July 2024, CCRIF disbursed $6.4 million to
Guatemala after heavy rains caused damage.
Similarly, in October 2023, CCRIF provided
$3.4 million to Antigua and Barbuda and the
British Virgin Islands after Tropical Storm

continued

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

Box 3.2 Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance Facility: Balancing benefits and challenges (continued)

Philippe. However, not all climate hazards trigger
payouts; for example, Hurricane Dean (2007)
caused damage in Jamaica, but the payout was
not triggered because wind speed was the key
parameter. Similarly, the cumulative effect of the
2008 hurricanes in Haiti was devastating, but the
winds were not strong enough to trigger a payout.

In response to member concerns over such gaps
or basis risk, CCRIF has adjusted its triggers and
added a secondary payout if losses are significant
but do not meet the full trigger threshold
(Kramer et al. 2022; Martinez-Diaz, Sidner, and
McClamrock 2019).

Ensuring resources flow quickly into state coffers after a hurricane is key, but
it does not necessarily help victims directly. To truly protect poor individuals,
there should be clear rules to direct the payouts to assist the affected
communities, and strong safety nets should be in place before climate hazards
hit with strong technical and administrative capacities on the ground. Without
these, support can be delayed. The next section on coping explores whether
social protection systems in the region are suited to this purpose and whether
index insurance could strengthen them.
Poor people benefit when clear, previously agreed-on rules guide insurance
payouts for relief following climate hazards. Mexico’s CADENA program lets
states buy weather insurance to protect poor farmers. Instead of covering
individuals, states pay premiums and get payouts based on triggers such as
rainfall deficit, then distribute funds. According to program rules, states must
preregister small farmers (those with less than 20 hectares or 60 livestock
units) who are deemed eligible, and this has helped CADENA reduce poverty;
an analysis from 2003 to 2010 found that it lifted 100,000 farmers out of
moderate poverty each year.10 Other works on CADENA suggest that payouts
encouraged more planting and higher spending, both plausible drivers of the
poverty reduction observed (de Janvry, Ramirez Ritchie, and Sadoulet 2016;
Fuchs and Wolff 2011; Fuchs Tarlovsky and Wolff 2016). However, beneficiary
surveys also show that 38 percent of beneficiaries faced six- to nine-month
delays in their payouts, and only a third of eligible farmers know about
CADENA. Mobile banking to send payouts to beneficiaries upon index triggers
and boosting awareness could further increase impact.11 Although not
definitive, de Janvry, Ramirez Ritchie, and Sadoulet (2016) suggest that the
income benefits of CADENA likely exceed its costs.
Another way to fund response to climate hazards is through donors buying
indexed insurance directly. This was first tested in 2006 by the World Food
Programme (WFP) and AXA (a global insurance and asset management group),
using drought-triggered payouts to support Ethiopia’s safety net program.
Equally promising is combining macro-level index insurance with letting

|

87

88

| Adapting to Adversity

people purchase micro-level top-ups. Governments can buy broad coverage to
handle fixed costs, which helps private firms offer individual microinsurance—
sometimes backed by subsidies. This setup helps with targeting and lets people
tailor support to their own needs. In other words, the government still
guarantees basic protection, but households can boost their coverage based on
what they own and need. In Nicaragua, the WFP is working on this kind of
blended approach (refer to box 3.3).
Index insurance is not just for farming; it can also protect shared natural
resources. A pilot project initiated by a Central American nongovernmental
organization, the Mesoamerican Reef Fund, backed by German development
bank KfW and the Nature Conservancy, partnered with Swiss Re to insure part
of a coral reef in Mexico against hurricane damage. When a storm hit in 2020,
the insurance paid out to Quintana Roo, funding quick coral reseeding and reef
restoration (Einhorn and Flavelle 2020). It also created jobs for locals who rely
on the reef for their income (Mesoamerican Reef Fund 2025).
Not enough is known about how index insurance affects people’s well-being
after climate hazards hit; more research is needed. That said, early studies
(including some work for this report) suggest that large-scale insurance can be
both cost-effective and meaningful. Still, even in countries like Mexico, with a

Box 3.3

Nicaragua’s experience with a hybrid insurance approach
Launched by the World Food Programme (WFP)
in 2020, Nicaragua’s Climate Risk Insurance
program combines national-level sovereign
insurance with local microinsurance to protect
against climate shocks such as droughts, floods,
and hurricanes. This two-tier approach creates a
safety net that covers both big shocks and smaller,
everyday risks—helping people recover faster and
build long-term resilience.
At the national level, the government works with
the Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance
Facility to insure against major events like
hurricanes and earthquakes. In 2022, WFP added
$70,000 to Nicaragua’s policy, unlocking up to
$2 million in potential payouts. That same year,
WFP also piloted microinsurance for farmers,
Source: Sugastti and Carter 2025.

covering more frequent, small-scale losses. The
program insured 432 people that year and is
shifting from traditional claims processes to
quicker parametric payouts without waiting for
government aid.
Both levels of insurance paid out in 2022. After
Hurricane Julia, a $640,000 payout helped
expand school meals for nearly 300,000 children,
and direct household payouts protected
livelihoods. Earlier, during Hurricane Iota in
2021, a pilot showed how fast microinsurance can
support farmers. By layering these tools,
Nicaragua is building a smarter safety net—and
WFP is now backing similar efforts in Belize,
Dominica, and St. Lucia, although these have yet
to trigger.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

long tradition of index insurance schemes, the institutional landscape for
climate risk financing has evolved, including changes to long-standing
successful programs such as FONDEN and CADENA. So why are these kinds of
index insurance models not more common across the region? The next section
and chapter 4 dive into that.

Insurance—Useful for some governments
There are a few plausible reasons why index insurance is not widely used by
governments in LAC. Politicians rarely get credit for ensuring the availability
of funds for disasters that might never happen—voters prefer visible, shortterm wins. Index insurance is not cheap, either. It is complex to design and
relies on data that reinsurers may not fully trust. It can even fail to pay out
after real losses, eroding trust among both insurers and those affected. Better
weather data and choosing parameters with care can help, but not all risks are
insurable. In some cases, setting aside reserve funds or borrowing (via
contingent loans) is more practical and quicker and better suited to deal with
frequent, lower-cost climate hazards. Contingent credit, in particular, does not
require preallocated funds and provides medium to large payouts as budget
support after a crisis, making it a solid option to deal with risks of medium
frequency and severity.
But borrowing is not always easy, especially for small or heavily indebted
countries. So, in countries like many in the Caribbean, with few financing
options but high exposure to and big budget impacts from climate risks, index
insurance remains a good option. And contingent credit does not always
directly reach affected communities. It is a good fit for moderate, midfrequency
risks. Reserve funds are useful but hard to build and often get repurposed, for
instance to partially free up cash during the COVID-19 pandemic.
Underpinning this discussion lies the concept of risk layering in climate risk
financing, which is basically about splitting potential costs into layers, based on
how big and how likely the losses are, then matching each layer with the most
cost-effective way to cover it. This is a useful concept to understand how
governments can approach their decisions when it comes to meeting financial
needs when climate hazards materialize. The small, frequent losses sit at the
bottom and are usually handled with cheaper tools such as reserve funds or
budget reallocations. The middle layer covers less frequent but more expensive
events, often using insurance or contingent credit. At the top are the rare,
catastrophic losses, which are so huge that high-powered tools such as CAT
bonds or international aid are needed. The point is to avoid overspending on
insurance for everyday events while still having the financial firepower for the
really big climate hazard events.
A review of 14 LAC countries looked at climate risk financing tools, splitting
them into risk retention (such as budget shifts, credit lines, and disaster funds
such as FONDEN) and risk transfer (such as traditional insurance, CCRIF, or

|

89

90

| Adapting to Adversity

CAT bonds). Across the region, there are 44 retention tools and 21 transfer tools
(refer to table 3.3). Most countries lean on disaster funds or contingent credit,
and fewer use budget reallocations. Traditional insurance is the most common
transfer option (used in 10 cases), but parametric insurance and CAT bonds are
still rare. Only Jamaica, Mexico, and Panama use a balanced, layered approach.
Altogether, the region manages $11.1 billion in risk-related resources—$4.3
billion from credit lines, $2.8 billion from disaster funds, $2 billion from CAT
funds, and $1.9 billion each from budget allocations and parametric insurance
(Cárdenas and Elizondo 2025).
Regardless of what disaster risk financing tools are in place, scaling up social
protection to address climate shocks creates a financial burden for
governments. As the next section shows, a World Bank (2023) assessment of the
adaptiveness of social protection systems in the region found that disaster
response financing is the weakest link in most countries’ capacity.
In sum, countries can purchase insurance to prepare for unexpected climate
hazards. Mexico cannot predict exactly when a hurricane will hit, but it knows
they will keep coming—and it can use public funds to insure against things such
as heavy rainfall that damages infrastructure. Insurance helps people and
governments trade for different “states of nature,” but no contract can cover
everything, and insurance markets do not always develop, nor do governments
always insure to protect themselves and their populations.
What cannot be prevented or insured against must be borne. When that
happens, the burden falls on households. It is not enough to predict a drought;
if there is no support, low-income families are left relying on friends and
relatives. But when hazards hit entire communities, those networks break
down, forcing families to sell assets or pull kids from school just to cope. The
next section looks at how governments can step in with safety nets to manage
these risks more effectively.

COPING
Public safety nets are vital for shielding the most vulnerable from climate
hazards. Well-designed food and cash transfer programs can be a critical last
line of defense. But if safety nets—or insurance—do not respond quickly,
families risk losing hard-won gains on welfare. As chapter 2 showed, climate
hazards that materialize can have deep, long-lasting, even generational impacts.
The next sections look at how governments can use their social protection
systems to help families cope better.

Social protection systems need to adapt better to climate
LAC has some of the strongest social protection systems in the developing
world. More than 75 percent of the poorest people receive benefits—mostly
through cash transfers—which can make up a third of their household
spending. Conditional cash transfers (CCTs), in particular, were pioneered in
the region—Mexico started the Programa de Educacion, Salud y Alimentacion
(PROGRESA) back in 1997—and have helped reduce poverty and build human
capital. From 1998 to 2014, social protection programs cut poverty by nearly
30 percent, and 18 countries still run active CCTs today (World Bank 2023),
reaching around 105 million people in 2019 (Stampini et al. 2021).
The success of CCTs in reducing poverty—thanks to wide coverage and decent
payouts—has pushed many countries to use their social protection systems as
tools for handling climate hazard events. These programs do not just provide a
safety net; they also help families stay stable, save, and even invest. With
predictable cash, people can take more productive risks and build assets that
cushion them if climate hazards hit, avoiding desperate moves such as pulling
kids out of school.
Overall, the results are positive. In Mexico, the PROGRESA-Oportunidades
program helped kids stay in school during tough times (de Janvry et al. 2006)
and reduced the impact of bad weather on education and caloric intake
(Adhvaryu et al. 2024; Hou 2010). In Nicaragua, when CCTs were paired with
job training or investment support, families were better able to ride out
weather-related hazards by diversifying their income (Macours, Premand,
and Vakis 2022). Outside Latin America, studies in Ethiopia, Niger, and
Zambia also saw that unconditional transfers improved food security and
decreased harmful coping strategies during droughts (Asfaw et al. 2017;
Knippenberg and Hoddinott 2019). Most solid studies show that higher
household incomes from CCTs help cushion the blow of materialized climate
hazards, although there are exceptions, such as Colombia (Arena, Guasti, and
Hussein 2023), where CCTs did not make much of a dent in climate
vulnerability.
Still, climate risks are a tough match for traditional CCTs. These programs
were not designed with climate in mind—they are mainly aimed at poor
households, not at vulnerable households who are not poor. Standard incomebased eligibility often misses people who are not poor now but could easily fall
into poverty in the aftermath of a climate hazard. de la Fuente and Serio
(2025) estimate that about 184 million people are vulnerable to poverty. Some
are already poor, but many hover just above the line—earning more than
$6.85/day (2017 purchasing power parity) but less than twice that (refer to
figure 3.10). Even for poor households, using CCTs to invest in long-term
resilience is not always realistic. The cash might be unpredictable or too
small to make a real difference.

Even with expanded coverage, there are big gaps. De la Fuente and Serio (2025)
found that in countries such as Honduras, and Panama—where climate-related
poverty risk is high—fewer than 40 percent of vulnerable people are covered by cash
transfer programs (refer to figure 3.11). On top of that, when climate hazards hit, quick
and flexible responses are required, but many safety nets still lack digital payment
systems and up-to-date social registries, making it hard to reach new people fast.
Countries have started to adapt cash transfer programs to cope with climate
hazards. During recent climate events and COVID-19, many scaled up support
by increasing payments or expanding coverage. Ecuador used cash vouchers
after the 2016 quake, and El Salvador, Mexico, and Peru have adapted existing
social protection programs to include climate-hazard-hit families. Dominica
did the same in 2017 (Beazley, Solórzano, and Barca 2019).
In 2021–23, the World Bank reviewed how well social protection systems in 18
LAC countries adapt to realized climate hazards (Tisei and Ed 2024). The focus
was on how these systems help poor victims and nonpoor vulnerable
households prepare for, cope with, and adapt to shocks. The assessment looked
at four key areas: (1) scalable programs and delivery systems to assist those in
need, (2) clear roles between social protection and disaster risk agencies,
(3) strong data and information systems (including early warnings and shockresponsive indicators), and (4) financing (which includes contingency or reserve
funds, contingent credit, and market-based risk transfer solutions) backed by
technical know-how and solid legal and institutional frameworks. Countries
were rated as latent, nascent, emerging, established, or advanced in each area.

Overall, countries in the region whose systems were qualified as established are
Argentina, Brazil, Chile, the Dominican Republic, and Uruguay. Countries that
are lagging and whose efforts were deemed nascent are Belize, Honduras, and
Paraguay (refer to table 3.4).
The review showed that although LAC countries are strongest in program
design and delivery—thanks to well-established social protection systems—
other areas still need work (refer to box 3.4). Digital payments have advanced
but do not yet reach enough people for a fast response. Many LAC countries
need to step up investments in EWSs, not just to improve monitoring and alerts
but also to connect them with national social protection programs. Right now,
coordination between climate risk and social protection agencies is weak, and
data systems, although somewhat flexible, are not fully ready to handle
materialized climate hazards. Only a few countries, such as Chile and the
Dominican Republic (with the Ficha Básica de Emergencia), have tools
specifically for emergency targeting, and Colombia’s Familias en Acción can

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

adjust its targeting protocols in response to manifested hazards (Beazley,
Solórzano, and Barca 2019). Financing to respond to climate hazards is the
major gap—only Argentina and Peru have in place emergency-linked funding
for climate hazards. Mexico’s FONDEN and the CCRIF exist, but they are not
directly tied to social programs. A few countries, such as the Dominican
Republic and Peru, have started allocating dedicated budget lines for
emergencies in their safety nets, but most still have a long way to go (refer
to Box 3.4).

Box 3.4

Few climate risk financing mechanisms are catered to poor individuals
There is broad agreement on the value of having
prearranged funding—whether through
insurance or credit—and that things such as
rainfall or wind speed can trigger resources. But
having money ready does not always mean there
are rules and systems in place to quickly get it to
those who need it most. Many disaster financing
tools, such as Fondo de Desastres Naturales
(FONDEN) or CADENA, lack clear rules or legal
mandates to prioritize vulnerable groups. Some
countries stand out: Colombia’s disaster fund
uses poverty and inequality to guide spending,
Peru’s Fondo Para Intervenciones ante la

Ocurrencia de Desastres Naturales gives higher
priority to areas with high poverty rates, and
FONDEN focuses on infrastructure and housing
in areas of multidimensional poverty. A
background study for this report (Cárdenas and
Elizondo 2025) reviewed the legal frameworks of
the climate risk financing instruments that exist
in Latin America and the Caribbean and found
that 12 explicitly direct resources to poor
individuals, and seven more do so indirectly by
allowing the tools to cover the needs of those
groups or social sectors after the occurrence of
climate hazards (refer to table 3.4.1).

Established social protection systems build resilience and speed
recovery from realized climate hazards
A background paper for this report (Sugastti and Carter 2025) uses the World
Bank’s (2023) classification of social protection systems in LAC—classifying
countries as having nascent, emerging, or established social protection—to see
how well they handle climate hazards. Using data from the Emergency Events
Database and World Development Indicators for 1981–2023, the study looks at
how countries with different levels of social protection manage the impact of
similar materialized climate hazards (whose intensity is proxied by the rate of
people affected by them) on the poverty gap.12

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

Stronger social protection systems help countries resist better and bounce
back faster from climate hazards once they occur. In countries with nascent
social protection systems, poverty spikes sharply after they get hit by climate
hazards, peaking four to five years after the event and staying high even a
decade later. Countries with emerging systems do better: Poverty still rises,
but the increase is smaller, peaking around year two, and recovery is steadier.
Countries with established systems—such as those in Argentina, Brazil,
Chile, the Dominican Republic, and Uruguay—handle climate hazards best.
Thanks to strong post-climate-hazard support, they prevent poverty from
rising at all, even 10 years out. In short, the stronger the system, the better the
country weathers the storm—in terms of both impacts on the poverty gap and
recovery speed (refer to figure 3.12).

98

| Adapting to Adversity

Many countries still struggle to move from reactive, ad hoc support after
climate hazards occur to a more proactive, risk-informed approach (Tisei and
Ed 2024). A good social protection system does not just prevent increases in
poverty, it also helps people recover faster. This is because when families do not
have to sell off their assets just to get by, they can bounce back more quickly
after a climate hazard takes place. So, making social protection more responsive
to climate shocks can boost its impact and reduce long-term damage. The next
section dives into one way to do this.

Improving adaptive social protection: The poorest people as active
economic agents
Food aid and emergency cash help meet immediate needs, but they should
also reduce future climate risk. Cash transfers are key, but long-term
solutions matter, too. Social protection should not just stabilize incomes—it
should help people escape poverty. Programs that support investment, such
as insurance or asset building, lower vulnerability and promote growth
(Janzen, Carter, and Ikegami 2021). Beyond protection, social programs
should encourage investment; when people feel secure, they take calculated
risks that improve their long-term prospects. So instead of just offering a
temporary fix, smart social protection can break the poverty cycle and build
real resilience.
Asset-building programs are gaining ground in Latin America’s social
protection scene, offering better long-term benefits than traditional CCTs,
which mostly pay off later in life. Although CCTs such as Mexico’s Prospera
and Brazil’s Bolsa Família still lead the way, they mainly nudge investments
in education and health. Newer options—like livelihood grants and lumpsum transfers—boost income stability and help people build assets to
support upward mobility.
Paraguay pairs its CCT program, Tekoporã, with Tenonderã, which helps
people build sustainable livelihoods after the cash support ends (refer to
box 3.5). Peru does something similar: Haku Wiñay and Noa Jayatai
complement the Juntos CCT program to boost self-sufficiency, although
Peru leans more toward rural development, whereas Tenonderã focuses on
broader economic inclusion. Honduras also links its CCT program, Bono
Vida Mejor, with asset-building support. According to the World Bank’s
Atlas of Social Protection Indicators of Resilience and Equity, these are
some of the few government-led programs in the region using a structured
graduation model. Others are less formal—Colombia’s Familias en Acción
connects to Red Unidos, which offers limited economic inclusion support
but lacks an asset-building component. Brazil’s Progredir is only loosely tied
to Bolsa Família and does not include asset-building transfers. Bolivia and
Ecuador also run CCTs without strong graduation pathways.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

|

Box 3.5

Paraguay’s conditional cash transfer program, Tekoporã, and its asset-building
graduation program, Tenonderã
Tekoporã is Paraguay’s main conditional cash
transfer program, which had supported more
than 186,000 poor and near-poor households by
2024. Launched in 2005, it provides financial aid
to families with school-age kids, pregnant
women, people with disabilities, and Indigenous
peoples on the condition that they keep up with
school and health checkups. The goal is to boost
education and health care access while reducing
poverty, with support lasting up to six years.
To build on that, Tenonderã was rolled out in
2014 as an asset-building program to complement

Tekoporã. Based on BRAC’s graduation model—a
sequenced approach that combines consumption
support, asset transfers, and skills training to
help ultra-poor households achieve sustainable
livelihoods—it offers seed capital and business
training to Tekoporã beneficiaries who have been
in the program for at least three years to become
financially independent through farming,
microbusinesses, or small-scale trade. By 2024, it
is expected to serve 16,500 households. Together,
the two programs form a bridge from short-term
support to lasting self-sufficiency.

Source: Sugastti and Carter 2025.

STRONGER TOGETHER: THE POWER OF COMBINED
INSTRUMENTS AND SYSTEMS FOR CLIMATE ADAPTATION
This chapter has thus far described and analyzed key instruments and policies
to manage climate risks, stressing the need for both prevention and response—
because although risk can be reduced, most times it cannot be fully eliminated.
These strategies should happen together, not one after the other. Preventing
risk before it materializes is usually cheaper and doable, even for poorer
nations. Governments can help by offering forecasts and early warnings so
people can evacuate in time and stay safe. Protecting ecosystems and forests—
often home to poor communities—can also boost both adaptation and climate
mitigation. In LAC, many countries have incubated innovative index insurance
and climate risk financing mechanisms. Strong social protection systems can
soften the impact of climate hazards.
Governments and households respond to climate hazards with a mix of
prevention and reaction, but the level of investment and readiness varies widely
across countries. Table 3.5 gives a diagnostic snapshot, combining each
country’s climate risk profile with the strength of its adaptation systems. On the
risk side, the table captures three dimensions: exposure (the share of poor
people exposed to climate hazards). vulnerability (the likelihood of falling into
poverty after climate hazards occur), and overall climate risk impact (based on
the Global Climate Risk Index, where a lower index score indicates greater
historical losses). On the systems side, five adaptation policy instruments are
assessed: EWSs, protected areas, financial tools for climate risk retention and
risk transfer, critical infrastructure, and adaptive safety nets.

Note: Each cell in the table reflects a three-tier ordinal score (1 = low, 2 = medium, 3 = high). For the risk indicators—exposure, vulnerability,
and climate risk impact—green indicates low risk, yellow indicates moderate risk, and red indicates high risk. For the adaptation systems,
green signals strong performance or system readiness, yellow indicates moderate development, and red reflects weak or underdeveloped
systems. Scores are assigned using a consistent methodology. For example, EWSs are scored from 1 to 3 on the basis of global classifications,
from less than basic to advanced; adaptive safety nets are rated from nascent to established; and risk retention and transfer are based on the
number of financial instruments in place. Other indicators, such as protected areas, critical infrastructure systems, exposure, vulnerability, and
climate risk impact, are grouped into terciles based on their distribution across countries in the sample. Gray cells represent missing or
unavailable data. EWS = early warning systems; LAC = Latin America and the Caribbean.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

Instead of assigning countries into rigid typologies, this framework enables
country-specific assessments of risk and system readiness, showing where
populations are most exposed and where systems fall short. Each row in the
table reflects a layered analysis of exposure, vulnerability, and system strength.
This visual tool is not intended to rank countries but rather to provide a
snapshot to guide tailored strategies, helping countries determine the right
combination of prevention, insurance, and postdisaster support on the basis of
their risk profile. For instance, Costa Rica scores low on exposure and climate
risk impact while maintaining strong systems—particularly in early warning
and financial tools—pointing to a balanced approach. Honduras, by contrast,
faces high exposure and vulnerability alongside weak safety nets, critical
infrastructure, and financial instruments, underscoring the need to strengthen
several fronts simultaneously.
Ultimately, countries benefit the most from layered, well-coordinated
adaptation systems that can flexibly respond to different climate hazards while
protecting those who are most vulnerable. The traffic light scoring system is a
practical tool to guide national planning, set investment priorities, and build
resilience in ways tailored to each country’s specific challenges and capacities.
We do not always know how much countries invest in risk reduction, but we do
observe that some efforts—such as EWSs—fall short. Strong social protection
systems can soften the impact of climate hazards as they occur, but they are not
generalized in these countries; many lack climate risk financing and clear plans
for directing support to social sectors. An ounce of prevention is worth a pound
of cure; preventive measures save lives and cost less than cleaning up after
climate hazards strike, but many governments still underinvest, and people
often ignore warnings. Safe housing, land titles, public services, and critical
infrastructure help communities prepare, but people stay in risky areas for jobs
or basic services. At the same time, human actions—such as clearing hillsides,
blocking drains, and encroaching on wetlands—make things worse. In rural
areas, overgrazing, deforestation, and groundwater overuse increase climate
risks, especially for poor, overcrowded communities. Too often, governments
and global organizations step in only after climate hazards hit, offering relief,
insurance, or social support. These efforts can help, but only if they are
sustained, which is not always the case. Waiting for a crisis is risky: Safety nets
and insurance do not always kick in fast enough to protect the poorest
individuals. Ethically and economically, the case for prevention is clear—
reducing risk is the smartest way to protect both lives and livelihoods.
Why then have most countries not combined protective and reactive measures
to minimize future losses caused by climate hazards? Chapter 4 argues that this
bias comes from a mix of informational, political, and financial factors, with
political economy likely being the biggest hurdle. It then explores potential
solutions to overcome these challenges and underlines policies to enhance
these instruments.
