NOTES
1. The proposed framework is consistent with other existing frameworks used for
similar purposes, such as in the 2013 World Development Report; the Adaptation
Principles; the World Bank’s Global Practice Finance, Competitiveness, and
Investment’s layering approach; and the Triple Dividend of Resilience.
2. In LAC countries, urban settlements have grown by 65 percent, from 50,000 square
kilometers in 1985 to 84,000 square kilometers in 2015. In 2015, 5 percent of all
settlement areas—or 4,000 square kilometers—were in zones with high or very
high flood risk (Rentschler et al. 2023).
3. High income refers to high and upper-middle income; low income refers to low and
lower-middle income.
4. Based on data from Nirandjan et al. (2022).
5. Note that these estimates may suffer from endogeneity issues, such as, for example,
geography, historical settlement patterns, and land use regulations that affect both
infrastructure placement and flood vulnerability by city. Placement can also be
decided as a function of risk awareness (such as hospitals, schools, and roads),
which also depends on whether the city is affected by recurrent flooding.
6. Refer to https://data.worldbank.org/indicator/ER.LND.PTLD.ZS?locations=ZJ-1W.
7. An analysis of 34 countries found no negative effects on poverty, with potential
positive impacts tied to proximity to protected areas with tourism. Households
within 10 kilometers of such areas would have 17 percent higher wealth and
16 percent lower poverty compared with a similar household more than
10 kilometers from a protected area. Positive effects were also seen in children’s
health, particularly in less-restrictive protected areas. Studies evaluating the
effectiveness of parks or systems of parks also provide evidence for a neutral or
positive effect, at least in Latin America. In Costa Rica, the establishment of
protected areas reduced poverty, mainly through tourism rather than increased
forest cover or impacts on infrastructure. A study in Bolivia showed positive poverty
impacts (asset-based poverty and measures of unmet basic needs), and a study in the
Peruvian Amazon found reductions in deforestation but no clear poverty effects.
8. Despite subsidies, individualized index insurance still sees low uptake in the
region. Credit constraints, low awareness, and lack of trust in providers all play a
role. A key issue is basis risk: when payouts (such as those based on rainfall) do not
match actual losses (such as crop failure), leaving farmers exposed and insurers at
reputational risk. High startup costs also deter insurers, especially without clear
paths to scale. This limited adoption slows market growth and prevents broader
cost-sharing across a larger risk pool.
9. In 2006, despite having FONDEN, Mexico became the first government to
purchase CAT bonds, transferring part of its climate risk to international capital
markets. This allowed the country to shift the risk of infrequent but severe floods
or droughts to regional or global reinsurance markets, boosting its financial
resilience. CAT bonds work as follows: A special-purpose vehicle issues the bond,
and the proceeds are held in an escrow account. Bond holders are promised an
interest rate higher than that earned by the escrow account, but only if the event is
not triggered. If a qualifying climate hazard occurs, the funds are released to the
government, but investors get nothing. Although effective, issuing such bonds
involves high fixed costs, making them too expensive for smaller countries. These
governments may prefer reinsurance through regional pools or private companies.
This strategy requires sophisticated government analysis and negotiation to
implement effectively.
10. Using propensity score matching and a difference-in-differences approach, de la
Fuente and Giné (2025) find that Weather Index Insurance reduces moderate
poverty, with no significant preprogram trends from 1990 to 2000. Refer to de la
Fuente and Giné 2025.

Protecting Poor People from Climate Hazard Risks

11. Households could also receive advance notice of their coverage under the disaster
response fund, which may encourage investment in riskier, higher-yield crops.
12. The study uses a panel vector autoregressive model with country fixed effects.
This model captures the dynamic relationship between climate shocks (whose
intensity is proxied by the rate of people affected by them) and the poverty gap over
time. Country fixed effects account for time-invariant differences across countries,
such as geography, baseline economic structures, or long-standing institutional
factors, improving the accuracy of estimates. However, although the classification
of social protection systems is central to the analysis, differences in the impact of
climate shocks may also reflect other time-varying country characteristics not
directly controlled for in the model.

4

Improving Adaptation

INTRODUCTION
Chapter 3 looked at five key adaptation instruments—early warning systems,
critical urban infrastructure, land and ecosystem protection, insurance, and
safety nets—and how they help vulnerable communities deal with climate
hazards. Some countries have made strides, but none have fully rolled out all
five, and no single measure has been widely adopted across Latin America
and the Caribbean (LAC). This chapter dives into what it takes to get
people to act on warnings and build safer homes and what measures can
enhance the adoption of these instruments across governments to better
protect their citizens.
Greenstone and Jack (2015) identify four key factors that hinder the effectiveness
of climate response in developing countries: low-income levels, high adaptation
costs, political economy constraints, and market failures. We believe these
factors can be overcome by what we call the 4 Is (refer to figure 4.1):
1. Incentives: Individuals can be encouraged to adopt better protective
­measures against climate risks.
2. Information and technology: Data and innovations from governments,
multilateral organizations such as the World Bank, and the private sector can
influence decisions, allowing governments to build on existing knowledge.
3. Investments: Securing funding can make adaptation efforts both sustainable
and cost-effective.
4. Institutions: A mix of media (to inform), political parties (to compete for
votes), and technocrats (to evaluate the merits of alternative policies)
ensures effective adaptation and accountability at all levels.
Each factor is examined in turn.

111

112

| Adapting to Adversity

INCENTIVES
Chapter 1 revealed that in numerous countries and cities in the region, poor
individuals face disproportionate exposure to climate hazards. Chapter 2
further highlighted that more lives are lost in poor countries in the region, with
poor individuals suffering more in both poor and better-off countries. Why do
those who are poor take greater risks? Confidence in understanding behavior,
whether through psychology or economics, increases if one can explain these
differences.

What drives poor individuals to act against climate risk?
Evidence of taking greater risks, for instance, is consistent with multiple
explanations, one being flawed perception of risk, especially of rare events. So,
although people in the Dominican Republic or St. Lucia know they live in a
hurricane-prone area, they may underestimate the likelihood of a major storm
and so take inadequate precautions. But poor and rich people alike are exposed
to a one-in-1,000-year storm (in terms of probability, the 1,000-year storm has a

Improving Adaptation

0.001 percent chance of happening in any given year), so they may both think
they are safe for 999 years. Lack of wealth does not seem to hardwire poor
individuals to risk misperceptions more so than any other group.
Another common view is that poor individuals often favor immediate rewards over
future gains. This might mean skipping education to avoid its perceived burden or
entering into early marriage and frequent divorce, sacrificing stable relationships.
Such choices may reflect short-sightedness or a conscious preference for instant
gratification—what economists call a high discount rate. Some argue that poor
people inherently possess a higher discount rate, so this mindset explains why they
would prefer present consumption over potential future benefits.1
By extension, poor individuals might ignore warnings or skip precautions
against climate hazards, such as moving away from flood-prone areas,
because these actions come with immediate costs. For example, evacuating
could mean losing an immediate source of income for a threat that might
never happen. Similarly, investing in floodproofing homes might seem
pointless and incur a high opportunity cost if the benefits are long term or
uncertain. But is this really the whole story?
Living in a shanty town may often not be about poor long-term planning; it is a
practical choice to stay close to work, even if that work means scavenging as a
ragpicker. When people have little now and expect little later, sacrificing the
present for the future may not make sense. Some economists argue that blaming
high discount rates or differences in tastes to explain poor people’s decisions
just masks a lack of understanding (Stigler and Becker 1977)—why those
preferences exist is the real question. In fact, many poor individuals make
enormous long-term sacrifices: leaving rural homes for harsh urban conditions
so they have money to send home and give their children more food and better
education. Often, people settling in slums in Latin American cities reflects
deeper systemic issues, such as the lack of public transport or regulations that
block affordable housing alternatives.
Galiani et al. (2017) compare poor slum dwellers living near their workplace
with other poor households in El Salvador, Mexico, and Uruguay who live
farther away but have better access to public services. They find that slum
residents earn significantly more, despite having a comparable level of
education and labor-market participation rates. As chapter 3 highlighted,
though, many of these slums—especially those on riverbanks or hilltops—are
highly vulnerable to floods because of poor infrastructure.
This raises a policy dilemma: Should slum dwellers be relocated to safer areas
that are farther from jobs? Chapter 3 already explored this trade-off. The key
point here is that overexposure to climate hazards or limited preventive action
often stems from rational choices, not just misjudged risks or high discount
rates. People may simply be responding to incentives as they are.
Another explanation uses conventional economic reasoning, pointing to
information gaps and market failures. For instance, storm-resistant homes may

|

113

114

| Adapting to Adversity

cost only a bit more, but buyers or renters often cannot tell how resilient a
home really is—potentially creating an Akerlof-type market-for-lemons
problem.2 Even when retrofitting makes sense, the lack of visibility in resale or
rental markets can discourage investment. Reputation systems or builder
guarantees could help, but in practice they often fall short, leading to fewer
precautions than warranted.
Poor individuals may not invest in climate prevention because of insecure
property rights. If they might get evicted at any time, why spend money fixing up
a place they do not own? The constant threat of eviction does not just stop home
upgrades, it weakens community bonds and discourages collective actions, such
as building shared flood defenses. By contrast, when people have secure property
or tenancy rights, everything changes. They are more likely to improve their
homes (or leave, in the case of a warning) and neighborhoods without the fear of
losing it all. The link to climate resilience may be indirect, because, for instance,
better housing materials tend to hold up better during storms. In Ecuador,
stronger property rights sparked more community projects (Lanjouw and Levy
2022). In Peru, giving slum dwellers legal ownership led to more self-funded
home upgrades and even boosted work participation (Field 2007). A study in Peru
found a 68 percent increase in housing investments after land titling, although
floodplain areas were left out, so the impact on those most vulnerable to floods is
not fully clear (Field 2005). Similarly, in Argentina, land titling in shantytowns
encouraged families to invest in housing (Galiani and Schargrodsky 2010).
There is less research on how property rights tie into climate risk reduction, but
the signs are promising—especially studies at the community level. In Rio de
Janeiro’s favelas, people with secure tenure were more likely to put in things such
as retaining walls to prevent landslides (Perlman 2010). In the Dominican
Republic, homeowners were more proactive than renters, building walls or digging
ditches around their homes to steer water away (World Bank 2023a). Similar
patterns showed up in Medellín, where tenure security helped drive communityled efforts on landslide prevention (Aristizabál Giraldo et al. 2020). And in Lima’s
informal settlements, titled households were more likely to participate in
community preparedness efforts against climate hazards (Chambers 2005).

Nudging, not mandating, individual decisions to
avoid risky locations
When families build shanties in risky locations, it is usually a revealed
preference—because they run out of options. Between 2008 and 2022, about
30 million people in LAC were displaced by storms and floods, according to the
Internal Displacement Monitoring Centre (https://www.internal-displacement​
.org/database/displacement-data/), although, surprisingly, not much movement
happens in cities in poorer countries in LAC, even after repeated flooding
(refer to chapter 3, “Resilient cities” section).3 Some may end up in floodprone areas. Giving them safer land and secure tenure would help, but it is not
just about location. People also need roads and buses to get to work. If the

Improving Adaptation

government handles that, people can build their own homes—even if they are
simple ones—on land that is less likely to be wiped out.
Connectivity is key to helping informal settlements adapt and thrive. Linking
slums to the formal city opens up access to jobs and boosts emergency response.
In Buenos Aires, where nearby areas face deeper poverty, governments—backed
by national and international support—have launched major slum upgrades.
Since 2017, places like Barrio 31 have seen big investments in resilience: better
infrastructure, easier access, and greener buildings (Bosi and Puig 2017). This
also lifts local economies and builds skills. In Medellín, the Metrocable system
is a good example of a transport system that has managed to slash travel time
from hillside slums to the city center to just seven minutes, making it easier for
people to get around and get ahead (Davila 2021).
Alternatively, removing public incentives for settling in hazard-prone areas,
such as infrastructure investments, can deter development in risky zones. In
the United States, the Coastal Barrier Resources Act of 1982 removed federal
support for flood protection, disaster assistance, and subsidized flood insurance
in risk-designated areas along the Atlantic and Gulf coasts, called the Coastal
Barrier Resources System. This led to an 85 percent drop in development
density in these areas, but a 20 percent increase in development in nearby
regions (Druckenmiller et al. 2023).
Another way to steer people away from high-risk areas is to repurpose the land.
In Medellín, the Jardín Circunvalar turned more than 65 hectares of damaged
hillside into a green space with native trees (C40 Cities 2016). It helps prevent
floods and landslides in the neighborhoods below and naturally limits further
building uphill. The park includes trails, bike lanes, and spots for growing
native orchids. Nearby, the government has built affordable housing and
involved locals in designing and maintaining the park—creating jobs, learning
opportunities, and a safer, greener space for everyone. Incidentally, this project
forms part of a broader initiative on green corridors across the city, which have
reduced temperatures (de Andrade 2023).
In sum, governments can encourage residents to relocate to safer locations
(or signal their commitment to preventive adaptation) by building roads,
restoring parks, and cutting back on services in hazardous locations.
Still, even with all the nudges, some people may still decide to stay where they
live out of necessity or conviction. Some argue that people assume the
government will step in with flood protection if they build in risky areas
(Kocormik-Mina et al. 2020). So, construction on floodplains continues.
Without clear rules, people build, and the government responds with levees
and dams—shifting the cost onto those living in safer areas. That is one reason
why these flood-prone areas end up being overcrowded (Rentschler et al.
2023), especially with people who cannot afford to move. There is a real need
for solid evidence in the region to see whether public investments in resilient

|

115

116

| Adapting to Adversity

infrastructure unintentionally encourage riskier behavior—a crowding-out
effect from climate proofing. If this is the case, governments should think of
new policies to keep that from happening, as discussed next.

Building codes or zoning?
Building codes can boost a community’s resilience to severe weather.
Sometimes international experts push for stricter codes as part of aid, but
supporters of tough codes often overlook two things: (1) property owners have
their own incentive to protect their assets and (2) they assume that people will
always follow government rules. To really improve, countries need better
overall building practices—including skilled designers, material testing, proper
equipment, and trained workers—and codes are just the start.
It is also easier to justify safety measures when they protect others, not just the
individual (for example, speed limits are easier to enforce than seat belt or
helmet laws). Some rules, like the smoking ban, can encourage better behavior,
and seat belt use has increased over time, partly because of their ubiquity. But
mandatory measures can also lead to corruption. For instance, the uneven
enforcement of land use rules resulting from corruption, often under the guise
of adaptation, has allowed wealthier neighborhoods to stay safe (because they
can protect themselves) while poorer informal communities are displaced or
relocated (D’Aoust, de la Fuente, and Lozano Gracia 2025).
Therefore, it is probably better to mandate things everyone can observe, such as
zoning and exclusion zones, rather than relying on inspections. Exclusion zones
stay the same regardless of the building technology, and allowing exceptions for
builders who claim their designs are safe could open the door to corruption
because of lack of transparency. It is likely more effective to regulate (1) things
that are easy to observe and (2) areas where technology is not changing quickly.
A key issue is that informal settlements often emerge in areas outside zoning
regulations, where residents de facto do not follow the rules. To ensure
compliance with land use or zoning, the first step is that people need legal
ownership of the land they have settled on.
Ultimately, transparent housing decisions and urban planning through zoning
and land use restrictions depend on titling and accurate, timely data.
Governments, businesses, and households need the right resources and
incentives to evaluate the costs and benefits of climate-smart adaptation
decisions such as zoning. These incentives only work if markets provide
reliable data on an investment’s future value and governments have reliable
information on climate risks and assets. Without this information, governments
avoid investing in infrastructure or land rights in flood-prone areas. But with
better information, they can better justify their zoning decisions, leading to
smarter urban planning in low-risk areas, such as more green spaces, better
drainage, and stricter zoning enforcement.

Improving Adaptation

In Porto Alegre, Brazil, for instance, the World Bank helped the municipal
government launch the Green, Resilient, and Inclusive Regeneration Project to
sustainably revitalize the city’s center with climate-smart solutions (World Bank
2023a). A major focus was reducing flood risks, especially in high-risk areas, by
upgrading drainage infrastructure. The project included hydrological studies,
which then informed ways to boost water permeability, along with expanding
drainage, improving microdrainage, and replacing outdated water systems.
In El Progreso, Honduras, repeated flooding and unmanaged growth had long
increased the city’s exposure to climate risks. With World Bank support, the
municipality conducted land use assessments and began integrating climate
risk data into urban planning. These efforts have helped local authorities justify
zoning restrictions and strengthen planning systems, paving the way for more
resilient and sustainable urban development (Bloch et al. 2014).
Up to this point, we have shown that market failures have held back some
adaptation efforts in the region, but that removing these barriers can help
people make smarter choices with respect to climate risks. Policies that address
information gaps, negative externalities, and principal-agent issues help people
make informed decisions. Because private providers do not necessarily have the
incentive to share hazard data, government action is essential. Clear property
rights strengthen resilience in poor urban areas. Public transport boosts
mobility, reduces congestion, and cuts pollution, benefiting everyone. Building
roads, restoring parks, and cutting back on services in hazardous locations
altogether can nudge people to reconsider their location, especially if their lives
are at risk. People can protect their homes with timely warnings. We now turn
to another key enabler of effective adaptation.

INFORMATION AND TECHNOLOGY
Easy access to information is key to better adaptation. Chapter 3 highlights how
accurate weather and hazard forecasts and their timely dissemination can save
lives. When people know the risks of living in flood- or storm-prone areas, they
take more precautions. Governments can also negotiate better insurance rates
by having full data on hazard exposure and asset values.

Improving weather and hazard forecasting: The physics and economics
Climate hazards arise from a variety of causes and unfold at different speeds.
Forecasting ability is increasing, although unevenly across hazards; the basic
physics of climate hazards is not new, but the technology to measure, monitor, and
predict is increasing by leaps and bounds. Weather systems can be predicted far
better now than 30 years ago, but the timing of some risks remains unpredictable.
Accurate forecasts are key. Thanks to better science and technology—such as
sharper satellite observations, cheaper instruments that send data instantly,
and stronger computing power—predictions have come a long way. In 2015,

|

117

118

| Adapting to Adversity

a seven-day forecast was already about as reliable as a three-day one was in 1985
(Duggan 2015). Right now, most hazards can be predicted with enough lead time.
Still, not all hazards are equally easy to predict, and climate change is making
things even trickier with more unpredictable weather. Take hurricanes:
Forecasts have gotten much better at tracking intensity, but surprises still
happen. Figure 4.2b shows the difficulty in predicting where hurricanes will
hit. In early July 2024, as Hurricane Beryl tore through the Caribbean,
European and US models (refer to blue lines in figure 4.2) pointed to landfall in
Mexico. Instead, four days later, Beryl slammed into Texas, flooding roads,
killing at least 36, and knocking out power for millions.
Better forecasts need solid data and powerful models, and both take resources.
The more accurate the data, the better the forecast. Data come from ground
sensors (thermometers, barometers) and satellites, both of which are costly to
launch and maintain. Models are expensive to develop, although cheaper to
operate once set up. Extra information helps too—such as combining rainfall
forecasts with watershed maps to predict floods or layering heat alerts with
data on housing and age (for example, how many old people live in homes
without air conditioners?) and commuting (how much more smog is likely?) to
pinpoint who is most at risk. Artificial intelligence (AI) weather forecasting is
another promising feature, in which a growing number of smart machines are
anticipating future global weather patterns with new speed and accuracy. A
research team at Google DeepMind used its AI tool GraphCast on a relatively
modest computer to predict Hurricane Beryl’s path—and it accurately forecast
the Texas landfall. In figure 4.2, the blue line shows GraphCast’s forecast
closely tracking the orange line of Beryl’s observed path. Notably, the model
relied solely on its prior knowledge of Earth’s atmosphere.

Improving Adaptation

Private sector involvement in climate forecasting could lower costs, but
competing data, rival forecasts, and diverse ownership create challenges. Some
underfunded agencies sell forecasts, with mixed results, whereas private
companies use weather data for airplane routes, timing of farming, and
commodities trading. Firms such as SpaceX launch satellites, startups use
drones to gather innovative data, and AI is reshaping weather
prediction algorithms. However, profit motives can lead to monopolies
and restricted access. Weather and hazard forecasts are nonrival,
but hazard warnings must remain freely available for public safety. If private
firms control key data, national weather agencies—even in wealthy countries—
could lose access, causing major public welfare losses. Each country must
ensure inclusive, accessible forecasting. Although private firms may provide
forecasts because they are generally good corporate citizens, the issue must be
carefully thought through in each country to ensure inclusiveness.
The problem could worsen if raw forecast data are not shared, particularly
across countries facing common climate risks. The National Oceanic and
Atmospheric Administration shares weather forecasts and data with
governments outside the United States through international collaborations.
But what about private companies? Should international treaties require private
companies to share data, too? The World Meteorological Organization sets
guidelines, but it has no enforcement power or penalties for noncompliance.
What can governments do to ensure full early warning system coverage in LAC?
Governments can enhance data quality and access by fostering public-private
partnerships. With free, open-source tools like PostGIS, Geoserver, and the
GeoNode.org project, collecting and sharing data are easier than ever. Public-private
partnerships highlight this potential; for example, CrisisReady (https://www​
.crisisready.io/) collaborates with Harvard, Direct Relief, tech firms, and
governments to integrate data-driven decisions into climate risk planning.
Another option is to pool resources and expertise across countries, as has been
done in the Caribbean. With shared regional risks, the Caribbean Disaster
Emergency Management Agency created the Regional Early Warning Systems
Consortium to improve data sharing and system interoperability. This both
strengthens national capacities and highlights best practices. The system brings
together institutions focused on specific risks—climate research centers, health
agencies, meteorological services, and coastal hazard groups—along with
disaster risk management and telecom agencies. Its goals are to standardize
alerts, align key messages, and promote new technologies to make early
warnings more effective across the region (World Bank 2023b). Overreliance on
the systems and expertise of one country should, however, be avoided.
The World Bank could spearhead a regional push for granular data
collection, building on past efforts such as the open-source initiative
called Central American Probabilistic Risk Assessment (CAPRA).
Expanding and replicating CAPRA could enhance risk assessment across
the region.4

|

119

120

| Adapting to Adversity

Warnings only work if people trust them
No forecast is perfect—hurricane paths, speed, and impact always carry
uncertainty. So repeated false alarms can lead to complacency. Accurate
forecasts help officials plan evacuations and save lives—if there is enough lead
time. But trust is just as crucial; people are more likely to evacuate when they
believe in their leaders.
Research consistently shows that trust drives compliance. Dunn and Laterzo
(2021) found that trust in leaders and institutions shaped COVID-19 compliance
in Brazil and Mexico. Likewise, Levitt et al. (2019) link political trust and
corruption perceptions to adherence to disaster-related building codes.
Although rigorous empirical evidence on trust and evacuation is limited,
insights from these studies suggest that political trust may be crucial for
effective evacuations.
However, not everyone trusts their leaders, and that makes it hard to get people
to follow warnings. In LAC, trust in government is generally low. Many
countries struggle to deliver basic services such as health care, education, or
pensions, and high crime—especially in places such as Honduras and
República Bolivariana de Venezuela—adds to public frustration. Corruption
and inequality only make things worse (Bargsted, Somma, and Castillo
2017). Latinobarómetro’s 2023 survey backs this up: Most people across
the region do not trust Congress or political parties (https://www​
.latinobarometro.org/latinobarometro-2023). Uruguay stands out with
relatively higher trust levels, but in most countries, more than 80 percent
have little or no faith in political parties (Arias, Kennard, and Verley 2024).
Furthermore, trust is not just low but can be fragile, and what works today
may not hold tomorrow if political dynamics shift, which makes it more
complicated.
If people overcome their mistrust and choose to evacuate, there still needs to
be a solid information system for response to climate impacts—one that
coordinates shelters, aid, police to manage traffic control, and security. As
simple as it may sound, many countries do not have such systems, so when
climate hazards hit, people flee in confusion. Hurricane Otis is a case in
point: It caught forecasters off guard, looting followed, and many people
might now ignore future evacuation orders. The solution is a clear system to
declare (1) the emergency, (2) what areas have been affected, and (3) what
predesignated shelters to go to—something many countries are still missing.
Although such systems cannot eliminate all risks, such as looting, they are
critical for reducing confusion and improving public trust during
emergencies. So when good forecasts meet effective government outreach to
vulnerable groups, the impacts of climate risk can be mitigated (refer to
box 4.1).

Improving Adaptation

|

Box 4.1

Warning and trust to reduce heat-related mortality
In India, the Ahmedabad Heat Action Plan was
launched after a deadly heat wave in 2010, and it
has since become a model for other cities facing
rising temperatures. The plan combines early
warning systems, public awareness campaigns,
and health system preparedness and features a
color-coded alert system (yellow, orange, red)
linked to increasingly proactive measures, such
as targeted outreach, cooling strategies, and
emergency response. A study by Hess et al.
(2018) found that implementation of the Heat

Action Plan was associated with a significant
reduction in all-cause summertime mortality,
with the largest declines occurring on the
hottest days. The plan’s success is attributed to
its integration of climate forecasts with local
government action, as well as its emphasis on
reaching vulnerable groups. Ahmedabad’s
experience suggests that well-designed heat
action plans can meaningfully protect poor and
vulnerable populations in tropical urban centers
as climate risks intensify.

Raising awareness in low-income communities—and giving them the right
tools—also helps them take warnings seriously and reduce risks. But getting
that information to them is not always easy. Just knowing about flood risks or
getting timely alerts can make people more prepared. Community programs
such as Jamaica’s Community Disaster Preparedness Program have helped
locals understand risks and act on them. Campaigns such as Prepárate Perú
teach people about the implications of climate change and encourage them to
take steps to stay safe.

Accurate information to set an insurance premium
Timely, accurate price information on insurance premiums helps individuals
and governments make better adaptation decisions. When premiums reflect
true risk and damage costs, they guide people on whether to buy coverage. But
balance is key: If premiums are set too low, they can encourage
overdevelopment in risky areas and weaken prevention efforts. If they are set
too high, they may discourage people from getting insured altogether.
Setting the right insurance premiums is no easy task; one has to estimate both
how likely a loss is and how big it could be. The damage also needs to be easy
to prove (death, not pain) and not be made worse by having insurance (that is,
moral hazard). If premiums match the true individual risk, things work fine.
But when insurers rely on broad risk factors rather than true individual risk
indicators, high-risk individuals are more likely to purchase coverage, and
lower-risk individuals opt out, which then drives up premiums overall. As
markets grow and competition kicks in, premiums can get more accurate,

121

122

| Adapting to Adversity

nudging people to take more precautions. However, as premiums rise, poorer
households, regardless of their actual risk level, may be priced out of the
market entirely, raising important distributional concerns alongside efficiency
losses. In the extreme cases, high costs for monitoring and administration can
make some risks too expensive to insure for everyone—especially rare ones or
those tied to hard-to-track behavior, such as fires: They are natural, but
farmers can worsen their impact by overgrazing, which degrades soil and
reduces its ability to retain moisture, thus making crop insurance more
difficult to price.
Even pricing index insurance is challenging, mainly because of basis risk—
the mismatch between actual losses and payouts. Sure, it avoids costly
inspections and pays out fast, but its Achilles heel is that it can miss real
losses or pay when there is no damage. False negatives (no payouts despite
losses) and false positives (payouts without losses) weaken the value of
insurance, with false positives straining public trust and resources.
Parametric insurance depends on clear, reliable triggers—such as rainfall or
temperature—that closely match actual damage. The key is keeping basis
risk low so payouts line up with reality. There are two types of basis risk:
design-based (when the index poorly predicts average losses in an area) and
zonal (when individual losses differ from the area’s average). Improving
remote sensing, crop models, and real-time data collection can enhance
index accuracy and reduce basis risk.
Advancements in remote sensing have made loss prediction more accurate,
helping to reduce design-based basis risk (Benami et al. 2021). Yet,
insurance zones remain underdeveloped, limiting their potential (Benami
and Carter 2021). Combining satellite data with crop models can improve
yield and loss predictions, making insurance more effective for farmers.
Ground truthing with local data, auditing mechanisms, and historical
weather records further refines accuracy, and digital tools such as mobile
platforms enhance real-time data collection, ensuring index triggers better
match actual losses.
Of course, with enough subsidies, almost any insurance program can stay
afloat—even when basis risk is high. A 2020 survey found that about 80 percent
of insurance programs surveyed were subsidized (Kramer et al. 2022).
Subsidies make insurance more affordable, especially when poor data drives
premiums up. Subsidies can jump-start private markets and boost demand, but
they also have drawbacks. If mistargeted, they may favor wealthier farmers or
encourage risky practices, such as planting high-risk crops on fragile land. In
the long run, careful cost-benefit analysis (CBA) and stronger evidence on how
subsidized programs actually perform are essential to better guide future policy
choices (Kramer et al. 2022).
A solid data system is crucial to overcome basis risk—especially for
governments backing insurance programs. Limited or patchy data from rain

Improving Adaptation

|

gauges or yield records make it tough to price premiums right. When insurers
are not sure about the real risks, they play it safe and hike premiums to cover
the unknowns. That is a big problem in places that need agriculture insurance
the most because high costs push farmers away, slowing down the growth of
sustainable insurance markets.
Another way to tackle basis risk is through public-private reinsurance
partnerships, where a public reinsurance facility helps absorb some of the
uncertainty that private insurers hesitate to take on (Carter 2013). This
could lead to fairer premium pricing, making insurance more accessible in
underserved areas. By reducing reliance on worst-case pricing, such a
partnership could lower premiums without subsidies, fostering sustainable
insurance markets. Over time, as better data become available, the public
sector could gradually step back, allowing the market to function
independently.
Finally, support from institutions such as the World Bank in developing
platforms such as CAPRA can help governments better understand their risk
portfolios, allowing them to negotiate fairer premiums and policy terms (refer
to box 4.2).

Box 4.2

Public asset databases and prototype risk models in LAC
Some countries in LAC have over the years
developed data systems to help governments
make better-informed decisions to retain or
transfer their climate risk. Mexico has set a
regional benchmark in managing climate risk.
Using its own geospatial databases of public
asset exposure and its own probabilistic loss
estimation catastrophe risk model tool called
R-FONDEN., it defines risk levels and decides
how much to transfer through two main tools:
all-risk catastrophe insurance (contracted since
2011) and catastrophe bonds for earthquakes and
hurricanes (contracted since 2009). The risk
levels obtained from R-FONDEN have also
helped to set the retention amounts managed
through the Fondo de Desastres Naturales.
Colombia, while making progress in mapping
public assets and climate hazards, still lacks a
national risk model and instead relies on
commercial models or risk profile estimates

derived from World Bank models. This
information has mostly guided the calibration of
its disaster fund and catastrophe-deferred
drawdown options, not active risk transfer, apart
from a one-time earthquake catastrophe bond
acquired under the Pacific Alliance Framework
around 2018, which was not renewed. Several
Central American countries—Guatemala,
Honduras, Nicaragua, and Panama—regularly
transfer risk through the Caribbean Catastrophe
Risk Insurance Facility (CCRIF) for hurricanes,
earthquakes, and heavy rain. Most rely on
CCRIF data and World Bank models, but
Nicaragua uniquely uses its own information
generated by Instituto Nicaragüense de Estudios
Territoriales. This information is mainly about
asset exposure, but it also includes threat
analysis, which has helped the Ministry of
Finance in the calibration of the triggers
proposed by CCRIF.

Informing voters and relief beneficiaries
A well-informed electorate can prompt a more responsive government during
climate emergencies. Enhanced access to information among victims, coupled
with an active mass media disseminating and coordinating information, can
enhance government accountability in relief spending. Although the precise
methods for disseminating information to influence politicians’ actions may
vary, certain commonalities exist. For instance, the public can be informed
about the performance of services in their community compared with that in
others, identification of responsibility, and whether policy changes have
enhanced services.
Transparency and citizen feedback can significantly improve responses to crises,
including those caused by climate hazards. After Chile’s 2010 earthquake, open
reporting on disaster aid let people track government spending and boosted
accountability. In Colombia, mobile platforms helped citizens share feedback on
climate efforts, strengthening trust and dialogue. Honduras used a transparent
tracking system called Sistema de Información sobre Donaciones (Information
System on Donations) after Tropical Storm Agatha to show how donations were
spent, keeping the process accountable and minimizing corruption.
Audits and improved records are another promising route for reducing corrupt
practices. Avis, Ferraz, and Finan (2018) study official corruption audits released
by the Brazilian government and find that public audits meaningfully reduced
subsequent corruption in the same municipality. Also, public information about
corruption significantly strengthened electoral accountability, with politicians
exposed as corrupt less likely to achieve reelection.
Another way to improve responses to climate hazards through better
information is making relief payments contingent on prespecified and public
thresholds to facilitate monitoring. Boudreau (2015) provides evidence that
Mexican leaders are more likely to declare a drought during election years but
that the use of prespecified parametric thresholds may discipline politicians
and mitigate election-year bias in declaring climate-led emergencies.
Unfortunately, many LAC countries still lack strong grievance and redress
mechanisms (GRMs), which are critical tools for keeping public officers
accountable to vulnerable groups in times of crisis. Some countries have no
formal GRMs at all or still rely on outdated systems such as appointment books
and Excel spreadsheets. Honduras briefly piloted GRMs, including a complaint
website for real-time reports during the 2020 Eta and Iota hurricanes under the
Bono Vida Mejor conditional cash transfer program. Going forward, investing
in modern GRMs backed by case management tools is key to boost
accountability and responsiveness to vulnerable populations.
Ultimately, even if the right incentives are identified and information is
available, change can be slow—or stalled—without the funding to back it up.
The next section looks at how governments can secure the resources they need
for adaptation.

Improving Adaptation

|

125

INVESTMENTS
Adaptation finance in LAC comes from many sources: governments, the private
sector, nongovernmental organizations, philanthropies, and community groups.
The biggest chunk is international public finance, delivered via grants, loans,
and equity. Funding in the region is heavily concentrated in Brazil, Colombia,
Costa Rica, and Mexico. From 2003 to 2023, multilateral climate funds
approved $5.7 billion for 614 projects in the region, with three major funds—the
Green Climate Fund, Clean Technology Fund, and Amazon Fund—making up
67 percent of that total (refer to box 4.3) (Climate Funds Update 2024).
According to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change,
LAC, for the period 2015–16, obtained 22 percent of climate financing from
multilateral climate funds.

Box 4.3

Climate adaptation financing sources in LAC
The Green Climate Fund (GCF) is the biggest
player in climate finance in the region, backing
37 projects in 13 countries with $2.42 billion
between 2003 and 2023. In 2023 alone, it invested
$43 million in Colombia and $33 million in
Bolivia. Other major sources include the Clean
Technology Fund ($718 million via the World
Bank) and Brazil’s Amazon Fund ($684 million in
grants) (Climate Funds Update 2024). Bilateral
donors such as Germany and the United Kingdom
also play a role. Since 2015, GCF has mobilized
more than $40 billion (including cofinancing) for
climate projects in more than 100 countries.
The Global Environment Facility (GEF), active
since 1991, offers grants and low-interest funds to
help developing countries tackle big
environmental issues. For Latin American
countries, GEF approved $623.8 million across
144 projects between 2003 and 2023 (Climate
Funds Update 2024). These projects cover climate
action, biodiversity, forests, land use, and water
protection. GEF also teams up with governments,

nongovernmental organizations, and the private
sector to make sure efforts are sustainable.
Its support has enabled countries to adopt
solutions they could not afford alone while
building local skills and long-term impact.
At the 2023 United Nations Climate Change
Conference, held in the United Arab Emirates,
countries reached a landmark agreement to create
a loss and damage fund—the first of its kind—to
support developing nations most vulnerable to the
impacts of climate hazards. For the first time,
there will also be a concrete timeline to develop
metrics for tracking and measuring adaptation
progress. The Adaptation Fund, established under
the United Nations Framework Convention on
Climate Change, has also played a significant role
in supporting climate adaptation in LAC. As of
2024, it has approved 42 projects in the region,
totaling approximately $315 million in grants,
focusing on areas such as agriculture, water
management, and disaster risk reduction
(Adaptation Fund 2024).
