| Adapting to Adversity

Box 4.3 Climate adaptation financing sources in LAC (continued)

The last major financing tool for adaptation in
LAC is global green bonds, which rely heavily
on private sector involvement. In 2022, green
bond issuances from the region hit $5.3
billion—a 48 percent drop from the year before,
reflecting a broader global slowdown. Still, by
year-end, total green bond issuance in LAC
reached $42.4 billion, making up 4 percent of
the region’s bond market. Brazil led with $15.2
billion, mostly from nonfinancial corporations,
followed by Chile ($12.7 billion) and Mexico
($3.3 billion). Since 2020, countries such as

Bermuda, the British Virgin Islands, the
Dominican Republic, and Guatemala have also
joined the green bond market. Nonfinancial
corporations dominate this market (53 percent),
whereas sovereign issuers make up 19 percent.
Financial corporations more than doubled their
volume—from $2.1 billion to $4.5 billion—
boosting their share to 10 percent. Meanwhile,
development banks and local governments saw
their shares shrink to 11 percent and 1.5 percent,
respectively, over the same period (Climate
Bonds Initiative 2022).

Climate financing for adaptation in LAC: Too little, too slow
LAC needs around $34 billion (2023 prices) a year for climate adaptation
(UNEP 2025), but current funding is not even close. Wealthy countries
promised $100 billion a year by 2020 to help poorer nations tackle climate
change, but they have fallen short. Climate change, however—and the lack of
resources to adapt to its consequences—is not just a LAC problem. If countries
in the region cannot adapt to climate change, the ripple effects—such as crop
failures, hurricanes, and other climate hazards—can drive migration into
wealthier nations, too.5 Meanwhile, reports keep sounding the alarm that the
funding gap is growing, just as climate impacts worsen and rich countries are
pulling back on their aid for climate adaptation efforts (UNEP 2025).
A large fraction of adaptation finance to date has been handed out as loans,
which countries may struggle to repay given the conditions faced in relation to
their debt. It is notably unclear whether they can assume further commitments
as borrowers, given the magnitude of the resources required.
Critics also say that even the money that is delivered often misses the mark,
lacking focus and impact where it is most needed (Carty, Kowalzig, and
Zagema 2020). Some studies reveal that countries facing the highest climate
vulnerability tend to receive a smaller proportion of climate-specific overseas
development assistance in relation to their total overseas development
assistance, with Latin America being a notable case (Development
Initiatives 2023).
On top of this, adaptation still gets only a small slice of climate finance, with
just $0.8 billion going to it, compared with $3.6 billion for mitigation such as
forest projects (Climate Funds Update 2024). Institutions such as the World
Bank have taken note of this situation and the urgent need to shift priorities.

Improving Adaptation

According to the World Bank’s (2025) LAC Adaptation Action Plan, the
region’s adaptation share of climate cobenefits over the past five fiscal years
is 34 percent, which remains below the 50 percent target.
The bottom line is that climate finance is falling way short. There is more focus
on mitigation and not enough grants, and the money is often not going where it
is most needed. To truly boost climate resilience—especially on the ground—
smarter financing, more private sector engagement, and funding that actually
matches real adaptation needs are needed.

Opportunities to leverage additional climate funds: From
multilateral development banks to countries
Multilateral development banks (MDBs) such as the World Bank have long
been central to development finance. Looking ahead, they could play a big role
in funding the adaptation efforts to tackle climate hazards. Boosting their
capital, using innovative financial tools, and partnering with the private sector
can help them scale up lending and impact.
Starting with more capital, some argue it is time to simplify climate finance
because, in the current setup, funding is spread across many institutions and
hard to navigate. Streamlining it into fewer funds with shared rules would make
it easier for countries to access support. Pouring more money into MDBs could
also boost impact: By some estimates, every $1 in capital can unlock $4–$5 in
loans. That is much more bang for the buck than one-to-one funding models
such as the Green Climate Fund (refer to Ocampo 2025).
MDBs can also help scale up adaptation efforts in LAC by pulling in more funding
and attracting private investment through smart policies and innovative finance
tools. Blended finance, which mixes development bank funds with private
capital, helps lower risk and unlock investment in areas such as climate-smart
farming, water systems, and insurance. Resilience bonds, which link financial
returns to achieving climate goals, support projects such as green infrastructure
and flood defenses. Chapter 3 showed how, for instance, protecting green areas
and index insurance can help communities that need it most. Unlocking more
capital to finance those policies through clear rules and solid partnerships
between governments, MDBs, and investors would go a long way.
A great example of a resilience bond in Latin America is the one issued in 2023
by Fideicomisos Instituidos en Relación con la Agricultura (FIRA) (a trust fund
managed by the Central Bank of Mexico). It was the region’s first green
resilience bond and raised about $165 million in local currency. The money is
going toward climate-smart investments in Mexico’s agriculture sector—
helping farmers and rural supply chains better handle the impacts of climate
hazards. Working with partners such as the French Development Agency,
the Global Green Growth Institute, and UKPact Mexico, FIRA (2023)
identified 88 eligible investment types across food and rural sectors.

|

127

128

| Adapting to Adversity

Another creative way to fund adaptation is through debt-for-nature swaps or
Blue Bonds, which can be thought of as refinancing a mortgage, but for a
country’s debt. Basically, lenders cut countries some slack on their debt in
exchange for environmental commitments, such as cutting emissions or
protecting ecosystems. Banks still profit by reselling the debt. These swaps have
been around for a while (the World Bank wrote about them in Ochiolini 1990)
but only recently gained traction.
Take the case of Ecuador: In May 2023, it pulled off the biggest deal on Blue
Bonds yet, turning $1.6 billion of debt into a commitment to protect the
Galapagos. With help from Credit Suisse, Ecuador bought back its debt for
$644 million, saving around $1 billion over 17 years. In return, it pledged to
invest $18 million a year in conservation. The new Galapagos Bond, worth $656
million, is backed by the United States’ Development Finance Corporation
(Whiting 2024).
Beyond private investment, regional cooperation is key to scaling up adaptation.
By pooling resources and tapping into global funds, countries can boost their
resilience. The Caribbean Catastrophe Risk Insurance Facility (CCRIF),
highlighted in box 3.2 in chapter 3, shows how nations can team up to manage
climate risks and access quick postdisaster payouts. MDBs could continue
expanding this model—by subsidizing premiums for insurance, like CCRIF—to
give more Latin American and Caribbean countries better financial protection and
soften the blow of climate hazards that materialize. MDBs can also continue
strengthening the climate resilience of countries through tools such as
catastrophe-deferred drawdown options (see note 8 in chapter 3), giving
countries quick access to funding during crises. But it is important to strike a
balance; more lending should not come at the cost of long-term debt sustainability.
At the national level, governments can help to attract private money into
financing climate adaptation through stronger legal frameworks and adequate
regulations and facilitating better access to climate risk data. Other public
instruments such as guarantees, concessional loans, tax breaks, and blended
finance can also lower risk and make adaptation projects more attractive.
Governments can also offer technical support and help build a pipeline of
projects, making it easier for the private sector to step in and invest. For
instance, experiences outside the region offer insights for LAC countries to
involve the private sector in climate insurance (refer to box 4.4).
A good example of public-private collaboration is the Disaster Risk Insurance
and Finance in Central America Consortium, launched in 2022 by the World
Bank, the World Food Programme, and the Partnership for Central America.
The consortium shows how teaming up with local insurers and financial
institutions can boost financial climate resilience. It focuses on creating largescale climate-related agriculture insurance for smallholder farmers in El
Salvador, Guatemala, and Honduras. The initiative expands access to credit and
increases lending capacity by reducing the perceived risk of climate-induced
agricultural losses (World Bank 2024b).

Improving Adaptation

|

Box 4.4

Unlocking private sector participation in insurance schemes outside LAC
This report focuses on LAC, but there is a lot to
learn from other regions. A recent World Bank
study highlights how different players are
boosting resilience in ways LAC could adapt
(World Bank 2024a).
In Zambia, NWK Agri-Services runs a weather
index insurance scheme that now covers
52,000 farmers—without subsidies. It fronts the
insurance premiums and inputs, and farmers pay
back with their crops. When drought hit in
2015–16, payouts helped 23,000 farmers bounce
back while also strengthening business ties.
The Philippines demonstrates how a
comprehensive, layered approach to disaster
risk financing can scale resilience across sectors
while expanding private sector involvement.
Over the past decade, the government has
developed one of the most sophisticated disaster
risk finance and insurance strategies among
emerging economies. It combines national-level
contingent credit lines, catastrophe bonds,

parametric insurance, and subsidies for
agricultural insurance to protect households,
farmers, and critical public infrastructure. A
key element of this strategy was the expansion
of agricultural insurance for smallholder
farmers through the Philippine Crop Insurance
Corporation (PCIC), a public entity now
overseen by the Department of Finance. The
government injected more than $500 million in
premium subsidies to increase insurance uptake
among vulnerable farmers, with coverage
reaching more than 3 million farmers and
fisherfolk by 2021. Recognizing the need to
improve efficiency and sustainability, the
government has since begun reforming the
PCIC to adopt more market-based principles
and bring in private insurers. Under this
evolving public-private partnership, the
government is leveraging the PCIC’s
institutional capacity and the operational
strengths of private insurers to co-deliver
insurance products.

In Brazil, the International Finance Corporation (IFC) and Inter-American
Development Bank partnered with Itaú Unibanco on a $250 million bond
issuance to fund biodiversity restoration and inclusive finance. As the anchor
investor, IFC contributed up to $200 million to support projects such as the
REVERTE Program, which aims to restore 1 million hectares of degraded land
by 2030 and to scale up credit access for micro-, small, and medium enterprises
(IFC 2025).
A final point worth making is that national governments can build trust with
donors and attract more private investment in adaptation by boosting
transparency and accountability in climate funding. Aligning adaptation goals
with national plans and budgets helps countries tap into more funding—both
local and global—while ensuring long-term resilience. Strengthening national
adaptation plans and embedding these measures into development plans,
poverty reduction strategies, and sector budgets are critical for making the most
of available resources. The latest UNEP Global Climate Adaptation Gap Report
(UNEP 2025) shows that although some 172 countries have national adaptation
policies, strategies, or plans in place, many still lack reliable data, timelines, and

129

130

| Adapting to Adversity

cost estimates, limiting their impact. In Latin America, closing these gaps and
linking policies to broader climate and development strategies will better direct
resources where they are needed most. Although more funding for adaptation
is necessary, the next section examines how to do more with the resources
governments already have.

Maximizing adaptation resources: Revisiting CBA and trade-offs
CBA has proved a particularly valuable tool to evaluate the efficacy of
competing adaptation policies and investments. An investment is deemed
worthwhile when its benefits outweigh costs, and governments should pick the
option with the highest benefit-cost ratio (UNFCCC 2009; World Bank and UN
2010).6 The Global Commission on Adaptation (2019), for instance, shows big
payoffs—from 2:1 to up to 10:1 benefit-cost ratios—for things such as early
warning systems, critical infrastructure, dryland farming, and mangrove
protection.
In a region such as LAC facing increasing climate risks and constrained
financial resources, CBA provides a framework for allocating resources
effectively. However, there is a need to move beyond conventional CBAs to
account for trade-offs as well as broader welfare impacts that often arise with
these projects to ensure that adaptation investments are both effective and
equitable. Recent efforts and proposals move in this direction (refer to box 4.5).
Traditional CBAs miss out on bigger benefits related to human welfare
and ecosystem services. If one looks at physical capital only, the true value
of adaptation measures could be underestimated—CBAs should consider
beyond the conventional costs and benefits, adding lives saved and welfare
improvements. For instance, research in Nicaragua shows that bridges do
not just stop flash floods, they keep people working, improve market
access, spur farm investments, and bump up savings (Brooks and Donovan
2020). Cartwright et al. (2013) illustrate this approach by proposing an
expanded framework where CBAs go beyond gross domestic product—
tracking how many people benefit and how often and who is getting the
most out of it.
Putting a dollar value on things such as ecosystems or human life is difficult.
Markets only show value in exchange, not true worth, and ethical factors are
just as important as the numbers. Good economists know their tools have limits.
Valuing life is tricky and controversial, but ignoring it would mean valuing it at
zero. So, economists estimate by using the Value of Statistical Life (VSL), the
value a person attaches to their own life, which is based on how much extra pay
someone demands for a riskier job. VSL comes from revealed preferences (not
from surveys, which is an alternative but highly flawed technique) but results in
a wide range of values, in part because of data and technique limitations. Even
with these limits, VSL is still useful for CBA, as long as its assumptions and
ethical trade-offs are clearly understood and explicitly stated.

Improving Adaptation

Box 4.5

How to judge the welfare benefits of adaptation policies
Governments and development agencies have
launched a variety of policies intended to stabilize
livelihoods in the face of climate hazards and
promote economic resilience. However, absent a
reliable measure of resilience, it is hard to gauge
the efficacy of these policies and whether the
pursuit of resilience is in fact wise public policy.
Only at the latest United Nations Climate Change
Conference in 2023 was a roadmap to arrive at an
agreed-on set of metrics and tools to measure
resilience established.

One proposal for measuring resilience at the
household level draws on insights from the
economics literature on the responsiveness of
household consumption and income to shocks.
The idea is to measure resilience using standard
impact evaluation and matching techniques by
comparing a household’s actual income or
consumption after a shock with a counterfactual
showing what it would have been without the
shock (Alloush and Carter 2024). This approach,
however, requires panel data.

Some adaptation strategies shift risks over time, making it hard to weigh costs
and benefits. For example, seawalls might hold off rising seas for now, but if sea
levels rise faster than expected, seawalls could backfire. Levees are another
case—they prevent small floods but can cause major damage if they fail, or they
might protect some areas while redirecting floodwater to poorer communities
downstream. Research supports this. Bro, Moran, and Calvi (2018) examined
the impacts of the Belo Monte hydropower dam in Northern Brazil and found
that it slightly boosted cocoa farming but hurt rural jobs and food production
over a decade. Similarly, Duflo and Pande (2007) found that big dams in India
helped farms downstream but worsened poverty near the dams. Even shortterm solutions like dams or reservoirs can make long-term problems worse. For
example, in Chile, a megadrought since 2010 has led the government to overuse
groundwater, helping temporarily but draining reserves and threatening future
water security (Jódar et al. 2024; Taucare et al. 2024).
It is also important to think about trade-offs between generations: What helps
people today might hurt people in the future. Discount rates are often used to
weigh these trade-offs, but they come with built-in assumptions that can
oversimplify the complexities of climate adaptation. If not used carefully, they
can lead to bad decisions, especially in contexts in which socioeconomic
disparities are pronounced. Take again the example of embankments: They might
protect one area now but push flood risks onto others—or future generations.
These tools can be useful, but only if one is honest about their limits and applies
them thoughtfully, especially when equity is on the line (refer to box 4.6).
Compounding these challenges is the uncertainty regarding future climate
change. Despite significant advancements in climate science, it remains
impossible to accurately forecast the likelihood or magnitude of specific climate
events in particular locations within defined timeframes. This uncertainty
complicates efforts to calculate the benefits of adaptation investments in a
given sector or place, further underscoring the importance of flexible, longterm strategies that can accommodate a wide range of potential futures.

|

131

132

| Adapting to Adversity

Box 4.6

Fairness in adaptation investments: Weighing the impact on different groups
In a region with some of the worst income
inequality in the world, making sure the
financing of climate adaptation is equitable
should be a top priority. The most vulnerable
people are often hit hardest by climate hazards,
but these inequalities are often overlooked when
assessing climate risks and designing adaptation
interventions. Although economists have pointed
out these issues in theory, there is still not much
hard data—and even less of it actually gets used in
real planning (Josephson et al. 2024).
Adaptation projects can unintentionally deepen
inequality. Big infrastructure investments often
favor wealthier urban areas, and poorer or informal
communities get left behind or face even greater
risk. Traditional cost-benefit analyses (CBAs)
usually do not factor in these imbalances, which end
up benefiting high-value assets and wealthier
populations. This means some communities benefit
while others lose out, and for marginalized groups,
the upfront costs of adaptation can outweigh the
(discounted future) long-term gains.
One way to make CBAs fairer is by using
distributional weights—basically, adjusting the

value of benefits depending on who gets them.
The idea is simple: A dollar means a lot more to
someone with a low income than it does to
someone who is already well off. In theory, this
could shift focus toward projects that help the
poorest people; in practice, however,
distributional weights are rarely used because
people worry that they are arbitrary, not
transparent, and might distort incentives
(Watkiss and Cimato 2016). That said, there are
ways to bring fairness into CBAs. The
International Institute for Environment and
Development, for example, piloted a
stakeholder-driven approach to CBA in five
countries, including Bolivia (Lunduka, Bezabih,
and Chaudury 2012). They looked at urban
water supply projects, measuring not just
economic efficiency but also who wins and who
loses across different social groups. This
approach made trade-offs clearer, facilitated
dialogue, and helped in picking strategies that
better served those who are most vulnerable. It
showed that factoring in distributional impacts
can make adaptation efforts both fairer and
more effective.

Governments can maximize the impact of adaptation finance by clearly
showing the economic and social benefits of competing priorities, backed by
solid data and CBAs. Yet, effective adaptation for poor households can be
stifled by political failures. Protective infrastructure and zoning often benefit
wealthier households, creating an uneven distribution of resources. Policies
meant to address market failures can go unimplemented, progress too slowly,
or fail altogether. The reasons for this gap are not fully clear, and
understanding of them remains limited. Political institutions can also play a
significant role in hindering adaptation, and the next and final section dives
into these challenges.

INSTITUTIONS
In a seminal paper, Kahn (2005) finds that economic development and higherquality institutions—measured by democracy, inequality, ethnic fragmentation,

Improving Adaptation

and the World Bank’s good governance index—reduce deaths from natural
hazards across 73 countries. Good institutions allow land markets to function
properly, regulate development in hazardous locations, and provide
infrastructure that lowers climate risk while promoting growth and better
adaptation. But this prescription seems almost tautological: If a government is
poorly run, it cannot do a good job anyway. By contrast, countries with good
institutions would face low costs for developing effective institutions for
adaptation. The real question is this: How can a country make its political
institutions work to improve the climate adaptation of its poorest households
and communities?
Why poor people make certain choices about where they live or work is not
always fully understood. When it comes to adapting to climate risks, it might be
more effective to give them better information and suitable incentives. This
section argues that to be effective, a government’s own incentives also need to
align with supporting the adaptation efforts of poor individuals. In simple
terms, these incentives are shaped by political institutions, or the rules of the
game that influence behavior across political, social, and economic spheres
(North 1990).
A background paper for this report presents a political economy framework to
assess whether politicians’ incentives align with promoting climate adaptation
for poor individuals (Arias, Kennard, and Verley 2024). The core idea is that
politicians prioritize staying in power, shaping policies and budgets to secure
support. To do so, they must maintain a winning coalition—that is, the
minimum number of backers needed to stay in office. Leaders secure loyalty by
distributing public goods (for example, early warning systems, dams, clean
water) and private goods (benefits exclusive to their supporters). Ultimately,
their policy decisions create winners and losers.
The background paper by Arias, Kennard, and Verley (2024) highlights three
ways political institutions can shape the way politicians provide climate
adaptation policies—and how to fix them when needed.
1. Elections and accountability matter. Competitive elections are essential for
ensuring that governments respond to public needs, including climate
adaptation. However, although elections promote responsiveness, very
frequent election cycles can also create short time horizons, incentivizing
visible, short-term actions over longer-term, less-tangible investments such
as adaptation. The goal is to balance electoral accountability with space for
long-term planning.
2. Clientelism undermines adaptation. When politicians trade favors (exchange of
goods or services) for votes, long-term climate adaptation suffers because
politicians often favor short-term handouts over long-term adaptation. In
clientelistic democracies, power lies with local brokers who mobilize votes
through handouts. As a result, the poorest individuals, those with the least
influence and more needs, lose out. Giving them real electoral influence helps,
but this does not automatically imply that local communities always know best.

|

133

134

| Adapting to Adversity

3. Access to information empowers citizens. Educated, informed voters and
communities can hold leaders accountable and demand better climate action.
Because political incentives sharpen in election years, weak adaptation for poor
individuals is ultimately a political failure, not just a technical or financial one.
The next sections test these ideas across different policy areas and how to
overcome them.

Resilience for urban poor people
Clientelistic practices are common across Latin American countries. Surveys
show vote buying is widespread. González-Ocantos and Oliveros (2019)
analyzed the 2014 round of the Latin American Public Opinion Project’s
AmericasBarometer data and found self-reported clientelism in every country,
although rates varied. About half reported 0–10 percent direct experience; most
of the rest reported 10–20 percent; and the Dominican Republic was highest at
24.4 percent. Observed clientelism rates were even higher, topping 30 percent
in the Dominican Republic and more than 20 percent in Brazil, Haiti,
Honduras, and Paraguay. Only nine countries had observed rates below
10 percent. True clientelism likely falls between direct and observed rates.
In cities, clientelist parties target poor individuals with selective benefits in
exchange for votes. This focus is not random but by design—immediate
handouts are more valuable to poorer voters who face job insecurity, short time
horizons, and limited access to resources. In Latin America, clientelism is
especially common in urban slums. Studies show high rates of vote buying in
Argentina (Murillo, Oliveros, and Zarazaga 2021); Brusco, Nazareno, and Stokes
(2004) find that almost half (44 percent) of respondents to a survey after the
2001 national legislative elections reported receiving food, clothing, medicine,
and construction materials in exchange for political support. This can weaken
essential services, including those needed for climate resilience.
Clientelism hinders adaptation: Many Latin American cities have widespread
unregulated housing, with settlements vulnerable to floods and landslides
(Auerbach et al. 2018). When residents do not officially own their homes, they
rely on politicians for basic services and eviction protection, making them easy
targets for political favors (Larreguy, Marshall, and Trucco 2015). In other
words, their informality makes service provision highly discretionary and
attractive for clientelism, further complicating resilience efforts. For instance,
Auerbach (2016) shows that in Indian slums, areas with stronger political party
networks get better infrastructure and services. These areas are not necessarily
those most exposed to climate hazards.
Another way through which clientelism affects urban adaptation is by shaping
how building codes are enforced—or ignored. When social programs fall short as
political tools, leaders often turn to forbearance—strategic nonenforcement of
laws affecting poor people (Holland 2017). This is again especially common in
informal settlements, where residents lack property rights and face the threat of

Improving Adaptation

eviction, making them vulnerable to political pressure. Holland (2017) documents
widespread forbearance-as-clientelism in Latin America’s urban slums. In the
context of climate adaptation, this is especially risky, because weak enforcement of
building and safety codes undermines climate risk prevention (Levitt et al. 2019).
Corruption can also hurt urban adaptation by making people less willing to follow
costly building codes. Levitt et al. (2019) analyzed the 2014 AmericasBarometer
data and found that when people perceive high corruption, they are less likely to
invest in safe construction. Interestingly and consistent with the following
discussion regarding voter myopia, living in climate hazard prone areas did not
make much difference—people were still not more willing to comply.
A promising way to fight clientelism (and improve adaptation) in poor urban
areas is by giving people secure property rights. A 30-year study in Mexico
found that land titling cuts the electoral vote share of incumbent municipal
leaders, those best placed to engage in clientelistic extortion, limiting their
ability to exploit voters. In Chile, Collado and Wang (2020) found that better
land rights led to more investment in energy efficiency, home upgrades, and
access to banking, showing just how powerful secure ownership can be to build
resilience without engaging in clientelism.
Involving community members directly in budget decisions, a practice known
as participatory budgeting (PB), promotes transparency and also reduces
opportunities for clientelism and social exclusion. This approach has been
successful in various contexts, with the most extensively studied case being
Porto Alegre, Brazil. A review paper indicates that PB was used to allocate
approximately 20 percent of the municipal budget through citizen deliberation,
leading to significant improvements in public service delivery, particularly in
low-income neighborhoods (Islam 2025). However, the effectiveness of this
model can vary on the basis of factors such as political commitment, resource
availability, and the strength of civil society. In some cases, PB has become a
routine, technocratic exercise or has been undermined by partisan interests.
Despite these challenges, the broader experience with PB demonstrates that
when embedded in a supportive institutional context, it can function as both a
redistributive and a democratizing tool that enhances the equity and efficiency
of public budgeting.
In addition, two broad sets of measures may help (1) defining what public
policy seeks to accomplish and having other (independent) bodies evaluate
whether the goals were accomplished ensures both learning and accountability
and (2) disclosing everything to the public by default generally promotes the
growth of institutions of public oversight.

Curbing deforestation is possible
As noted in chapter 3, paying communities to preserve their forests can prevent
logging or land conversion for farming. Another policy route is regulation—
limiting deforestation directly rather than through pricing. Governments could

|

135

136

| Adapting to Adversity

expand protected areas, restrict land use, or better enforce existing
deforestation limits.
Ultimately, national policies to protect forests, whether through pricing
mechanisms or regulations, need local government support to work. Even if
detailed data on illegal deforestation exist (from sources such as the Hansen
et al. 2013 Global Forest Change Dataset, or MapBiomas), political will is
essential for action (Burgess, Costa, and Olken, forthcoming). In Brazil,
deforestation was sky high from 2001 to 2005 but dropped sharply from 2006
to 2013 after tougher fines, enforcement, and satellite monitoring (Balboni et al.
2023). Yet, political interests can hinder progress, as seen when deforestation
surged again in 2014 amid the dismantling of environmental regulation and
agencies. Most research on the politics of deforestation in Latin America
focuses on Brazil because of the prominence of the Amazon, the world’s largest
tropical rainforest—larger than the entire European Union.
Designating protected areas can be used as a political tool. Because conservation
can hurt local economies, national leaders sometimes declare protected zones to
punish opponents or reward allies. In Brazil, the president can create protected
areas by executive order. Magonnet, Kopas, and Urpelainen (2022) found that
municipalities in this country led by opposition mayors between 1997 and 2012
were 26–32 percent more likely to be designated as protected areas, likely to
preserve economic benefits for politically aligned regions.
Local officials may also turn a blind eye to illegal deforestation before elections
if it helps their chances of winning, especially through campaign contributions.
Logging brings profits, benefiting both loggers and politicians. Pailler (2018)
found that in Brazil, deforestation was 8–10 percent higher in election years
when incumbents sought reelection, likely to fund campaigns. Similarly,
Harding et al. (2023) showed that in Colombia, mayors relying on private
funding enforced forestry laws less strictly than those using public funds.
These examples show how political cycles fuel deforestation, with clientelism
driving permit handouts and weakening enforcement, especially in the
Amazon. The big question is how to incentivize politicians and civil servants to
shift from forest destruction to conservation.
Although rigorous studies on shifting politicians’ incentives for forest
conservation are limited, some research suggests that information campaigns,
empowered voters, monitoring, transparency, and voter pressure can change
bureaucratic behavior and influence political incentives, helping to hold
politicians accountable even for their environmental decisions. For example, a
study of natural resource management in Ecuador between 1997 and 2008
found that local governments were incentivized to pursue costly reforms, such
as reforestation projects, because of the presence of organic political parties
and participatory decision-making institutions (Kauffman and Terry 2016).
Organic parties are electoral vehicles created by social movements to advance
their interests from within the political system. The study found that these

Improving Adaptation

parties provided formal ties between local politicians and social movements,
making elected officials more accountable to a mobilized civil society and
promoting innovative policy reforms. In this way, institutions that link
politicians with a mobilized civil society can incentivize elected officials to
pursue reforms that improve government performance.
A study in Brazil found that random audits of municipal governments, which
publicly disclosed how incumbent mayors used federal funds, led to a
significant reduction in corruption levels over a 10-year period. This finding
highlights the effectiveness of transparency and monitoring mechanisms in
promoting accountability in local governance (Avis, Ferraz, and Finan 2018).
Stronger local monitoring can help curb tree cover loss by empowering
Indigenous and local communities, who often manage forests better than
outsiders. In the Peruvian Amazon, a study tested a program that transferred
early deforestation alerts from the Global Forest Watch network to Indigenous
communities. Trained and incentivized community members then patrolled
their forests in response. The program, randomly assigned to 39 of 76
communities, found that those with local patrols actually saw less tree cover
loss than those without them (Slough, Kopas, and Urpelainen 2021).
Reforms that enhance the representation of Indigenous groups may also create
powerful incentives for local leaders to advocate for sustainable forestry and—
more important—put in power the very people with the local expertise and
precise information to be able to tackle deforestation. Some evidence in favor of
this proposal comes from a wholly different geographic context yet may hold
important lessons for the LAC region: Gulzar, Lal, and Pasquale (2024) find that
increasing representation of scheduled tribes in local government in India led
to significant increases in tree cover and reductions in deforestation.
Countries can cut rates of tropical forest loss if they garner the political will to do
so, and the countries that have accomplished this can provide lessons for others.

Prevention, relief, and political markets
There is plenty of evidence that prevention beats disaster relief, yet politicians
and voters keep choosing the latter. Investing in dams and other protective
infrastructure (including in natural assets such as forests and mangroves), as
well as early warning systems, could save lives and cut costs, as chapter 3
shows, but, instead, people wait for climate hazards to occur. Countries, and
particularly poor countries, may prefer the immediate economic gains from
exploitation to the uncertain future returns from conservation and prevention.
Moreover, and strangely enough, there is a wide range of evidence showing that
climate hazards can actually help incumbents stay in power. As it turns out, bad
climate policy can be good politics.
Early studies from India and the United States show that although voters blame
politicians for climate hazards turned into disasters, they reward them for

|

137

138

| Adapting to Adversity

handing out postdisaster aid (Cole, Healy, and Werker 2012; Healy and
Malhotra 2009). Healy and Malhotra’s (2009) groundbreaking research on the
United States reveals that voters applaud disaster relief spending but ignore
preparedness investments. They argue that politicians cater to the voters’ shortterm desire for instant gratification, but the reality is more complex.
Studies across Latin America show the same pattern: Voters reward politicians
who provide disaster relief. Aid becomes a political tool, boosting reelections. In
Colombia, flood-hit areas (2010–11) saw a 23 percent rise in incumbent support
because of food aid, not infrastructure (Gallego 2018). In Mexico, politicians
declare emergencies more often during election years to sway voters (Boudreau
2015). In Brazil, drought relief follows the same pattern (Bedran-Martins and
Lemos 2017). Experimental evidence shows drought-stricken residents are more
likely to seek personal favors from politicians, reinforcing clientelist ties (Bobonis
et al. 2022). How can politicians be incentivized to prioritize preventive spending
over relief spending—and prevent misuse of relief funds for clientelism while
ensuring they reach those who are most vulnerable?
Anticipatory cash transfers are given before shocks hit and are based on a
forecast. They can be a starting point for preemptive measures that are more
politically attractive rather than waiting for a crisis to occur. Instead of
replacing relief, they actually strengthen it by showing the clear benefits of
early action to help people avoid losses and bounce back faster, making it more
appealing for politicians to act before a crisis (refer to box 4.7).
Box 4.7

Anticipatory transfers and funding to adapt safety nets against climate hazards
One idea that has been gaining increasing
traction under adaptive social protection is to
focus on anticipatory transfers and early
response systems. Countries such as Bangladesh,
Nepal, Niger, and Nigeria have used safety nets
to deliver support before climate hazards hit.
Politicians still get credit for helping victims, but
earlier action leaves households better off. For
instance, anticipatory cash transfers in
Bangladesh reduced food insecurity by 36
percent in flood-prone households while
protecting assets and improving children’s
nutrition (Pople et al. 2021). This approach relies
on predictive analytics and early warning
systems to forecast extreme weather events with
sufficient confidence, triggering the
prearranged release of emergency cash several

days before flooding peaks. In the 2020 Jamuna
River pilot, vulnerable households received
mobile money transfers ahead of the flood,
allowing them to prepare and avoid more
damaging coping strategies. This contrasts with
traditional humanitarian responses that often
take weeks or months to mobilize after a climate
hazard has occurred.
Similarly, in Niger, the government piloted an
anticipatory cash transfer program in 2021 that
used satellite-based drought triggers to deliver
early support before the lean season. The early
transfers improved food security by 8 percent,
raised monthly food consumption by 17.6
percent, and boosted life satisfaction and
mental health (Pople et al. 2024). More
important, these benefits persisted even after
continued

Improving Adaptation

|

Box 4.7 Anticipatory transfers and funding to adapt safety nets against climate shocks (continued)

the transfers ended, with households that
received early transfers maintaining better
food security and well-being during the lean
season than those who received traditional or
smaller, spread-out transfers. The study
highlights how well-timed support, based on
early warning systems, can significantly
improve outcomes for vulnerable households
facing realized climate hazards.

Politicians still need to get the money to fund
relief efforts. Mali offers one model: After years
of investing in scalable safety nets, it now
combines contingency funds and sovereign
insurance to fund responses. During the 2023–24
drought, this approach delivered emergency cash
to 140,000 households—the first time that
insurance directly supported a national safety net
in Africa (World Bank 2024b).

Anticorruption audits and town hall meetings have shown success in curbing
clientelism in climate-hazard-prone contexts. Bobonis et al. (2022) analyze
randomized audits in Brazil, demonstrating their efficacy in reducing
campaign handouts, citizen demands for private goods, and politicians’
fulfillment of requests. Similarly, the provision of goods susceptible to
political capture drastically reduces such practices (refer to box 4.8).
In contexts in which town hall meetings are prevalent, debates can be
instrumental in combating clientelism. An experimental study in Benin
(Fujiwara and Wantchekon 2011) examined the impact of town hall meetings
based on programmatic, nonclientelist platforms. The study found that such
meetings reduced the prevalence of clientelism and did not affect voter
turnout. This suggests that informed public deliberation can diminish
clientelist practices.
Like an onion, possible improvements to reduce clientelism have several layers:
better voter education at the individual level, mechanisms of community and
possibly local government oversight (the role of the press), and competitive
democracies at the political level. But arrangements that work well in one
country need not be mimicked in another; indeed, many diverse arrangements
may serve the purpose well.
It should be noted that high-level political will should not come at the
expense of sound technical cadres and systems in place across multiple
agencies. From environmental science to climate risk finance, governments
need to build knowledge and skill sets in ministries of planning, social
development, central banks, and insurance regulators. This includes
strengthening the ability to interpret climate data, design risk-informed
policies, and deploy financial tools such as insurance or contingency
financing. Countries that have been able to move up the capabilities ladder
have done so by investing in their civil services, retaining technical staff, and
enabling coordination across levels of government.

139

140

| Adapting to Adversity

Box 4.8

Reducing drought vulnerability and clientelism in Brazil
Bobonis et al. (2022) examine a randomized
controlled trial based on a water cistern
construction program in partnership with a
nongovernmental organization in the Brazilian
northeast in which households were randomly
assigned to receive water cisterns for free or were
assigned to the control group. The cisterns were
built during 2012, before October’s 2012 municipal
election. The rain-fed water cisterns were a strategy
for low-income rural households to cope with
droughts, thus reducing vulnerability and their
need to depend on politicians for water supplies
(a common clientelistic feature of the region).

The water cistern intervention yielded promising
outcomes: It lessened economic vulnerability,
diminished clientelism, and hindered the
reelection prospects of incumbent mayors.
Citizens who received cisterns significantly
reduced their requests for private goods from
politicians by 17 percent, with a 3 percentage point
decrease. This decline persisted not only during
the election year, but also afterward. Notably, the
impact was most pronounced among citizens
engaged in clientelist relationships, with their
requests dropping by 38 percent, compared with
no effect for those without such ties.

Beyond communities and governments, multilateral agencies such as the World
Bank may also have a role to play in helping to better align political institutions
with pro-poor climate adaptation. It is easy—and all too common—to consider
country and government synonymous, but although climate hazards hit private
households and businesses the hardest, loans and aid flow to governments. This
can make governments more accountable to donors than to their own people,
hence the saying “he who pays the piper calls the tune.” To fix this, climate
hazard relief should be independent of political discretion. Understanding that
a country’s decision-making system matters more, a setup akin to a Project
Implementation Unit under a World Bank project could reduce inefficiencies
and political interference. Leveraging existing institutional incentives could
also ensure that donor funds prioritize prevention. Strong commitments—
through local laws or international agreements—could tie aid more directly to
preventive spending. This approach aligns with the International Bank for
Reconstruction and Development’s Development Policy Loan with catastrophedeferred drawdown options, which pushes to have strong macroeconomic
policies and disaster risk management frameworks in place. There is still room
for a more assertive approach, linking support to clear prevention benchmarks.
In sum, insufficient or ineffective climate adaptation policies for poor individuals
are rooted in politics, not just technical, financial, or resources challenges.
Government responses to climate hazard that materialize can also be driven by
political economy concerns that operate during climate crises.
Governments respond to various pressures because politicians and other
decision-makers respond to incentives, as noted earlier (for example, election
contributions and votes). Donors should continue providing technical
assistance but also be alert to bad decisions that could result from misaligned

Improving Adaptation

incentives, not just the lack of knowledge. This final section has acknowledged
some of those incentives and explored ways to leverage existing resources and
make the most out of them.
Overall, understanding how individuals—rich and poor alike—make decisions is
important to improve concern for their welfare. Why people choose what they
do and where they live is not always understandable. Following Greenstone and
Jack (2015), the report identified four key factors influencing the effectiveness
of climate response: low income levels, high adaptation costs, political economy
considerations, and market failures. To overcome these factors and attain
successful adaptation, the report proposes four elements:
1. Providing incentives for individuals and politicians to adopt climate-resilient
measures;
2. Leveraging information and technology generated by governments, multilateral organizations such as the World Bank, and the private sector;
3. Securing investments to adequately fund adaptation endeavors; and
4. Establishing multiple institutions (for example, media to inform the public,
political parties to compete for votes, and technocrats to discuss the technical
merits of alternatives) to facilitate adaptation at various government levels
and ensure checks and balances.
All efforts should have be focused on the poorest households and regions, given
their high vulnerability to climate hazards.

NOTES
1. Some research from development economics (Oster 2005) argues that because of
the multitude of risks often faced by individuals in poor countries, discount rates are
likely to be higher than in the developed world.
2. The Akerlof-type market for lemons is a situation in which information asymmetry
between buyers and sellers leads to market failure. Buyers cannot distinguish
between high-quality (peaches) and low-quality (lemons) products, so they offer a
lower price, driving good-quality products (peaches) out of the market and leaving
only low-quality goods (lemons) (Akerlof 1970).
3. Globally, it is the same story—no evidence of a decline in population growth for
cities in low-income countries that have faced repeated flooding in the past.
See Gandhi et al. (2024).
4. CAPRA kicked off in 2008 as a joint effort by several big players—including the UN,
World Bank, Inter-American Development Bank, and regional disaster prevention
groups—to strengthen institutional capacities in Central America to better
understand and manage disaster risks. It provided a Geographic Information
System–based platform to assess hazards such as earthquakes, floods, hurricanes,
landslides, and volcanoes, giving decision-makers tools to plan at local, national, and
regional levels. Supported by international donors such as Australia, Spain, and the
Global Facility for Disaster Reduction and Recovery, CAPRA has been run by
Universidad de los Andes (Uniandes) since 2017, after it was selected through a
competitive World Bank–led process.

5. Climate hazards in LAC—particularly heat and drought—are associated with
increased rural-to-urban and international migration. In Mexico, high temperatures
reduce local employment and contribute to internal migration as well as migration
to the United States, especially among unskilled workers and youth (Baez et al. 2017;
Jessoe, Manning, and Taylor (2018).
6. Although this section discusses the challenges with the CBA approach, they are
applicable to other, less common approaches such as the cost-effectiveness analysis
(World Bank and UN 2010).

Glossary

The terms in this report are used differently across disciplines.
Adaptation is the process of adjustment to actual or expected climate and its
effects to moderate harm or exploit beneficial opportunities.
Exposure is the people; livelihoods; species or ecosystems; environmental
functions, services, and resources; infrastructure; or assets in places and
settings that could be adversely affected by the hazard.
Hazard is the potential occurrence of a natural or human-induced physical
event (flood, storm, drought) or trend that may cause loss of life, injury, or other
health impacts, as well as damage and loss to property, infrastructure,
livelihoods, service provision, ecosystems, and environmental resources. This
report focuses on the following climate hazards: drought, floods, storms,
landslides, and heat waves.
Poverty Two definitions of poverty are used in this report. A person or
household is classified as monetarily poor if their household income
(per person) is less than the poverty line. This report uses an international
poverty line ($6.85 purchasing power parity 2017 per person per day) as the
benchmark for international comparisons. Two poverty measures are used to
aggregate poverty status across households: The poverty incidence is the
percent of the population living in poor households. The poverty gap measures
how far, on average, the poor population is from the total poverty line. A person
or household is classified as nonmonetarily poor if they suffer from overlapping
deprivations in education, quality of materials in housing, access to services,
and lack of durable goods.
Risk is often calculated as a multiplicative function of hazard, exposure, and
vulnerability. It is multiplicative because for risk to exist, all three—hazard,
exposure, and vulnerability—have to be present.

151

152

| Adapting to Adversity

Vulnerability is the propensity or predisposition to be adversely affected,
including sensitivity or susceptibility to harm and lack of capacity to cope and
adapt. Some communities absorb and recover more readily than others because
of physical assets (building design and strength), social capital (community
structure, trust, and family networks), and political access (ability to get
government help and affect policies and decisions). Measures to reduce
vulnerability include mitigation (which reduces the hazard’s likelihood, as in
reforesting slopes to prevent rapid runoff and floods or reducing greenhouse gas
emissions to reduce the frequency and intensity of extreme weather events),
prevention (measures to reduce damage, as with higher plinths for floods),
preparedness (evacuation plans), and relief (help after a disaster).
Source: World Bank staff.
Note: For formal definitions, https://www.ipcc.ch/report/ar6/wg2/downloads​
/report/IPCC_AR6_WGII_Annex-II.pdf.

How can Latin America and the Caribbean protect their hard-won progress
against poverty in the face of mounting climate hazards and a changing
climate? This book provides a powerful, evidence-based answer.
Drawing on new data, original research, and previous studies from across
the region, Adapting to Adversity examines how climate hazards threaten
the livelihoods of the poor and near-poor—and what can be done to
strengthen their resilience. It reveals the stark reality that more than a
third of the region’s people, including nearly half of all poor households,
live in areas highly exposed to droughts, floods, hurricanes, heatwaves,
and landslides. For millions living one shock away from falling back into
poverty, climate risk is not a future concern but a daily struggle.
This book explores who is most at risk, how climate hazards intersect
with poverty and inequality, and what policies are proving most effective.
It highlights successful innovations such as early warning systems,
catastrophe insurance facilities, and adaptive social protection programs,
while exposing the persistent gaps that leave many communities
dangerously unprotected. Beyond diagnosis, Adapting to Adversity
offers a roadmap for action. It calls for layered, coordinated systems that
combine prevention, financial protection, and social safety nets to make
development gains durable. With adaptation needs projected to reach
$55 billion annually by 2030, the book argues that financing, institutional
reform, and political will are critical to turning resilience into reality.
Essential reading for policymakers, development practitioners, and scholars,
this book makes a compelling case: in Latin America and the Caribbean,
adaptation is not a luxury—it is development itself.
